Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
The Three Rational Judgments: Necessary, Impossible, Possible
Sanusi defines the rational judgment and its three divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja’iz) and how they form the foundational taxonomy of kalam.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are the grammar of theological reasoning.

Before we can discuss Allah’s attributes or prophethood, we need a clear vocabulary for what the mind can and cannot accept. Sanusi opens Umm al-Barahin by defining the three rational judgments—necessary, impossible, and possible. This small set of categories is the key to all later arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read Sanusi's opening carefully. Notice the concise definitions. The translation helps but try to follow the Arabic rhythm.
2
Identify each judgment and its definition
Find the three terms: al-wajib, al-mustahil, al-ja’iz. See how each is defined by what the mind can or cannot conceive.
Necessary is defined by inconceivability of non-existence, impossible by inconceivability of existence, possible by conceivability of both.
3
Connect to kalam
Ask yourself: Why are these three enough? How would you use them to talk about God? The commentary will show you.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمْدُ لِلهِ، والصَّلاةُ والسَّلامُ عَلى رَسُولِ اللهِ.
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الحُكمَ العَقْليَّ ينحصِرُ في ثلاثة أقسام: الوُجُوب، والاستحالة، والجواز. فالواجبُ مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي العَقلِ عَدَمُهُ. والمُستَحيلُ مَا لاَ يُتصَوَّرُ فِي العَقْل وُجُودُه ، والجَائِزُ مَا يَصِحُ في العَقلِ وُجُودهُ وعَدَمُهُ.
Praise be to Allah, and prayers and peace be upon the Messenger of Allah. Know that the rational judgment is confined to three divisions: necessity (al-wujub), impossibility (al-istihalah), and possibility (al-jawaz). The necessary (al-wajib) is that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind. The impossible (al-mustahil) is that whose existence is not conceivable in the mind. The possible (al-ja’iz) is that whose existence and non-existence are valid in the mind.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin after the basmala and hamdala. It defines the central concept of 'rational judgment' (al-hukm al-‘aqli), which is a mental assessment of a proposition. Sanusi states that rational judgments are limited to three categories: necessity (wujub), impossibility (istihalah), and possibility (jawaz). He then defines each: Necessary (wajib) is what the mind cannot conceive as not existing—its non-existence is rationally impossible. Impossible (mustahil) is what the mind cannot conceive as existing—its existence is rationally impossible. Possible (ja’iz) is what the mind can conceive as either existing or not existing—both are rationally coherent. This tripartite division is the foundational taxonomy used in kalam to discuss attributes of God, prophethood, and other theological matters.

Key Terms
الحكم العقلي
الوجوب
الاستحالة
الجواز
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some students think 'wajib' in this context means legally obligatory (like in fiqh).
Correction: Sanusi is using 'wajib' as a rational category, not a legal one. It means rationally necessary—what the mind cannot deny.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the definition of the 'possible' (al-ja’iz)?
A) That whose existence is conceivable but non-existence is not.
B) That whose existence and non-existence are both conceivable in the mind.
C) That whose existence is necessary.
D) That whose non-existence is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments—necessary, impossible, possible—are the basic tools for all theological reasoning in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah alone is necessary (wajib), and you are merely possible (ja'iz), then your existence at this moment is a gift sustained by Him, not something you own. What would change in your daily life if you truly lived with the awareness that you could cease to exist at any instant if Allah withdrew His sustaining will? Name one specific habit you will start today to remind yourself of this truth.
?
Since it is rationally impossible (mustahil) for Allah to have any imperfection or injustice, why do you sometimes fear the opinions of people more than the judgment of Allah? What does that reveal about your heart's true belief in His necessary attributes? Commit to one action this week that realigns your fear toward Him alone.
?
The possible (ja'iz) category includes both your good deeds and your sins—they are not forced upon you. If you know that your actions are possible and that Allah gives you the choice, how does that accountability weigh on you right now? What is one area of your life where you are not taking that choice seriously, and what step will you take today to exercise your freedom responsibly?

Rational Judgments

The Three Rational Judgments and Their Obligation
Sanusi defines the three rational judgments and states the obligation to know them for Allah and the messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of rational judgment (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) and why knowing them for Allah and the messengers is a legal obligation.

Core Idea
Rational judgments are the intellectual grammar of theology—and knowing them is a religious duty.

Before we can speak correctly about Allah and the messengers, we need a shared vocabulary for what is intellectually necessary, impossible, or possible. In this passage, Sanusi defines these three rational judgments and then grounds our theological duty in them. This is the starting point of kalam: not abstract philosophy, but a divinely mandated inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the confident opening 'اعْلَمْ' (Know) which signals that what follows is foundational.
2
Identify the three judgments
Find the three categories: al-wajib, al-mustahil, al-ja'iz. Note how each is defined by what the mind can or cannot conceive.
Each definition uses the phrase 'يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي العَقْلِ' (conceivable in the mind).
3
Observe the shift to obligation
Sanusi then says 'وَيَجبُ' (and it is obligatory). This shifts from definition to legal duty. Who is obligated? Every mukallaf. What must they know? The three judgments for Allah and for the messengers.
4
Reflect on the link
Ask yourself: why is knowing these categories a religious duty? The passage ties theological correctness to rational categories.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الحُكمَ العَقْليَّ ينحصِرُ في ثلاثة أقسام: الوُجُوب، والاستحالة، والجواز. فالواجبُ مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي العَقلِ عَدَمُهُ. والمُستَحيلُ مَا لاَ يُتصَوَّرُ فِي العَقْل وُجُودُه، والجَائِزُ مَا يَصِحُ في العَقلِ وُجودهُ وعَدَمُهُ. وَيَجبُ عَلى كلِّ مُكَلّفٍ شَرْعاً أنْ يَعرِفَ مَا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّ مَوْلانا عَزَّوَجَلَّ، ومَا يَستَحيلُ، ومَا يَجُوزُ. وَكَذَا يَجبُ عَليهِ أنْ يَعرف مِثلَ ذَلِكَ فِي حَقِّ الرُّسُلِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ والسَّلامُ.
Know that the rational judgment is limited to three divisions: obligation, impossibility, and possibility. The obligatory is that whose non-existence is inconceivable in the mind. The impossible is that whose existence is inconceivable in the mind. The possible is that whose existence and non-existence are both valid in the mind. And it is legally obligatory upon every legally responsible person to know what is obligatory in the case of our Lord, mighty and majestic, and what is impossible, and what is possible. Likewise, it is obligatory upon him to know the like of that in the case of the messengers, may peace and blessings be upon them.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a prescriptive statement. Sanusi begins by defining the three rational judgments: wajib (obligatory), mustahil (impossible), and ja'iz (possible). For each, he gives a definition based on the mind's conceptual capacity. The wajib is that whose non-existence the mind cannot conceive; the mustahil is that whose existence the mind cannot conceive; the ja'iz is that whose existence or non-existence the mind can equally conceive. He then states the legal obligation (shar'an) upon every mukallaf (legally responsible person) to know which of these three judgments apply to Allah and to the messengers. This establishes the foundational duty of theological knowledge.

Key Terms
حكم عقلي (rational judgment)
وجوب (obligation)
استحالة (impossibility)
جواز (possibility)
مكلف (legally responsible person)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three rational judgments are abstract philosophical categories with no practical religious relevance.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that knowing them is a shar'i obligation upon every mukallaf, making them foundational to Islamic theology and worship.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of al-wajib (obligatory) in rational judgment?
A) That whose existence is inconceivable in the mind
B) That whose non-existence is inconceivable in the mind
C) That whose existence and non-existence are both valid in the mind
D) That which is legally obligatory upon every Muslim

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) are the categories of theological reasoning, and learning them is a religious duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you treating the knowledge of what is wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz for Allah as a mere intellectual curiosity, or as a personal, non-negotiable duty that you must fulfill today? What is stopping you from actually sitting down to learn these categories properly?
?
The passage says every mukallaf must know these judgments. Do you honestly consider yourself part of that 'every'? If you hesitate at your own qualification, what does that say about your seriousness towards your religious obligations?
?
You know from this lesson that the one who is obligated by the law must know these rational judgments. Have you ever felt the weight of that obligation? If not, ask yourself: why are you comfortable living with ignorance about what your Lord demands?

Proof of Permissibility of Allah's Actions
Sanusi proves that actions of possible things are permissible for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that Allah's actions concerning possible things are neither obligatory nor impossible, but permissible.

Core Idea
Allah’s actions are free from rational necessity or impossibility.

Sanusi presents a concise rational proof for a key theological point: that Allah's actions concerning created possibilities are not constrained by rational obligation or impossibility. This proof uses the principle of a thing's nature to show that if an action were necessary or impossible, the category of 'possible' would be violated. The following passage is the core of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the logical structure.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the three rational categories
The passage mentions 'obligatory' (wajib), 'impossible' (mustahil), and 'permissible' (ja'iz). Understand that these are the three rational judgments in kalam.
The possible (mumkin) is the default state of created things.
3
Follow the conditional logic
The proof argues: if an action were obligatory or impossible, the possible thing would change its nature, which is inconceivable. Therefore, the action must remain permissible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the implication for Allah's freedom
Consider why this proof is important: it establishes that Allah's actions are not constrained by rational necessity, preserving His free will and power.
This connects to divine omnipotence and justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا بُرْهانُ كوْنِ فِعل المُمْكِناتِ أو تركها جَائِزاً في حَقِّهِ تَعالى: فلأنَّهُ لَوْ وَجَبَ عليهِ تَعالى شيءٌ مِنها عَقلاً، أو استحال عقلاً لانقلب المُمْكِنُ واجِباً أو مستحيلاً، وذلك لا يُعْقَلُ.
As for the proof that the doing or refraining from possible things is permissible in His right, exalted is He: because if anything among them were obligatory upon Him, exalted is He, rationally, or impossible rationally, the possible would become obligatory or impossible, and that is not rationally conceivable.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage provides the proof (burhan) that actions of possible things (al-mumkinat) are permissible (ja'iz) for Allah—neither obligatory (wajib) nor impossible (mustahil). The argument is: if an action were obligatory, then the possible thing would become necessary (wajib), changing its nature. Similarly, if it were impossible, the possible would become impossible. Both are inconceivable (la yu'qal). Therefore, they remain permissible.

Key Terms
برهان
ممكنات
جائز
واجب
مستحيل
لا يعقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah is obligated to reward or punish based on human merit.
Correction: This proof shows that no action of Allah regarding possible things is rationally necessary; reward and punishment are permissible, not obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what would happen if an action were obligatory upon Allah?
A) The action would become impossible.
B) The possible would become obligatory.
C) Nothing would change.
D) The action would become permissible.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility (jawaz) of Allah's actions is proven by the impossibility of changing the nature of possible things from their essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is not obligated to give you anything, and every blessing is pure grace, what does that mean for your sense of deserving? How often do you act as if you are owed something by Him?
?
This proof shows that no possible action is rationally necessary upon Allah. What hidden assumption in your heart might be saying 'Allah should have done this for me' or 'Allah owes me that'? Repent from that today.
?
Since your very existence is a possible thing that Allah chose to bring into being without any obligation, how should that reshape your gratitude and worship? Will you now approach Him as a humble recipient of grace, not a claimant of rights?

From the Shahada: Dependence, Oneness, and the World's Origination
Sanusi derives oneness and temporal origination from the principle of dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first shahada logically implies the total dependence of creation on Allah, His oneness, and the temporal origination of the world.

Core Idea
The shahada reveals that everything depends on Allah, affirming His uniqueness and the world's beginning.

The statement 'There is no god but Allah' is not just a declaration of faith; it contains profound rational implications. In this passage, Sanusi begins by establishing the foundational principle that everything in existence is contingent and dependent on Allah. From this single principle, he derives two crucial doctrines: Allah's oneness (wahdaniyya) and the temporal origination of the universe (huduth al-alam). This lesson uncovers how the kalām argument from dependence leads to these conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening premise
The passage starts by asserting that nothing from contingent things is independent of Allah. Notice the rhetorical question 'how could it be?' This sets the stage.
This premise is the key to the entire argument.
2
Derivation of oneness
Sanusi argues that if a second god existed, both would be incapable and thus would need something else, contradicting universal dependence.
This is a tawhid argument.
3
Derivation of the world's origination
If any part of the world were eternal, it would be independent of Allah. Since all things depend on Him, nothing can be eternal. Therefore, the world must have a beginning.
This establishes contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شَيْءٌ مِنَ الحَوادِثِ، فَلا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ؟ وَيُوجِبُ لَهُ أَيْضاً: الوَحْدَانِيَّةَ؛ إِذْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ ثَانٍ فِي الأَلوهِيَّةِ لَمَا افْتَقَرَ إِلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ لِلُزُومِ عَجْزِهِمَا حِينَئِذٍ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ؟ وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً: حُدُوثُ الْعَالَمِ بِأَسْرِهِ؛ إِذْ لَوْ كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنْهُ قَدِيماً لَكَانَ ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءُ مُسْتَغْنِياً عَنْهُ تَعَالَى، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَجِبُ أَنْ يَفْتَقِرَ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ؟ وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً: أَنَّهُ لا تَأْثِيرَ لِشَيْءٍ مِنَ الْكائِنَاتِ فِي أَثَرٍ مَا، وإِلا لَزِمَ أَنْ يَسْتَغْنِيَ ذَلِكَ الأَثَرُ عَنْ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ عُمُوماً وَعَلَى كُلِّ حَالٍ؟ هَذَا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ أَنَّ شَيْئاً مِنَ الْكائِنَاتِ يُؤَثِّرُ بِطَبْعِهِ. وَأَمَّا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَهُ مُؤَثِّراً بِقُوَّةٍ جَعَلَهَا اللهُ فِيهِ كَمَا يَزْعُمُهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الْجَهَلَةِ؛ فَذَلِكَ مُحَالٌ أَيْضاً؛ لأَنَّهُ يَصِيرُ حِينَئِذٍ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ مُفْتَقِراً فِي إِيجَادِ بَعْضِ الأَفْعَالِ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ لِمَا عَرَفْتَ مِنْ وُجُوبِ اسْتِغْنَائِهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ. فَقَدْ بَانَ لَكَ تَضَمُّنُ قَوْلِ: لا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ لِلأَقْسَامِ الثَّلاثَةِ الَّتِي يَجِبُ عَلَى الْمُكَلَّفِ مَعْرِفَتُهَا فِي حَقِّ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ وَهِيَ: مَا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى، وَمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ، وَمَا يَجُوزُ.
Nothing from contingent things is independent of Him, so nothing is independent of Him; how could it be, when He is the one upon whom everything else depends? And it also necessitates for Him oneness; for if there were a second in divinity, then nothing would depend on Him due to the necessary incapacity of both then; how could it be, when He is the one upon whom everything else depends? And from it is also derived: the temporal origination of the entire world; for if any part of it were eternal, that thing would be independent of Him, exalted; how could it be, when He is the one to whom everything else must depend? And from it is also derived: that nothing from the existent things has any effect in any effect; otherwise, that effect would be independent of our Master, Majestic and Mighty; how could it be, when everything else depends on Him generally and in every state? This is if you suppose that something from the existent things has effect by its nature. And as for if you suppose it to be effecting by a power that God placed in it, as many of the ignorant claim; that is also impossible; because then our Master, Majestic and Mighty, would become dependent in producing some actions upon an intermediary, and that is false due to what you have known of the necessity of His independence, Majestic and Mighty, from everything else. So it has become clear to you that the statement 'There is no god but God' comprises the three divisions which the responsible person must know regarding our Master, Majestic and Mighty: what is necessary regarding Him, exalted; what is impossible; and what is possible.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the first shahada (La ilaha illa Allah) logically entails the three rational judgments about Allah (necessary, impossible, possible) and fundamental doctrines of tawhid. The argument proceeds as follows: First, it asserts that nothing from contingent things (al-hawadith) can be independent of Allah; everything depends on Him. From this foundational premise, Sanusi derives multiple conclusions in sequence. (1) The oneness (al-wahdaniyya) of Allah: if a second divine existed, both would be incapable and thus would need something else, contradicting the premise that everything depends on Him. (2) The temporal origination (huduth) of the entire world: if any part were eternal, it would be independent of Allah, again contradicting universal dependence. (3) The denial of causal efficacy (ta'thir) from any created being: if a creature had influence by its nature, the effect would be independent of Allah; the same applies if the creature acts by a power God placed in it – that would make God dependent on an intermediary, impossible due to His necessary self-sufficiency. The passage concludes that the shahada contains the three categories: wajib (necessary), mustahil (impossible), and ja'iz (possible) regarding Allah. This is a classic kalam argument from the principle of dependence (iftiqar) to establish tawhid, contingency, and divine causality.

Key Terms
الوحدانية
حُدوث العالم
الافتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shahada only affirms God's existence, not His oneness or the world's createdness.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the shahada logically entails these doctrines through the principle of universal dependence.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the shahada imply about the world's eternity?
A) It implies the world is eternal.
B) It implies the world is originated.
C) It implies nothing about the world.
D) It implies the world is both.

Key Takeaway
The shahada's implication that everything depends on Allah directly proves His oneness and the temporal origination of the universe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if every breath, every ability, and every possession depends entirely on Allah, or do you act as if some area of your life is self-sufficient? If you fully internalized that nothing is independent of Him, what would you stop worrying about—and what would you start relying on Him for today?
?
If the entire world—including your own existence—is temporally originated, meaning it had a beginning, how does that reshape your view of your past regrets and future plans? Are you living as if you are eternal, or as if you are a creature who came into being and will return?
?
The passage says belief in a second god implies mutual incapacity. Do you ever treat people, status, or ambitions as 'little gods' that you depend on as if they were independent? How does that undermine your shahada? What step will you take this week to redirect that trust back to Allah alone?

Rational Judgments

From the Shahada: No Created Causality and the Three Categories
Sanusi denies created causality and derives the three rational categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the shahada denies any causal efficacy to created beings and leads to the three rational judgments about Allah (necessary, impossible, possible).

Core Idea
No creature has true causal power; only Allah is the ultimate cause, making Him necessary and all else contingent.

The first shahada not only establishes dependence, oneness, and origination; it also addresses the nature of causality. In this passage, Sanusi argues that no created being (ka’in) can have any genuine effect—whether by its own nature or by a power granted by Allah—because that would compromise Allah’s absolute independence. This argument culminates in the celebrated conclusion that the shahada contains the three categories of rational judgment regarding Allah: what is necessary for Him, what is impossible, and what is possible.

Guided Path
1
Denial of natural causality
Sanusi first considers the claim that a creature acts by its own nature. He argues this would make the effect independent of Allah.
This is a key doctrine in Ash'ari kalām.
2
Denial of delegated power
Some claim that Allah gives a creature power to cause effects. Sanusi says this would make Allah dependent on an intermediary, which is impossible.
Both forms of causality are rejected.
3
Conclusion: three categories
The author concludes that the shahada encompasses three realms: necessary (wajib) for Allah, impossible (mustahil), and possible (ja'iz) for creation.
This is the famous tripartite division.
4
Reflect on overall logic
The entire argument from dependence leads to this comprehensive framework.
This is foundational for all further kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شَيْءٌ مِنَ الحَوادِثِ، فَلا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ؟ وَيُوجِبُ لَهُ أَيْضاً: الوَحْدَانِيَّةَ؛ إِذْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ ثَانٍ فِي الأَلوهِيَّةِ لَمَا افْتَقَرَ إِلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ لِلُزُومِ عَجْزِهِمَا حِينَئِذٍ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ؟ وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً: حُدُوثُ الْعَالَمِ بِأَسْرِهِ؛ إِذْ لَوْ كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنْهُ قَدِيماً لَكَانَ ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءُ مُسْتَغْنِياً عَنْهُ تَعَالَى، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَجِبُ أَنْ يَفْتَقِرَ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ؟ وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً: أَنَّهُ لا تَأْثِيرَ لِشَيْءٍ مِنَ الْكائِنَاتِ فِي أَثَرٍ مَا، وإِلا لَزِمَ أَنْ يَسْتَغْنِيَ ذَلِكَ الأَثَرُ عَنْ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ عُمُوماً وَعَلَى كُلِّ حَالٍ؟ هَذَا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ أَنَّ شَيْئاً مِنَ الْكائِنَاتِ يُؤَثِّرُ بِطَبْعِهِ. وَأَمَّا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَهُ مُؤَثِّراً بِقُوَّةٍ جَعَلَهَا اللهُ فِيهِ كَمَا يَزْعُمُهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الْجَهَلَةِ؛ فَذَلِكَ مُحَالٌ أَيْضاً؛ لأَنَّهُ يَصِيرُ حِينَئِذٍ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ مُفْتَقِراً فِي إِيجَادِ بَعْضِ الأَفْعَالِ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ لِمَا عَرَفْتَ مِنْ وُجُوبِ اسْتِغْنَائِهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ. فَقَدْ بَانَ لَكَ تَضَمُّنُ قَوْلِ: لا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ لِلأَقْسَامِ الثَّلاثَةِ الَّتِي يَجِبُ عَلَى الْمُكَلَّفِ مَعْرِفَتُهَا فِي حَقِّ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ وَهِيَ: مَا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى، وَمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ، وَمَا يَجُوزُ.
Nothing from contingent things is independent of Him, so nothing is independent of Him; how could it be, when He is the one upon whom everything else depends? And it also necessitates for Him oneness; for if there were a second in divinity, then nothing would depend on Him due to the necessary incapacity of both then; how could it be, when He is the one upon whom everything else depends? And from it is also derived: the temporal origination of the entire world; for if any part of it were eternal, that thing would be independent of Him, exalted; how could it be, when He is the one to whom everything else must depend? And from it is also derived: that nothing from the existent things has any effect in any effect; otherwise, that effect would be independent of our Master, Majestic and Mighty; how could it be, when everything else depends on Him generally and in every state? This is if you suppose that something from the existent things has effect by its nature. And as for if you suppose it to be effecting by a power that God placed in it, as many of the ignorant claim; that is also impossible; because then our Master, Majestic and Mighty, would become dependent in producing some actions upon an intermediary, and that is false due to what you have known of the necessity of His independence, Majestic and Mighty, from everything else. So it has become clear to you that the statement 'There is no god but God' comprises the three divisions which the responsible person must know regarding our Master, Majestic and Mighty: what is necessary regarding Him, exalted; what is impossible; and what is possible.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the first shahada (La ilaha illa Allah) logically entails the three rational judgments about Allah (necessary, impossible, possible) and fundamental doctrines of tawhid. The argument proceeds as follows: First, it asserts that nothing from contingent things (al-hawadith) can be independent of Allah; everything depends on Him. From this foundational premise, Sanusi derives multiple conclusions in sequence. (1) The oneness (al-wahdaniyya) of Allah: if a second divine existed, both would be incapable and thus would need something else, contradicting the premise that everything depends on Him. (2) The temporal origination (huduth) of the entire world: if any part were eternal, it would be independent of Allah, again contradicting universal dependence. (3) The denial of causal efficacy (ta'thir) from any created being: if a creature had influence by its nature, the effect would be independent of Allah; the same applies if the creature acts by a power God placed in it – that would make God dependent on an intermediary, impossible due to His necessary self-sufficiency. The passage concludes that the shahada contains the three categories: wajib (necessary), mustahil (impossible), and ja'iz (possible) regarding Allah. This is a classic kalam argument from the principle of dependence (iftiqar) to establish tawhid, contingency, and divine causality.

Key Terms
التأثير
الأقسام الثلاثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creatures can cause effects independently or by Allah's permission as intermediaries having real causal power.
Correction: Sanusi argues that any causal role for creatures would compromise Allah's independence, so they have no true causal efficacy.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is it impossible for a creature to cause an effect by a power Allah placed in it?
A) Because that power would be weak.
B) Because it would make Allah dependent on an intermediary.
C) Because the creature would become eternal.
D) Because it would imply two gods.

Key Takeaway
The shahada's implication that nothing has causal effect leads to the tripartite rational framework: necessary, impossible, and possible regarding Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you have been anxious about an outcome—like a job interview, a medical result, or a relationship. In that fear, did your heart truly believe that the created thing (the interviewer, the doctor, the other person) had no real power? Or was your emotional state revealing a hidden attribution of causality to that creature? What would it mean to live every moment with the conviction that only Allah is the real Agent? Write down one practical step to shift your reliance from means to the Maker of means.
?
Sanusi argues that even believing a creature acts by a power 'given' by Allah leads to attributing dependence to Allah. Consider your du'as: when you ask Allah to 'use' a doctor to heal you, are you treating the doctor as an intermediary that Allah 'needs'? How can you reform your understanding so that your supplication reflects total direct dependence on Allah, while still using the means without ascribing any inherent efficacy to them? Commit to one change in how you pray this week.
?
The Shahada contains the three categories: necessary, impossible, and possible for Allah. Reflect: is there any attribute or action you have unconsciously assumed is impossible for Allah (e.g., that He cannot forgive a particular sin, or cannot change your heart)? That assumption is a form of shirk in His power. Identify one such limiting belief, repent from it, and replace it with the correct belief that nothing is impossible for Allah (within His attributes).

The Four Rational Categories of Haram and Makruh (Part 1)
Sanusi defines four categories with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions and examples of haram li-dhatihi, haram li-ārid, makruh li-dhatihi, and makruh li-ārid as presented in the first part of the source.

Core Idea
A ruling varies with its cause or is fixed in itself.

Sanusi begins by clarifying the terms 'in itself' and 'due to an extrinsic circumstance' within the definition of a valid subject matter. The key criterion is whether the ruling (prohibition or dislike) varies with an underlying cause. This passage provides concrete examples from Islamic law to illustrate each category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the four categories
The passage presents four categories: haram li-dhatihi, haram li-ārid, makruh li-dhatihi, makruh li-ārid. Find each one and notice the criterion given: does the ruling vary with its cause?
The author uses the phrase 'يدور معها وجوداً وعدماً' to indicate variation.
3
Examine the examples
For each category, note the specific example and why it fits. For instance, fornication is haram li-dhatihi because its prohibition does not vary with the cause (lineage mixing). Wine is similar. Wudu with usurped water is haram li-ārid because the prohibition varies with the cause (usurpation).
The examples are from Shafi'i fiqh.
4
Understand the underlying logic
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for kalam? It shows that rulings can be intrinsic to acts or contingent on external factors, which is essential for understanding moral responsibility and divine commands.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: بِحَيْثُ لَا يَكُونُ مُحَرَّماً لِذَاتِهِ وَلَا مَكْرُوهاً لِذَاتِهِ يظهر أن المراد بالمحرم لذاته والمكروه لذاته ما لم يكن تحريمه وكراهته لعلَّةٍ يدور معها وجوداً وعَدَماً، والمحرَّمُ لعارض والمكروه لعارض ما كان تحريمه وكراهته لعلَّةٍ يدور معها وجوداً وعدماً. فالزنا وشربُ الخَمْرِ من قبيل المحرَّم لذاته؛ لأن تحريم الزنا لا يدور مع اختلاط علته - التي هي الأنساب - وجوداً وعدماً؛ إذ قد تنتفي العلَّةُ ويوجَدُ التحريمُ، كما إذا وَطِئَ رجلٌ صغيرةً، وكذلك تحريمُ شُرْبِ الخمر لا يدور مع علته - التي هي الإسكارُ؛ إذ قد ينتفي الإسكار ويوجد التحريم، كما إذا اعتاد الشخص شرب الخمر بحيث لا يُؤثِرُ في عقله شيئاً، أو شَرِبَ قدراً لا يُسْكِرُ، والوضوء بماء مغصوب من المحرَّمِ لعارض؛ لأن تحريمه يدورُ مع علته التي هي الاستيلاء على حقّ الغير عدوّاناً - وجوداً وعدماً، والنَّظَرُ لفرج الحليلة من قبيل المكروه لذاته؛ لأن كراهته لا تدور مع علته - التي هي خَوْفُ الطَّمْسِ مع عدم الحاجة؛ إذ العلة تنتفي وتوجد الكراهة كما إذا أخبره معصوم بأنه لا يحصلُ له طَمْسٌ إِذا نَظَرَ لفرج حليلته، وأكلُ البَصَلِ من المكروه لعارض؛ لأن كراهته تدور مع علته - التي هي تأذِي غيرِه ولو مَلَكاً - وجوداً وعدماً، فإذا انتفتِ العلَّةُ بأن طُبِخَ انتفتِ الكراهة.
وبهذا اندفع ما يقال: لا يُعْقَلُ فرق بين المحرَّم والمكروه لذاتهما وبين المحرم والمكروه لعارض؛ لأنه إذا نظر للشرب من حيث هو فجائز، وإن نظر لكونه متعلقاً بالخمر فتحرمُ على المحرَّمِ لذاته، وتُكْرَهُ على المكروه كذلك.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري: (قوله: لِذَاتِهِ) المراد بالمحرم لذاته ما تعلق النهي الجازم بنفسه كالزنا وشرب الخمر، وبالمكروه لذاته ما تعلق النهي غير الجازم بنفسه كأكل البصل والنظر إلى الفرج، وبالمحرم لعارض: ما تعلق النهي الجازم بلازمه كالصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة، فإن الشارع لم ينه عنها، وإنما نهى عن الغصب اللازم لها، وهو شغل مُلك الغير حال تلك الصلاة، فالصلاة نفسها مأمور بها مثاب عليها كما نقله البجيرمي على "المنهج" آخر باب الجماعة؛ لأن نفس الصلاة ليست غصباً؛ إذ لو كانت غصباً لانتفى الإثم بانتفائها حال المكث في الأرض المغصوبة واللازم باطل وبالمكروه لعارض: ما تعلق النهي غير الجازم بلازمه كأكل الخبز بالبصل، فإن أكل الخبز ليس منهياً عنه، وإنما المنهي عنه أكل البصل المصاحب له، وهذا المثال بحسب ما ظهر لنا، ولم نجد مثالاً منقولاً.
فهو حرام، كما أنه إن نظر للوضوء في ذاته فهو جائز، وإن نظر لكونه بماء مغصوب فهو حرام، وكذا يُقال في المكروه، فإن كان المراد بالمحرَّم والمكروه لذاتهما: ما كان تحريمه وكراهتُه لا لعلَّةٍ ولعارض ما كان ما ذُكِرَ لها. ورُدَّ عليه: أن للكُلّ عِلَلاً، ولا فَرْقَ.
وما تقرر من كون أكل البصل مكروهاً لعارض هو ما قرره لنا شيخنا المحشي غيرَ مرَّةٍ في الدرس، والظاهر أنه من المكروه لذاته، لكن بقَيْدِ كونه نَيْناً كما ذكره العلامةُ الشَّرْقَاوِيُّ في "حاشية التحرير" في باب الوضوء، فهو بالقيد المذكور تَلْزَمُهُ الكراهة لذاته، خلافاً لما استفيد من الفرق المتقدّم فالمناسِبُ: التمثيل للمكروه لعارض بالوضوء بالماء المشمس.
His saying: 'such that it is not forbidden in itself nor disliked in itself'. It appears that what is meant by forbidden in itself and disliked in itself is that whose prohibition and dislike are not due to a cause that varies with existence and non-existence; and forbidden due to an extrinsic circumstance and disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance is that whose prohibition and dislike are due to a cause that varies with existence and non-existence. So fornication and drinking wine are of the type of forbidden in itself, because the prohibition of fornication does not vary with the mixing of lineages—which is its cause—in existence and non-existence; for the cause may be absent while the prohibition exists, as when a man has intercourse with a minor girl. Likewise, the prohibition of drinking wine does not vary with its cause—which is intoxication; for intoxication may be absent while the prohibition exists, as when a person becomes accustomed to drinking wine such that it does not affect his intellect at all, or drinks an amount that does not intoxicate. Wudu with usurped water is of the type of forbidden due to an extrinsic circumstance, because its prohibition varies with its cause—which is taking over another's right by aggression—in existence and non-existence. Looking at the vulva of one's wife is of the type of disliked in itself, because its dislike does not vary with its cause—which is fear of being overwhelmed by desire without need; for the cause may be absent while the dislike exists, as when an infallible informant tells him that no such overwhelming will occur if he looks at his wife's vulva. Eating raw onion is of the type of disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance, because its dislike varies with its cause—which is causing harm to others, even angels—in existence and non-existence; so if the cause is removed by cooking it, the dislike is removed. By this is refuted what is said: no difference can be understood between forbidden and disliked in themselves and forbidden and disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance, because if one considers drinking in itself, it is permissible, but if one considers it as being attached to wine, then it is forbidden for that which is forbidden in itself, and disliked for that which is disliked in itself. Gloss by Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'in itself') What is meant by forbidden in itself is that to which a decisive prohibition is attached in itself, like fornication and drinking wine. By disliked in itself is that to which a non-decisive prohibition is attached in itself, like eating raw onion and looking at the vulva. By forbidden due to an extrinsic circumstance is that to which a decisive prohibition is attached as a necessary consequence, like prayer in usurped land, for the Lawgiver did not prohibit prayer itself, but prohibited the usurpation that is necessary for it, which is occupying another's property during that prayer. So prayer itself is commanded and rewarded, as al-Bujayrimi transmitted from al-Minhaj at the end of the chapter on congregation, because prayer itself is not usurpation; for if it were usurpation, then the sin would be removed by its non-existence while staying in the usurped land, and the conclusion is false. By disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance is that to which a non-decisive prohibition is attached as a necessary consequence, like eating bread with onion, for eating bread is not prohibited; only the accompanying eating of onion is prohibited. This example is according to what appeared to us, and we have not found a transmitted example. So it is forbidden, just as if one considers wudu in itself, it is permissible, but if one considers it as being with usurped water, it is forbidden. And the same is said regarding the disliked. If what is meant by forbidden and disliked in themselves is that whose prohibition and dislike are not due to a cause, and by due to an extrinsic circumstance is that which has a cause as mentioned, then it is objected: that everything has causes, and there is no difference. What has been established regarding eating raw onion being disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance is what our teacher the commentator taught us repeatedly in the lesson. The apparent view is that it is disliked in itself, but with the condition of being raw, as mentioned by Allama al-Sharqawi in his gloss on al-Tahrir in the chapter on wudu. So with the mentioned condition, the dislike is necessary in itself, contrary to what is understood from the previous distinction. The appropriate example for disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance is wudu with water heated by the sun.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This lengthy passage presents a detailed classification of forbidden and disliked acts according to the Shafi'i school. The author begins by clarifying the terms 'in itself' and 'due to an extrinsic circumstance' within the definition of a valid subject matter. The first definition uses the criterion of whether the ruling varies with a cause. For forbidden in itself (harām li-dhātihi), the prohibition does not vary with its cause, as illustrated by fornication (where the cause is lineage mixing, but prohibition remains even if the woman is a minor) and wine (where the cause is intoxication, but prohibition remains even if the person is immune to intoxication). For forbidden due to an extrinsic circumstance (harām li-ʿāriḍ), the prohibition varies with its cause, as wudu with usurped water (the cause is aggression, so if the water is not usurped, no prohibition). Similarly for disliked in itself (makrūh li-dhātihi), e.g., looking at the wife's vulva without need (the disgust does not vary with the fear of desire). For disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance (makrūh li-ʿāriḍ), e.g., eating raw onion (the dislike varies with causing harm to others). Then the author addresses an objection that there is no real distinction because an act like drinking can be viewed in itself as permissible, but when attached to wine it becomes forbidden; but this objection misses the point because the classification is about the ruling's attachment to the act's essence or to an external factor. Al-Ajhuri's gloss provides an alternative definition based on the type of prohibition: whether the decisive or non-decisive prohibition is attached to the act itself or to a necessary concomitant. He gives the example of prayer in usurped land: prayer itself is commanded, but the prohibition attaches to the necessary usurpation. A further objection is raised that everything has a cause, so the distinction collapses. The author replies by refining the example of onion: the correct view is that it is disliked in itself when raw, not due to an extrinsic circumstance. The appropriate example for disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance is wudu with water heated by the sun (which is disliked due to causing harm to the skin). This nuanced discussion highlights the careful reasoning of kalām in distinguishing intrinsic versus extrinsic rulings.

Key Terms
حرام لذاته
حرام لعارض
مكروه لذاته
مكروه لعارض
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the distinction is arbitrary or that all prohibited acts are intrinsic.
Correction: The passage shows that some prohibitions depend on an external cause (e.g., wudu with usurped water) and thus are extrinsic, while others (e.g., wine) are intrinsic regardless of the intoxicating potential.

Checkpoint
Why is fornication considered haram li-dhatihi?
A) Because its prohibition varies with the cause of lineage mixing.
B) Because its prohibition does not vary with the cause of lineage mixing.
C) Because it is always permissible in certain contexts.
D) Because its prohibition depends on the presence of a minor girl.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic rulings depends on whether the ruling remains constant when its cause is absent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever told yourself that a sin is 'not that bad' because you didn't experience its typical harmful effect? How does the example of drinking a non-intoxicating amount of wine challenge this reasoning? What would it take for you to accept that the Shariah's prohibition stands regardless of your personal experience?
?
The category of makruh li-dhatihi includes looking at your wife's vulva even without the fear of being overwhelmed. Is there a 'minor' or 'private' action in your life that you consider harmless because it doesn't affect anyone else? How does this lesson compel you to align your feelings with the Divine order, not your own judgment?
?
When you use something that is 'haram li-ārid', like water that belongs to someone else for wudu, do you tend to justify it by the benefit of the act itself? How can the example of wudu with usurped water train you to prioritize the underlying cause of aggression over the apparent act of worship? Commit this week to checking the external circumstances of your actions before assuming their permissibility.

Rational Judgments

Refining the Distinction: Objections and Examples (Part 2)
Objection, alternative definition, and example refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the distinction is meaningless, the alternative definition based on type of prohibition, and the refinement of the example of onion.

Core Idea
The distinction is meaningful; each category has a clear logical basis.

After establishing the four categories, the source addresses a potential objection: that the distinction collapses because any act can be considered in itself or with an attachment. The gloss by al-Ajhuri provides an alternative definition based on whether the prohibition is decisive or non-decisive and whether it attaches to the act itself or its necessary concomitant. Finally, the author refines the example of onion, showing that it is disliked in itself when raw, and suggests a better example for disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its refutation
Read the objection that no difference is understood. The author refutes it by showing the logical criterion still applies. Then read al-Ajhuri's gloss for an alternative formulation.
The gloss introduces terms like 'decisive prohibition' (nahy jazim) and 'non-decisive prohibition'.
2
Compare the two definitions
Compare the first definition (based on cause variation) with al-Ajhuri's definition (based on attachment of prohibition to act itself vs. its concomitant). See that both lead to the same classification of examples.
Prayer in usurped land is given as an example of haram li-ārid: prayer itself is commanded, but the prohibition attaches to the concomitant usurpation.
3
Analyze the refinement about onion
Notice that the author initially classifies eating raw onion as makruh li-ārid, but then corrects it: according to Shaykh al-Sharqawi, it is makruh li-dhatihi with the condition of being raw. Understand why this refinement is necessary.
The final suggestion for makruh li-ārid is wudu with water heated by the sun (water mushammas).
4
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how this discussion demonstrates the careful reasoning of kalam in distinguishing categories. The ability to refine examples shows the importance of precise definitions.
This concludes the two-part lesson.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: بِحَيْثُ لَا يَكُونُ مُحَرَّماً لِذَاتِهِ وَلَا مَكْرُوهاً لِذَاتِهِ يظهر أن المراد بالمحرم لذاته والمكروه لذاته ما لم يكن تحريمه وكراهته لعلَّةٍ يدور معها وجوداً وعَدَماً، والمحرَّمُ لعارض والمكروه لعارض ما كان تحريمه وكراهته لعلَّةٍ يدور معها وجوداً وعدماً. فالزنا وشربُ الخَمْرِ من قبيل المحرَّم لذاته؛ لأن تحريم الزنا لا يدور مع اختلاط علته - التي هي الأنساب - وجوداً وعدماً؛ إذ قد تنتفي العلَّةُ ويوجَدُ التحريمُ، كما إذا وَطِئَ رجلٌ صغيرةً، وكذلك تحريمُ شُرْبِ الخمر لا يدور مع علته - التي هي الإسكارُ؛ إذ قد ينتفي الإسكار ويوجد التحريم، كما إذا اعتاد الشخص شرب الخمر بحيث لا يُؤثِرُ في عقله شيئاً، أو شَرِبَ قدراً لا يُسْكِرُ، والوضوء بماء مغصوب من المحرَّمِ لعارض؛ لأن تحريمه يدورُ مع علته التي هي الاستيلاء على حقّ الغير عدوّاناً - وجوداً وعدماً، والنَّظَرُ لفرج الحليلة من قبيل المكروه لذاته؛ لأن كراهته لا تدور مع علته - التي هي خَوْفُ الطَّمْسِ مع عدم الحاجة؛ إذ العلة تنتفي وتوجد الكراهة كما إذا أخبره معصوم بأنه لا يحصلُ له طَمْسٌ إِذا نَظَرَ لفرج حليلته، وأكلُ البَصَلِ من المكروه لعارض؛ لأن كراهته تدور مع علته - التي هي تأذِي غيرِه ولو مَلَكاً - وجوداً وعدماً، فإذا انتفتِ العلَّةُ بأن طُبِخَ انتفتِ الكراهة.
وبهذا اندفع ما يقال: لا يُعْقَلُ فرق بين المحرَّم والمكروه لذاتهما وبين المحرم والمكروه لعارض؛ لأنه إذا نظر للشرب من حيث هو فجائز، وإن نظر لكونه متعلقاً بالخمر فتحرمُ على المحرَّمِ لذاته، وتُكْرَهُ على المكروه كذلك.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري: (قوله: لِذَاتِهِ) المراد بالمحرم لذاته ما تعلق النهي الجازم بنفسه كالزنا وشرب الخمر، وبالمكروه لذاته ما تعلق النهي غير الجازم بنفسه كأكل البصل والنظر إلى الفرج، وبالمحرم لعارض: ما تعلق النهي الجازم بلازمه كالصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة، فإن الشارع لم ينه عنها، وإنما نهى عن الغصب اللازم لها، وهو شغل مُلك الغير حال تلك الصلاة، فالصلاة نفسها مأمور بها مثاب عليها كما نقله البجيرمي على "المنهج" آخر باب الجماعة؛ لأن نفس الصلاة ليست غصباً؛ إذ لو كانت غصباً لانتفى الإثم بانتفائها حال المكث في الأرض المغصوبة واللازم باطل وبالمكروه لعارض: ما تعلق النهي غير الجازم بلازمه كأكل الخبز بالبصل، فإن أكل الخبز ليس منهياً عنه، وإنما المنهي عنه أكل البصل المصاحب له، وهذا المثال بحسب ما ظهر لنا، ولم نجد مثالاً منقولاً.
فهو حرام، كما أنه إن نظر للوضوء في ذاته فهو جائز، وإن نظر لكونه بماء مغصوب فهو حرام، وكذا يُقال في المكروه، فإن كان المراد بالمحرَّم والمكروه لذاتهما: ما كان تحريمه وكراهتُه لا لعلَّةٍ ولعارض ما كان ما ذُكِرَ لها. ورُدَّ عليه: أن للكُلّ عِلَلاً، ولا فَرْقَ.
وما تقرر من كون أكل البصل مكروهاً لعارض هو ما قرره لنا شيخنا المحشي غيرَ مرَّةٍ في الدرس، والظاهر أنه من المكروه لذاته، لكن بقَيْدِ كونه نَيْناً كما ذكره العلامةُ الشَّرْقَاوِيُّ في "حاشية التحرير" في باب الوضوء، فهو بالقيد المذكور تَلْزَمُهُ الكراهة لذاته، خلافاً لما استفيد من الفرق المتقدّم فالمناسِبُ: التمثيل للمكروه لعارض بالوضوء بالماء المشمس.
His saying: 'such that it is not forbidden in itself nor disliked in itself'. It appears that what is meant by forbidden in itself and disliked in itself is that whose prohibition and dislike are not due to a cause that varies with existence and non-existence; and forbidden due to an extrinsic circumstance and disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance is that whose prohibition and dislike are due to a cause that varies with existence and non-existence. So fornication and drinking wine are of the type of forbidden in itself, because the prohibition of fornication does not vary with the mixing of lineages—which is its cause—in existence and non-existence; for the cause may be absent while the prohibition exists, as when a man has intercourse with a minor girl. Likewise, the prohibition of drinking wine does not vary with its cause—which is intoxication; for intoxication may be absent while the prohibition exists, as when a person becomes accustomed to drinking wine such that it does not affect his intellect at all, or drinks an amount that does not intoxicate. Wudu with usurped water is of the type of forbidden due to an extrinsic circumstance, because its prohibition varies with its cause—which is taking over another's right by aggression—in existence and non-existence. Looking at the vulva of one's wife is of the type of disliked in itself, because its dislike does not vary with its cause—which is fear of being overwhelmed by desire without need; for the cause may be absent while the dislike exists, as when an infallible informant tells him that no such overwhelming will occur if he looks at his wife's vulva. Eating raw onion is of the type of disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance, because its dislike varies with its cause—which is causing harm to others, even angels—in existence and non-existence; so if the cause is removed by cooking it, the dislike is removed. By this is refuted what is said: no difference can be understood between forbidden and disliked in themselves and forbidden and disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance, because if one considers drinking in itself, it is permissible, but if one considers it as being attached to wine, then it is forbidden for that which is forbidden in itself, and disliked for that which is disliked in itself. Gloss by Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'in itself') What is meant by forbidden in itself is that to which a decisive prohibition is attached in itself, like fornication and drinking wine. By disliked in itself is that to which a non-decisive prohibition is attached in itself, like eating raw onion and looking at the vulva. By forbidden due to an extrinsic circumstance is that to which a decisive prohibition is attached as a necessary consequence, like prayer in usurped land, for the Lawgiver did not prohibit prayer itself, but prohibited the usurpation that is necessary for it, which is occupying another's property during that prayer. So prayer itself is commanded and rewarded, as al-Bujayrimi transmitted from al-Minhaj at the end of the chapter on congregation, because prayer itself is not usurpation; for if it were usurpation, then the sin would be removed by its non-existence while staying in the usurped land, and the conclusion is false. By disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance is that to which a non-decisive prohibition is attached as a necessary consequence, like eating bread with onion, for eating bread is not prohibited; only the accompanying eating of onion is prohibited. This example is according to what appeared to us, and we have not found a transmitted example. So it is forbidden, just as if one considers wudu in itself, it is permissible, but if one considers it as being with usurped water, it is forbidden. And the same is said regarding the disliked. If what is meant by forbidden and disliked in themselves is that whose prohibition and dislike are not due to a cause, and by due to an extrinsic circumstance is that which has a cause as mentioned, then it is objected: that everything has causes, and there is no difference. What has been established regarding eating raw onion being disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance is what our teacher the commentator taught us repeatedly in the lesson. The apparent view is that it is disliked in itself, but with the condition of being raw, as mentioned by Allama al-Sharqawi in his gloss on al-Tahrir in the chapter on wudu. So with the mentioned condition, the dislike is necessary in itself, contrary to what is understood from the previous distinction. The appropriate example for disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance is wudu with water heated by the sun.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This lengthy passage presents a detailed classification of forbidden and disliked acts according to the Shafi'i school. The author begins by clarifying the terms 'in itself' and 'due to an extrinsic circumstance' within the definition of a valid subject matter. The first definition uses the criterion of whether the ruling varies with a cause. For forbidden in itself (harām li-dhātihi), the prohibition does not vary with its cause, as illustrated by fornication (where the cause is lineage mixing, but prohibition remains even if the woman is a minor) and wine (where the cause is intoxication, but prohibition remains even if the person is immune to intoxication). For forbidden due to an extrinsic circumstance (harām li-ʿāriḍ), the prohibition varies with its cause, as wudu with usurped water (the cause is aggression, so if the water is not usurped, no prohibition). Similarly for disliked in itself (makrūh li-dhātihi), e.g., looking at the wife's vulva without need (the disgust does not vary with the fear of desire). For disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance (makrūh li-ʿāriḍ), e.g., eating raw onion (the dislike varies with causing harm to others). Then the author addresses an objection that there is no real distinction because an act like drinking can be viewed in itself as permissible, but when attached to wine it becomes forbidden; but this objection misses the point because the classification is about the ruling's attachment to the act's essence or to an external factor. Al-Ajhuri's gloss provides an alternative definition based on the type of prohibition: whether the decisive or non-decisive prohibition is attached to the act itself or to a necessary concomitant. He gives the example of prayer in usurped land: prayer itself is commanded, but the prohibition attaches to the necessary usurpation. A further objection is raised that everything has a cause, so the distinction collapses. The author replies by refining the example of onion: the correct view is that it is disliked in itself when raw, not due to an extrinsic circumstance. The appropriate example for disliked due to an extrinsic circumstance is wudu with water heated by the sun (which is disliked due to causing harm to the skin). This nuanced discussion highlights the careful reasoning of kalām in distinguishing intrinsic versus extrinsic rulings.

النهي غير الجازم
لازم
ماء مشمس

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the distinction is merely a matter of perspective (e.g., drinking vs. drinking wine).
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is about the inherent attachment of the prohibition to the act or to its necessary concomitant, not just perspective.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ajhuri's gloss, what makes prayer in usurped land haram li-ārid?
A) The prayer itself is prohibited.
B) The prohibition attaches to the concomitant usurpation, not to the prayer.
C) The prayer is invalid because of the usurped land.
D) The prohibition is due to the water used.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic rulings is refined through scholarly discussion, and examples are adjusted to fit the logical criteria precisely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars in this passage refine their examples based on precise logical criteria. When you learn a ruling, do you accept it as it is, or do you seek to understand the deeper reason (ʿilla) behind it? How can you train yourself to think more deeply about your religious knowledge?

Consider an action you perform that may have both intrinsic value and attached circumstances (like praying in a place with questionable ownership). Does the knowledge that the act itself is rewarded despite external flaws give you hope or make you more careful? Definitions of Prohibited and Disliked Acts by Essence and by Circumstance
Al-Bajuri defines and exemplifies the four categories of ruled acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between rulings that attach to an act's essence versus those that attach to a circumstance, and the role of definitive vs. non-definitive prohibition in Islamic legal theory.

Core Idea
An act's ruling may be due to its essence or an external circumstance; this distinction is fundamental.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of ruled acts into four categories: prohibited by essence, disliked by essence, prohibited by circumstance, and disliked by circumstance. Al-Bajuri defines each with examples and explains how the prohibition applies either directly or to a necessary concomitant. These distinctions are essential for understanding how rulings attach to actions in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Read the passage and list the four types: haram li-dhatihi, makruh li-dhatihi, haram li-ghayrihi, makruh li-ghayrihi. Notice the definitions.
The author defines each in turn.
2
Understand the two types of prohibition
Notice the distinction between nahy jazim (definitive prohibition) and nahy ghayr jazim (non-definitive). The first applies to prohibited acts, the second to disliked.
This is key for distinguishing haram from makruh.
3
Analyze the circumstance examples
For haram li-ghayrihi, prayer in usurped land: the prohibition attaches to the concomitant usurpation, not the prayer itself. For makruh li-ghayrihi, eating bread with onion: the onion is the disliked element.
Notice how the act itself remains commanded/permissible.
4
Review the additional discussion
The passage includes a discussion about whether acts have causes, and a clarification about eating raw onion being disliked by essence with a condition. This shows the nuance.
Focus on the main definitions; the additional discussion is for depth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: لِذَاتِهِ المراد بالمحرم لذاته ما تعلق النهي الجازم بنفسه كالزنا وشرب الخمر، وبالمكروه لذاته ما تعلق النهي غير الجازم بنفسه كأكل البصل والنظر إلى الفرج، وبالمحرم لعارض: ما تعلق النهي الجازم بلازمه كالصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة، فإن الشارع لم ينه عنها، وإنما نهى عن الغصب اللازم لها، وهو شغل مُلك الغير حال تلك الصلاة، فالصلاة نفسها مأمور بها مثاب عليها كما نقله البجيرمي على "المنهج" آخر باب الجماعة؛ لأن نفس الصلاة ليست غصباً؛ إذ لو كانت غصباً لانتفى الإثم بانتفائها حال المكث في الأرض المغصوبة واللازم باطل وبالمكروه لعارض: ما تعلق النهي غير الجازم بلازمه كأكل الخبز بالبصل، فإن أكل الخبز ليس منهياً عنه، وإنما المنهي عنه أكل البصل المصاحب له، وهذا المثال بحسب ما ظهر لنا، ولم نجد مثالاً منقولاً. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي
فهو حرام، كما أنه إن نظر للوضوء في ذاته فهو جائز ، وإن نظر لكونه بماء مغصوب فهو حرام، وكذا يُقال في المكروه، فإن كان المراد بالمحرَّم والمكروه لذاتهما: ما كان تحريمه وكراهتُه لا لعلَّةٍ ولعارض ما كان ما ذُكِرَ لها. ورُدَّ عليه : أن للكُلّ عِلَلاً، ولا فَرْقَ.
وما تقرر من كون أكل البصل مكروهاً لعارض هو ما قرره لنا شيخنا المحشي غيرَ مرَّةٍ في الدرس، والظاهر أنه من المكروه لذاته، لكن بقَيْدِ كونه نَيْناً كما ذكره العلامةُ الشَّرْقَاوِيُّ في "حاشية التحرير" في باب الوضوء، فهو بالقيد المذكور تَلْزَمُهُ الكراهة لذاته، خلافاً لما استفيد من الفرق المتقدّم فالمناسِبُ : التمثيل للمكروه لعارض بالوضوء بالماء المشمس.)
(His saying: 'by essence' – the intended meaning of 'prohibited by essence' is that which the definitive prohibition pertains to itself, like adultery and drinking wine. And 'disliked by essence' is that which the non-definitive prohibition pertains to itself, like eating raw onion and looking at the private part. And 'prohibited by circumstance' is that which the definitive prohibition pertains to its necessary concomitant, like prayer in usurped land, for the Lawgiver did not prohibit prayer itself, but rather prohibited the usurpation that is necessary for it – which is occupying someone else's property during that prayer. So prayer itself is commanded and rewarded, as al-Bujayrimi transmitted in his gloss on 'al-Minhaj' at the end of the chapter on congregation, because prayer itself is not usurpation; for if it were usurpation, the sin would be removed by its absence while staying in the usurped land, and the concomitant is false. And 'disliked by circumstance' is that which the non-definitive prohibition pertains to its necessary concomitant, like eating bread with onion; for eating bread is not prohibited, rather the prohibited thing is the onion accompanying it. This example is according to what appeared to us, and we did not find a transmitted example. – Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi
So it is forbidden, just as if one considers ablution in itself, it is permissible, and if one considers it as being with usurped water, it is forbidden. And similarly is said regarding the disliked. If what is meant by 'prohibited and disliked by essence' is that which its prohibition or dislikedness is not due to a cause or circumstance – then whatever was mentioned for them [i.e., the examples] would have a cause. And it was objected: that everything has causes, and there is no difference.
And what was settled about eating onion being disliked by circumstance is what our teacher the glossator (al-Muhashshi) taught us repeatedly in class. Yet apparently it is disliked by essence, but with the condition of being raw, as the scholar al-Sharqawi mentioned in his gloss on 'al-Tahrir' in the chapter on ablution. So with that condition, the dislikedness necessarily pertains to its essence, contrary to what is understood from the previous distinction. So the suitable example for disliked by circumstance is ablution with sun-heated water.)
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the four categories of ruled acts in Islamic law: prohibited by essence (harām li-dhātihi), disliked by essence (makrūh li-dhātihi), prohibited by circumstance (harām li-ghayrihī), and disliked by circumstance (makrūh li-ghayrihī). The author begins by defining each. 'Prohibited by essence' is an act that the definitive prohibition (al-nahy al-jāzim) applies to itself, e.g., zinā (adultery) and drinking wine. 'Disliked by essence' is an act that the non-definitive prohibition applies to itself, e.g., eating raw onion (akl al-baṣal) and looking at the private part (al-naẓar ilā al-faraj). 'Prohibited by circumstance' is an act where the definitive prohibition applies to its necessary concomitant (lāzim), not to the act itself. The classic example is prayer in a usurped land (al-ṣalāh fī al-arḍ al-maghṣūbah): the Lawgiver did not prohibit prayer itself—in fact, prayer is commanded and rewarded—but he prohibited the usurpation that accompanies it, i.e., occupying the owner's property during prayer. The author explains this reasoning and cites al-Bujayrimi's transmission. 'Disliked by circumstance' is similar, with a non-definitive prohibition applying to a concomitant. The example given is eating bread with onion: bread itself is not prohibited, but the onion that accompanies it is disliked. The author notes this example is based on what appeared to them, and they did not find a transmitted example. The passage then includes a note by al-Anbabi clarifying that looking at ablution in itself is permissible, but if done with usurped water, it becomes forbidden. A discussion follows about whether the categories are distinguished by absence of cause: if prohibited by essence means no cause, then the examples given do have causes, and it's objected that everything has causes. The author ends with a clarification from his teacher: apparently, eating raw onion is disliked by essence when it is raw (as stated by al-Sharqawi), and the suitable example for disliked by circumstance is ablution with sun-heated water (al-wuḍū' bi al-mā' al-mushammas). This passage guards against confusing whether a ruling attaches to the act itself or to an external circumstance.

Key Terms
المحرم لذاته
المكروه لذاته
المحرم لعارض
المكروه لعارض
النهي الجازم
النهي غير الجازم
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prayer in usurped land is prohibited (haram) in itself because of the location.
Correction: Prayer itself is commanded and rewarded; the prohibition attaches to the concomitant act of usurpation (occupying the owner's property), not to the prayer.

Checkpoint
According to the definitions, why is prayer in usurped land considered 'prohibited by circumstance' (harām li-ghayrihī)?
A) Because prayer itself is forbidden in such a place.
B) Because the prohibition applies to the necessary concomitant, which is usurpation.
C) Because the prohibition is non-definitive.
D) Because prayer is not commanded in that situation.

Key Takeaway
An act's legal ruling may derive from its essence or from a necessary concomitant; this distinction clarifies why the same act can be ruled differently depending on context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a worship you perform regularly. Is there any circumstance—like location, time, or possessions—that might change its ruling? Are you willing to examine and correct it today?
?
When you engage in a permissible act like eating, do you consider whether it might be connected to something disliked or prohibited? How can you become more conscious of the circumstances surrounding your actions?
?
The lesson shows that even the highest act of worship (prayer) can be tainted by a forbidden circumstance. In your own life, are there spiritual acts you render defective by ignoring their lawful prerequisites? What will you change starting now?

The Ash'ari Identity View and Its Resolution
Al-Anbabi presents the majority Ash'ari view that the name is identical to the named, with proofs and counter-response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on the identity of name and named, the proofs cited, and the resolution that clarifies the meaning of 'name'.

Core Idea
The name is identical to the named when 'name' means the denoted meaning.

This passage from al-Anbabi's taqrirat presents the majority Ash'ari view that the name is identical to the named. It cites Quranic verses and poetry as evidence, then addresses a critique, concluding with a resolution that distinguishes between different meanings of 'name'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three distinct proofs and the back-and-forth between objections and response.
2
Identify the majority view and the proofs
The passage states that most Ash'aris say the name is identical to the named. Find the three proofs: two Qur'anic verses and a line of poetry.
The verses are Sabbih isma rabbika and ma ta'buduna min dunihi illa asma'. The poetry is by Labid.
3
Understand Al-Sa'd's objection
Al-Sa'd in Sharh al-Maqasid argues that these proofs actually imply distinction (al-mughayara) because glorification and worship are directed to the essences, not the names.
He suggests the word 'name' in the verse is either for indicating that the deities are nothing or is redundant.
4
See the counter-response and resolution
The counter-response says that Al-Sa'd's interpretation already assumes distinct named objects and names, so it doesn't prove distinction. The resolution: if by 'name' is meant the denoted meaning, it is identical; if literal, it is other.
This resolves the apparent disagreement between the view and the objection.
5
Reflect on the resolution's nuance
Consider how the debate hinges on what 'name' means. The resolution shows that the Ash'ari position is not absolute but depends on intended meaning.
This is a key epistemological point in Islamic theology: clarifying terms resolves many disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: أَنَّهُ غَيْرُ الْمُسَمَّى) الحاصل أن أكثرَ الأَشَاعِرَةِ قالوا: الاسمُ عَيْنُ المسمّى بدليل قوله تعالى: (سَبِّحِ اسْمَ رَبِّكَ ) ، ( مَا تَعْبُدُونَ مِن دُونِهِ إِلَّا أَسْمَاء )، وقول لَبِيد العامِرِيّ يخاطب ابنتيه في النَّيَاحَةِ عليه(۳): فَقُومَا وَقُولَا بِالَّذِي تَعْرِفَانِهِ وَلَا تَخْمِشَا وَجْهاً وَلَا تَحْلِفَا شَعْرَا إِلَى الْحَوْلِ ثُمَّ اسْمُ السَّلَامِ عَلَيْكُمَا وَمَنْ يَبْكِ حَوْلاً كَامَلاً فَقَدِ اعْتَذَرَا قال السَّعْدُ في "شرح المقاصد": وفي الاستدلال بالآيتين اعتراف بالمغايرة حيث يقال: التسبيحُ والعبادة للذوات دون الأسماء، على أن التسبيحَ يَصِحُ لنفس الاسم بمعنى: تنزيهه عما ينافي التعظيم كما في البَيْضَاوِي، والعبادة تتعلق به ظاهراً لغرض الإشارة إلى أن هذه الآلهة عَدَمٌ في حضرة الألوهية، فكأنها أسماء لا مُسَمَّيات لها، ولفظ (اسم) في البيت مُقْحَم إشارةً إلى أنه ليس سلاماً حقيقياً؛ إذ هما لا يأمنان بعده. وقد يقال: لا اعتراف بالمغايرة في الاستدلال بالآيتين؛ لأن قولهم: التسبيح والعبادة للذات دون الأسماء على زَعْمِ القائل بأن هناك مسمياتٍ وأسماء. نعم؛ إن أَريد به المدلول؛ فهو عين المسمى، وعليه يُحْمَلُ كلام من أطلق أنه عَيْنُ المسمّى.
(His saying: 'That it is other than the named') The result is that most Ash'aris said: The name is identical to the named, as evidence by His saying, 'Glorify the name of your Lord', and 'You do not worship besides Him except names', and the saying of Labid al-'Amiri addressing his two daughters concerning lamenting over him: 'So stand and say that which you know, and do not scratch a face nor cut hair, until the year ends, then the name of peace be upon you both; whoever weeps for a full year has excused himself.' Al-Sa'd said in Sharh al-Maqasid: In the inference from the two verses there is an admission of distinction, since it is said: praising and worshiping are for the essences, not the names, considering that praising is valid for the name itself in the sense of declaring it free from what contradicts veneration, as in al-Baydawi, and worship attaches to it apparently for the purpose of indicating that these deities are nothing in the presence of divinity, as if they are names with no named objects, and the word 'name' in the verse [sic: actually the line of poetry] is redundant to indicate that it is not a real greeting, for they are not safe after him. And it may be said: There is no admission of distinction in the inference from the two verses, because their saying that praising and worshiping are for the essences and not the names is based on the claim that there are named objects and names. Yes, if by it [the name] is meant the denoted meaning, then it is identical to the named, and upon this is carried the speech of him who states that it is identical to the named.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage, from al-Anbabi's taqrirat, presents the view of the majority of Ash'aris that the name is identical to the named (al-ism 'ayn al-musamma). It gives three proofs: two Qur'anic verses (Sabbih isma rabbika and what you worship besides Him are but names) and a line of poetry by Labid. Then al-Sa'd in his Sharh al-Maqasid argues that these proofs actually imply distinction (al-mughayara) because the acts of glorification and worship are directed to the essences themselves, not to the names; the word 'name' in the verse is either for the purpose of indicating that the objects of worship are nothing, or it is redundant. Then a counter-response is given: this interpretation of the proofs already assumes that there are distinct named objects and names, so it doesn't prove distinction. Finally, the resolution is stated: if by 'name' is meant the denoted meaning (al-madlul), then it is identical to the named; if it is meant literally, then it is other. This resolves the apparent disagreement.

المسمى
عين المسمى

شرح المقاصد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari position is that the name is absolutely identical to the named without any qualification.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is the view of most (not all) Ash'aris, and the resolution depends on what is meant by 'name'—if the denoted meaning, then identical; otherwise, distinct.

Checkpoint

A) The name is always other than the named.
B) The name is always identical to the named.
C) If by 'name' is meant the denoted meaning, it is identical; if meant literally, it is other.
D) The debate is meaningless.

Key Takeaway
The identity of name and named depends on what is meant by 'name'; if the denoted meaning, they are identical; otherwise they are distinct.

?
The resolution says the name is identical to the named when we mean the denoted meaning. Additional Proofs That the Name Is Other Than the Named
Al-Anbabi presents scriptural, rational, and absurdity arguments for name-named distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural, rational, and reductio ad absurdum arguments for the distinction between a name and its named entity in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Names are not identical to their named entities—otherwise saying 'fire' would burn your mouth.

This lesson examines further arguments from the Quran, reason, and poetry that establish the name as other than the named. Al-Anbabi presents a verse from Surah Taha, a logical difficulty with multiple names for one thing, and a vivid absurdity to drive home the point. These proofs reinforce the broader theological stance that divine names are not identical to God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow. Notice the three distinct arguments.
2
Identify the three arguments
The passage gives three proofs: a Quranic verse, a rational argument about multiplicity, and a reductio ad absurdum. Find each one.
3
Understand the rational argument
Why is it impossible for multiple names to be identical to one named entity? Think about what it means for a single thing to be multiple distinct things.
This is the core logical point.
4
Consider the absurdity
If the name were identical to the named, what would happen when saying 'fire'? This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opposite leads to an impossible conclusion.
It is not meant literally, but as a logical proof.
5
Reflect on the poetic evidence
The passage mentions al-Busiri's Hamziyya as supporting the distinction. Consider how poetry can be used as evidence in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: إن الاسم غيرُ المسمّى لقوله تعالى: لَهُ الْأَسْمَاءُ الْحُسْنَى، ولا بُدَّ من المغايرة بين الشيء وما هو له، ولتعذُّرِ الأسماء مع اتحاد المسمّى، ولو كان عَيْنَهُ لاحترق فَمُ من قال: نار، إلى غير ذلك من المفاسد. وعلى المغايَرَةِ ظاهرُ قول صاحب "الهَمْزِيَّةِ "(۲):
And it is said: The name is other than the named because of His saying: 'To Him belong the most beautiful names', and there must be distinction between a thing and that which belongs to it, and because of the impossibility of [multiple] names with the named being one, and if it [the name] were identical to it, then the mouth of the one who says 'fire' would burn, and other such absurdities. And the apparent meaning of the saying of the author of al-Hamziyya indicates distinction:
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage, from al-Anbabi's taqrirat, provides additional proofs for the position that the name is other than the named. It cites the Quranic verse 'To Him belong the most beautiful names' (20:8), arguing that there must be a distinction between a thing and its attributes/names. It then gives a rational argument: if there are multiple names for one named entity, it is impossible for them all to be identical to that entity, because a single thing cannot be multiple distinct things. Furthermore, practical absurdities follow: if the word 'fire' were identical to the actual fire, then saying 'fire' would burn the mouth. The passage concludes by noting that the author of the Hamziyya (al-Busiri) also indicates the distinction, though the quote is not fully given.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument that saying 'fire' would burn is meant literally rather than as a logical absurdity.
Correction: The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: it assumes the opposite (that name is identical to named) and shows it leads to an impossible consequence, thereby proving the original claim.

B) There would be no need for names.

Key Takeaway
The name is distinct from the named, as proven by reason, revelation, and poetic testimony.

What steps can you take to move from knowing the name to truly knowing the Named?
