The Dispute over Name and Named: Clarification and Proofs
Al-Ajhuri explains the dispute over whether the name is identical to the named and cites proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the dispute over whether the name is identical to the named hinges on the intended meaning of 'name'—as the word or as the essence.

Core Idea
The name is either the word or the essence; confusion arises from ambiguous usage.

In this passage, al-Ajhuri resolves a long-standing theological dispute by clarifying that the disagreement over whether the name is the same as the named is really a matter of definition. When 'name' refers to the essence, it is identical; when it refers to the verbal expression, it is distinct. This simple distinction, supported by rational arguments and scriptural evidence, dissolves the apparent controversy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core question
Identify the dispute: Is the name (ism) identical to the named (musamma) or distinct? Read the passage to see how al-Ajhuri frames the issue.
2
Distinguish the two uses of 'name'
Notice that al-Ajhuri explains that the dispute is about the referent (madlul) of 'name', not the word itself. He shows that if by 'name' we mean the essence, it is the same; if the word, then distinct.
The word 'name' itself is always other than the essence by agreement.
3
Examine the proofs for distinction
Review the Quranic verse 'To Him belong the Most Beautiful Names' and the argument that many names would be impossible with one named, and the absurdity of saying 'fire' and burning.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider that the dispute is unnecessary once we clarify the intended meaning. Al-Zad's commentary indicates that rational people should not argue over this ambiguity.
The passage concludes by citing a poetic line from al-Busiri's al-Hamziyya as supporting the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم؛ إن أَريد به المدلول؛ فهو عين المسمى، وعليه يُحْمَلُ كلام من أطلق أنه عَيْنُ المسمّى. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري. (وقوله: نَعَمْ إِنْ أُرِيدَ بِهِ الْمَدْلُولُ ... إلخ) بأن أريد بالاسم: الذات، فتحصل من كلامه أن لفظ (اسم) إن أريد به: الأسماء الدالة على الذات، أو استعمل مطلقاً بأن لم يرد به الأسماء ولا الذات؛ كان محمولاً على الأسماء، وإن أريد به الذات؛ كان محمولاً عليها، وكان مدلوله حينئذ : عين المسمى، وإذا علمت أن محل الخلاف هو مدلول (اسم) تعلم أن لفظ اسم غير الذات اتفاقاً؛ إذ لا يعقل عاقل أن المركب من الحروف والأصوات يكون متحداً بالذات، وهذا الخلاف جار في ماصدقات الاسم كلها، ومنها: لفظة اسم التي في البسملة مثلاً كما وجدته بهامش الرملي في الكلام على البسملة. وفي زاده على البيضاوي ما حاصله أن الخلاف في كون الاسم غير المسمى أو عين المسمى مما لا ينبغي أن يقع بين العقلاء، فإنه إن أريد به: اللفظ كما في قولك: كتبت زيداً، فهو غير المسمى ضرورة، وإن أريد به الذات كما في قولك: جاء زيد؛ فهو عين المسمى ضرورة ثم قال والظاهر أن هذا الخلاف مبني على أن الاسم قد يطلق ویراد به اللفظ كما في قولك: كتبت زيداً، وقد يطلق ويراد به: الذات كما في قولك: كتب زيد بالبناء للفاعل، وقد يطلق بلا قرينة ترجح أن المراد به اللفظ أو الذات كما في قولك: رأيت زيداً، فإنه محتمل لأن يكون رأى ذاته ولأن يكون رأى لفظ زيد مرسوماً على حائط مثلاً، فمن قال : إن الاسم غير المسمى؛ يحمله على أن المراد: أنه رأى اللفظ، تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي. وقيل: إن الاسم غيرُ المسمّى لقوله تعالى: لَهُ الْأَسْمَاءُ الْحُسْنَ ()، ولا بُدَّ من المغايرة بين الشيء وما هو له، ولتعذُّرِ الأسماء مع اتحاد المسمّى، ولو كان عَيْنَهُ لاحترق فَمُ من قال: نار، إلى غير ذلك من المفاسد. وعلى المغايَرَةِ ظاهرُ قول صاحب الهَمْزِيَّةِ (١): (١) من سورة طه ٨ الله (٢) صاحب الهمزية هو محمد بن سعيد الصنهاجي البوصيري المصري، شرف الدين، أبو عبد ،شاعر حسن الديباجة مليح المعاني أشهر شعره البردة ومطلعها أمن تذكر جيران بذي سلم، والهمزية، ومطلعها: كيف ترقى رقيك الأنبياء، توفي سنة (٦٩٦هـ).
Yes, if by it is meant the referent, then it is the very same as the named, and upon this is carried the speech of one who says it is the very same as the named. Marginal gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri. (His words: 'Yes, if by it is meant the referent... etc.') meaning that if by 'name' is meant the essence, then from his speech it is concluded that the word 'name', if by it is meant the names indicating the essence, or if used absolutely without intending names nor the essence, then it is taken as referring to names; but if by it is meant the essence, then it is taken as referring to it, and its referent then is the very same as the named. And when you know that the point of dispute is the referent of 'name', you know that the word 'name' is distinct from the essence by agreement, for no rational person can conceive that a compound of letters and sounds is identical with the essence. And this dispute applies to all the referents of 'name', including the word 'name' in the basmala, for example, as I found in the marginal gloss of al-Ramli concerning the discussion of the basmala. And in his 'Zad' on al-Baydawi, the summary is that the dispute over whether the name is other than the named or the very same as the named should not occur among rational people, because if by it is meant the word, as in your saying 'I wrote Zayd', then it is other than the named necessarily, and if by it is meant the essence, as in your saying 'Zayd came', then it is the very same as the named necessarily. Then he said: and it is apparent that this dispute is based on the fact that 'name' may be used and by it is meant the word, as in your saying 'I wrote Zayd', and may be used and by it is meant the essence, as in your saying 'Zayd wrote' (with active construction), and may be used without a context that tips the scale towards the intended meaning of the word or the essence, as in your saying 'I saw Zayd', for it is probable either that he saw his essence or that he saw the word 'Zayd' written on a wall, for example. So the one who says the name is other than the named construes it as meaning that he saw the word—decisions of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi. And it is said: the name is other than the named, due to His statement, the Exalted: 'To Him belong the Most Beautiful Names' (Quran 20:8), and there must be difference between a thing and that which belongs to it, and due to the impossibility of many names with a single named, and if it were the very same, the mouth of one who said 'fire' would burn, etcetera from other absurdities. And in favor of difference is the apparent meaning of the saying of the author of al-Hamziyya: (1) from Surah Taha 8 (2) the author of al-Hamziyya is Muhammad ibn Sa'id al-Sanhaji al-Busiri al-Misri, Sharf al-Din, Abu 'Abd, a poet with fine style and pleasant meanings; his most famous poems are al-Burda, which begins 'a-min tadhakkuri jiranin bi-dhi salami', and al-Hamziyya, which begins 'kayfa tarqa ruqiyaka al-anbiya'; he died in 696 AH.)
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (hashiya) by al-Ajhuri on Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin, specifically on the question of whether 'ism' (the name) is identical to 'al-musamma' (the named) or distinct. The passage begins by clarifying that the dispute is about the referent (al-madlul) of the word 'ism'. If 'ism' is taken to mean the essence (al-dhat), then it is the very same as the named; if taken to mean the verbal expression, then it is distinct. Al-Ajhuri then explains that the point of contention is the referent of 'ism', not the word itself; everyone agrees that the articulated word composed of letters is not identical to the essence. He notes that this dispute applies to all instances of 'name', including the phrase 'bismillah'. The passage then cites al-Zad's commentary on al-Baydawi, which argues that the dispute is unnecessary because it is simply a matter of ambiguity: when 'name' means the word, it is distinct; when it means the essence, it is identical. Al-Ajhuri then presents an argument for the view that the name is other than the named, using the Quranic verse 'To Him belong the Most Beautiful Names' to show that there must be difference between a thing and what belongs to it, and also using the absurdity that if the name were identical to the thing, then saying 'fire' would burn one's mouth. He also cites a poetic line from al-Busiri's al-Hamziyya as supporting the distinction. Throughout, the author emphasizes that the confusion arises from not specifying the intended meaning of 'name'.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
المدلول
الذات
اللفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the dispute is about the word 'name' itself, but it is actually about the referent (madlul) of 'name'.
Correction: The passage clarifies that everyone agrees the word 'name' as a verbal expression is distinct from the essence; the dispute is about what the word 'name' refers to.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key to resolving the dispute over whether the name is the same as the named?
A) Whether the name is derived or not
B) Whether by 'name' is meant the word (lafz) or the essence (dhat)
C) Whether the name is Arabic or non-Arabic
D) Whether the named is God or a creature

Key Takeaway
The dispute over whether the name is identical to the named is resolved by clarifying whether 'name' refers to the word or the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Bismillah' before an action, do you consciously distinguish between uttering a word and invoking the very Being of Allah? How might that awareness change the way you begin your task?

The passage shows that confusion over words can lead to absurdities like believing saying 'fire' would burn. Are there any words or phrases in your faith that you have taken too literally, missing their deeper referent? What step will you take to correct that today?

If the word 'Allah' is not identical to Allah, what does that imply about your relationship with the word? Will you treat the utterance of His name more reverently now, knowing it points to something infinitely greater than the sound?

The Nine Combinations of Lam and Al in 'Al-Hamd'
Bajuri enumerates the nine possible combinations of lam and al in 'al-hamd' and states the impossibility of one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nine possible combinations of the prepositions lam and al in the phrase 'al-hamd' and which combination is impossible according to Bajuri.

Core Idea
The grammar of 'al-hamd' holds theological consequences: the eternal cannot be possessed.

In this passage, Bajuri begins by analyzing the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' through the lens of Arabic grammar. He considers the possible meanings of the lam and the definite article al, each having three possibilities, leading to nine combinations. The analysis is not merely grammatical—it touches on the nature of praise and ownership in relation to Allah. This lesson walks through the enumeration and identifies which combination is deemed impossible and why.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three meanings of lam
Learn the three possible meanings of the preposition lam: possession (الملك), entitlement (الاستحقاق), and attribution (الاختصاص).
2
Understand the three meanings of al
Learn the three possible meanings of the definite article al: universality (الاستغراق), specific reference (العهد), and gender (الجنس).
3
Multiply to get nine combinations
Multiply the three lam meanings by the three al meanings to get nine possible interpretations.
This is a combinatorial analysis.
4
Identify the impossible combination
Bajuri states that one combination is impossible: lam for possession combined with al for specific reference when the specific refers to the eternal praise only. Understand why the eternal cannot be possessed.
The theological reason: the eternal cannot be owned.
5
Consider exceptions
If the specified praise is composite (e.g., praise of Allah and His prophets), then it becomes temporal and possession is possible.
This exception shows the nuance of the analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو للاستغراق، أو للعَهْدِ، فيتحصل من ذلك احتمالات تِسْعَةٌ قائمةٌ من ضَرْب ثلاثة في مثلها، يمتنع منها واحد وهو جَعْلُ اللام للمُلك مع جعل (أل) للعهد إذا جُعِلَ المعهود: الحمد القديم فقط؛ لأن القديم لا يُمْلَكُ بخلاف ما إذا جُعِلَ الحمدُ المعهودُ: مَنْ يُعْتَدُّ بحمده كحمده تعالى وحَمْدِ أنبيائه و أصفيائه؛ لأن المعهود حينئذ هو المجموع المركَبُ من القديم والحادث، وما تركَّبَ منهما فهو حادث. وأما إن جُعِلَتْ (أل) للاستغراق؛ فيصحُ جَعْلُ اللام للملك بالنظر للأفراد الحادثة، أو للاستحقاق أو للاختصاص بالنظر للأفراد القديمة، وإن لوحِظَ المجموع؛ صَحْ جَعْلُها للملك أيضاً، وإن جُعِلَتْ للجنس؛ صَحْ جَعْلُها للملك بالنظر لتحقق الجنس في ضمن الأفراد الحادثة، أو للاستحقاق أو للاختصاص بالنظر لتحققه في الأفراد القديمة ما لم يلاحظ المجموع كما في الذي قبله.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري.
ثم ما ذكره من أن اللام هنا محتملة للمعاني الثلاثة ذكره ابن قاسم في "حاشيته على جمع الجوامع"، لكنه مخالف لما في حاشية الصبان على الأشموني" حيث فرق بين الثلاثة بأن لام الملك هي الواقعة بين ذاتين ثانيتهما تملك، ولام الاختصاص: هي الواقعة بين ذاتين ثانيتهما لا تملك ولام الاستحقاق: هي الواقعة بين معنى وذات. فقضية هذا الفرق: أن تكون اللام هنا للاستحقاق فقط.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبايي.
فحينئذٍ لا يظهرُ جَعْلُ اللام هنا للاختصاص إلا بالنَّظَرِ للاستعمال المُشَار إليه بقولنا فيما تقدم: وقد يطلقون... إلخ، ولا يظهر هنا أيضاً جَعْلُها للملك إلا أن يكون هناك طريقةً أخرى غير ما تقدم. فحرّر.
Or for universality, or for specific reference; thus from that result nine possibilities arising from multiplying three by three, one of which is impossible: making the lam for possession with making 'al' for specific reference when the specific referred to is the eternal praise only, because the eternal cannot be possessed. Unlike if the specific praise is made to be one whose praise is considered, such as His praise and the praise of His prophets and saints, because then the specified is the composite of the eternal and the temporal, and what is composed of both is temporal. And if 'al' is made for universality, then making the lam for possession is valid with regard to temporal individuals, or for entitlement or attribution with regard to eternal individuals, and if the whole is considered, making it for possession is also valid. And if it is made for gender, then making the lam for possession is valid with regard to the realization of the gender within temporal individuals, or for entitlement or attribution with regard to its realization in eternal individuals, as long as the whole is not considered, as in the previous case. Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: Then what he mentioned, that the lam here is possible for the three meanings, was mentioned by Ibn Qasim in his gloss on Jam' al-Jawami', but it contradicts what is in the gloss of al-Sabban on al-Ushmuni, where he distinguished between the three: the lam of possession is that which occurs between two entities the second of which is owned; the lam of attribution is that which occurs between two entities the second of which is not owned; and the lam of entitlement is that which occurs between a meaning and an entity. The consequence of this distinction is that the lam here is for entitlement only. Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: So then the making of the lam here for attribution is not apparent except by considering the usage referred to by our saying earlier: 'and they sometimes use...' etc., and also here the making of it for possession is not apparent unless there is another way besides what preceded. So investigate.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage from the hashiya analyzes the grammatical and theological implications of the phrase 'al-hamd' in 'al-hamdu lillah'. The author first enumerates the possible meanings of the prepositional lam (ل) and the definite article al (أل). Since each has three possible meanings, the total combinations are nine. The lam can be for possession (الملك), entitlement (الاستحقاق), or attribution (الاختصاص). The al can be for universality (الاستغراق), specific reference (العهد), or gender (الجنس). The author then states that only one combination is impossible: lam for possession with al for specific reference when the specified is the eternal praise alone, because the eternal cannot be possessed. However, if the specified praise is a composite of eternal and temporal (such as the praise of God and His prophets), then it is temporal and can be possessed. Next, the author explains the permissibility of other combinations: if al is for universality, lam for possession is valid for temporal instances; for eternal instances, lam for entitlement or attribution applies. Similarly for al for gender. Then al-Ajhuri's gloss cites a disagreement: Ibn Qasim said lam has all three possibilities, but al-Sabban distinguished them as above, concluding that lam here is only for entitlement. Finally, al-Anbabi's note says that making lam for attribution is only valid if based on a special usage, and lam for possession is also not apparent unless there is another approach. The passage ends with 'so investigate', indicating this is an unsettled issue.

Key Terms
اللام
أل
الملك
الاستحقاق
الاختصاص
الاستغراق
العهد
الجنس
القديم
الحادث
المركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The eternal praise of Allah cannot be mentioned in a possessive grammatical structure.
Correction: The issue is not that the eternal praise cannot be mentioned, but that if lam is for possession and al is for specific reference to eternal praise only, that implies ownership of the eternal, which is impossible because the eternal cannot be possessed. However, if the praise is composite including temporal praise, it can be possessed.

Checkpoint
Which combination of lam and al is deemed impossible by Bajuri?
A) Lam of entitlement with al for gender
B) Lam of possession with al for specific reference when the specific is the eternal praise alone
C) Lam of attribution with al for universality
D) Lam of possession with al for gender

Key Takeaway
The grammar of 'al-hamd' reveals that the eternal cannot be possessed, affecting the interpretation of the phrase.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'al-hamdulillah', do you ever feel a sense of ownership over your praise—as if it is your possession to offer or withhold? How does the lesson's assertion that the eternal cannot be possessed challenge that feeling? Will you recite 'al-hamdulillah' with a different awareness of who truly owns all praise?
?
The lesson separates praise into eternal and temporal categories. In your daily life, when do you find yourself praising Allah with a sense of entitlement or expectation of return? Confess to yourself: is there any praise you've offered that you considered 'yours' rather than a gift? Commit to offering praise purely for Allah, without trying to possess it.
?
If the eternal cannot be possessed, what does that mean for your attempts to 'hold on to' blessings, knowledge, or even your own good deeds? Are you trying to possess what is ultimately eternal? This lesson calls you to let go of ownership in your relationship with Allah. Will you surrender the illusion that anything you have is truly yours?

The Scholarly Disagreement on the Meaning of Lam in 'Al-Hamd'
Bajuri's glosses debate the meaning of lam in 'al-hamd'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement over whether the lam in 'al-hamd' can mean possession, entitlement, or attribution, and the implications of al-Sabban's distinction.

Core Idea
The precise meaning of lam in 'al-hamd' is a matter of scholarly debate with theological nuances.

After Bajuri's enumeration, later commentators engaged in a debate about the specific meaning of lam in the phrase. Ibn Qasim maintained that lam can take all three meanings, while al-Sabban argued a strict distinction between possession, entitlement, and attribution, concluding that only entitlement is possible here. Al-Anbabi adds further nuance by pointing out special usages that might allow other meanings. This lesson explores that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss from al-Ajhuri
Al-Ajhuri cites Ibn Qasim's view that lam can have all three meanings, then contrasts it with al-Sabban's distinction.
Notice the three-way distinction: lam of possession between two entities where the second is owned, lam of attribution where the second is not owned, and lam of entitlement between a meaning and an entity.
2
Understand al-Sabban's conclusion
According to al-Sabban's distinction, lam here can only be for entitlement, because praise is not an entity that can be owned or attributed to another entity in that sense.
3
Read al-Anbabi's note
Al-Anbabi adds that making lam for attribution is only apparent if we consider a special usage mentioned earlier, and making it for possession is also not apparent unless there is another method.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this grammatical debate matters for theology: it determines whether praise is owned by Allah (which would be impossible if the praise is eternal) or is an entitlement due to Him.
This shows the interdisciplinary nature of kalam.

?

?

?

Write a brief resolve to say 'Alhamdulillah' at least ten times today with this corrected understanding.

The Rational Judgment: Definition and Components
Al-Bajuri defines rational judgment and explains its three component parts of a proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of rational judgment and identify its three essential parts in a proposition.

Core Idea
Every rational judgment has three parts: subject, predicate, and their connection.

Al-Bajuri begins by defining rational judgment as the affirmation or negation of a thing for another, without relying on repetition or convention. This definition is the foundation of all theological reasoning. The accompanying hashya clarifies that every judgment consists of three parts: the subject, the predicate, and the relation (affirmation or negation). Understanding this structure is essential for analyzing statements about God and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of rational judgment
Read the Arabic definition carefully. Note that the judgment is purely rational, not based on experience or custom. Understand that it is either affirmation or negation.
The definition excludes judgments based on repeated experience or social convention.
2
Understand affirmation and negation
The hashya explains that 'affirmation' means believing in existence, and 'negation' means believing in non-existence. This is the core of the judgment.
The judgment is a belief, not a linguistic utterance.
3
Identify the three parts of a proposition
From the hashya, identify that a proposition has three parts: the subject (maḥkūm ʿalayhi), the predicate (maḥkūm bihi), and the third part is the connection (thubūt or intifāʾ).
The third part is implied in the act of affirmation or negation itself.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this logical structure is important for kalam. It ensures that theological arguments have clear subjects and predicates, avoiding confusion.
This foundational concept appears throughout the text of Umm al-Barahin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: الحكم العقلي: وهو إثباتُ أَمْرِ لأمر أو نَفْيُهُ عنه من غير تَوَقُّف على تَكْرَارٍ ولا وَضْع واضع ، وينحصر في ثلاثة أقسام كما سيذكره المصنف. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَهُوَ إِثْبَاتُ... إلخ) المراد بالإثبات اعتقاد الثبوت، والمراد بالنفي اعتقاد الانتفاء، والأمر الأول المحكوم به والثاني المحكوم عليه، وهذان جزان من أجزاء القضية، والثالث الثبوت الذي في ضمن الإثبات والانتفاء الذي في ضمن النفي، فالمأخوذ من هذا التعريف أن أجزاء القضية ثلاثة.
The first: the rational judgment: it is the affirmation of a thing for a thing or its negation from it without depending on repetition nor convention, and it is limited to three divisions as the author will mention. Commentary of al-ʿAllāma Aḥmad al-Ajhūrī: (His words: 'and it is the affirmation... etc.') By affirmation is meant belief in existence, and by negation is meant belief in non-existence. The first thing is the predicate, the second is the subject, and these are two parts of the proposition, and the third is the existence that is implicit in affirmation and the non-existence that is implicit in negation. Thus from this definition it is taken that the parts of the proposition are three.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī). It is defined as affirming a thing of another thing or negating it from another, without relying on repeated experience (takarrūr) or conventional stipulation (waḍʿ wāḍiʿ). The hashya clarifies that 'affirmation' means believing in the existence of the attribute for the subject, and 'negation' means believing in its non-existence. Every judgment consists of three parts: the subject (maḥkūm ʿalayhi), the predicate (maḥkūm bihi), and the connection (thubūt or intifāʾ) which is either affirmation or negation. This is the basic structure of a proposition in logic.

Key Terms
الحكم العقلي (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī)
إثبات (ithbāt)
نفي (nafy)
قضية (qaḍiyya)
محكوم به (maḥkūm bihi)
محكوم عليه (maḥkūm ʿalayhi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That rational judgment is based on personal opinion or repeated experience.
Correction: Rational judgment is based on pure reason, not on empirical repetition or social convention, as explicitly stated in the definition.

Checkpoint
According to the hashya, how many parts does a proposition (qaḍiyya) have?
A) Two.
B) Three.
C) Four.
D) Five.

Key Takeaway
A rational judgment logically consists of three parts: subject, predicate, and the relation of affirmation or negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent conclusion you made about Allah or an Islamic belief. Can you break it down into its subject, predicate, and the relation (affirmation/negation)? Write it down. Does this exercise unsettle you? If so, why?
?
Al-Bajuri says rational judgment does not depend on repetition or convention. When have you held a belief simply because it was repeated or socially accepted, without examining it rationally? Identify one such belief and commit to re-evaluating it using sound reason.
?
If every rational judgment has three parts, what does this imply about the care you must take when making claims about Allah? Are your judgments precise? Or do you sometimes conflate subject and predicate? Repent and resolve to be more rigorous in your thinking.

Customary Judgment: Definition and Four Types
Al-Bajuri defines customary judgment and enumerates its four types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of customary judgment in Islamic theology, its basis in repeated observation, and its four distinct logical forms.

Core Idea
Customary judgments are patterns we learn from repetition, not from logic or revelation.

Al-Bajuri now turns to the second category of judgment: the customary judgment (ḥukm ʿādī). Unlike rational judgments, which are immediately certain, customary judgments depend on repeated experience. Our actions, like eating leading to satiety or fire burning, are governed by such judgments. This lesson unpacks the definition, the condition of repetition, and the four logical subdivisions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of customary judgment
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage where Al-Bajuri defines customary judgment as affirming or negating something for something else by means of repetition.
Notice the key phrase 'bi-wāsiṭat al-takarrur' (by means of repetition).
2
Understand the condition of repetition
Read the note from al-Anbābī: a single experience does not create a customary judgment; repetition (twice or more) is required.
This distinguishes customary judgment from rational judgment, which can be known from a single occurrence.
3
Examine the four subdivisions
The passage lists four linking relations: existence-existence, non-existence-non-existence, existence-non-existence, and non-existence-existence. Look at each example.
The first and third are affirmations; the second and fourth are negations.
4
Identify affirmation and negation
Al-Ajhūrī explains that the first and third types are affirmations (ithbāt) and the second and fourth are negations (nafy). Verify this with the examples.
This classification helps in analyzing causal statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: الحكم العادي: وهو إثبات أمر لأمر أو نفيه عنه بواسطة التكرار، ويَنْحَصِرُ في أربعة أقسام: رَبْطُ وجودِ بوجودٍ كرَبط وجود الشبع بوجود الأكل، ورَبَّطُ عَدَمٍ بعدم كربط عدم الشَّبَع بعدم الأكل، وربط وجود بعدم كربط وجود البزد بعدم السَّتْر، وربط عَدَم بوجود كربط عدم الإحراق بوجود الماء. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: رَبْطُ وُجُودِ بِوُجُودِ) أي: إثبات أمرٍ وجودي لأمر وجودي كإثبات الشبع للأكل في قولنا: الأكل مشبع. وقوله: رَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِعَدَمٍ) أي: نفي أمر وجودي عن أمر عدمي في قولنا: ليس عدم الأكل مشبعاً، فقد نفينا أمراً وجودياً هو الشبع عن أمر عدمي هو عدم الأكل. (وقوله: وَرَبْطُ وُجُودٍ بِعَدَمٍ أي: إثبات أمر وجودي لأمر عدمي، كما في قولنا: عدم الستر محصل للبرد. وقوله: وَرَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِوُجُودِ) أي: نفي أمر وجودي عن أمر وجودي كما في قولنا النار مع وجود الماء على الحطب مثلاً ليست محرقة، فقد نفينا أمراً وجودياً هو الإحراق عن أمر وجودي هو النار عند وجود الماء، وبهذا ظهر أن الأول والثالث من باب الإثبات والثاني والرابع من باب النفي. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: بِوَاسِطَةِ التَّكْرَارِ) فإذا حَكَمَ الشخص بأن شُرْبَ القهوة أو أَكل الضَّأْن يُزَكِّي الفهم بواسطة استعماله لذلك أوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ؛ لم يكن حُكْماً عادياً، بل عقلياً، وإذا حَكَمَ بذلك بواسطة استعماله مرتين فأكثر؛ كان حكماً عادياً.
The second: the customary judgment: it is the affirmation of a thing for a thing or its negation from it by means of repetition, and it is limited to four divisions: (1) the linking of existence with existence, like linking the existence of satiety with the existence of eating; (2) the linking of non-existence with non-existence, like linking the non-existence of satiety with the non-existence of eating; (3) the linking of existence with non-existence, like linking the existence of cold with the non-existence of covering; (4) the linking of non-existence with existence, like linking the non-existence of burning with the existence of water. Commentary of al-ʿAllāma Aḥmad al-Ajhūrī: (His words: 'the linking of existence with existence') meaning: affirming an existential thing for an existential thing, like affirming satiety for eating in our saying: 'eating satiates.' (His words: 'the linking of non-existence with non-existence') meaning: negating an existential thing from a non-existential thing in our saying: 'non-existence of eating does not satiate,' for we have negated an existential thing, which is satiety, from a non-existential thing, which is non-existence of eating. (His words: 'and the linking of existence with non-existence') meaning: affirming an existential thing for a non-existential thing, as in our saying: 'non-existence of covering causes cold.' (His words: 'and the linking of non-existence with existence') meaning: negating an existential thing from an existential thing as in our saying: 'fire, with the existence of water on the wood, for example, does not burn,' for we have negated an existential thing, which is burning, from an existential thing, which is fire, in the presence of water. Thus it becomes clear that the first and third are from the category of affirmation, and the second and fourth are from the category of negation. The notes of al-ʿAllāma Muḥammad al-Anbābī: (His words: 'by means of repetition') So if a person judges that drinking coffee or eating mutton sharpens the mind by using it once, it is not a customary judgment but a rational one; and if he judges that by using it twice or more, it is a customary judgment.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of judgment: customary judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿādī). It is based on repeated observation (takarrūr). It has four subdivisions, each linking either existence or non-existence in various combinations. The hashya explains each: (1) existence to existence (e.g., eating causes satiety), (2) non-existence to non-existence (e.g., not eating does not cause satiety), (3) existence to non-existence (e.g., lack of covering causes cold), (4) non-existence to existence (e.g., with water present, fire does not burn). The first and third are affirmations, the second and fourth are negations. The note from al-Anbābī clarifies that a judgment becomes 'customary' only if it is based on at least two repetitions; a single experience is still a rational judgment.

Key Terms
الحكم العادي (al-ḥukm al-ʿādī)
التكرار (al-takarrur)
ربط (rabt)
وجود (wujūd)
عدم (ʿadam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any observed link, even from a single experience, constitutes a customary judgment.
Correction: Al-Anbābī clarifies that a judgment based on a single occurrence is rational, not customary. Customary judgment requires at least two repetitions.

Checkpoint
How many times must an experience be repeated for it to become a customary judgment?
A) Once.
B) Twice or more.
C) Three times.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
Customary judgments are habits of nature known by repeated observation, and they come in four logical forms: existence/existence, non-existence/non-existence, existence/non-existence, and non-existence/existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many times have you assumed that something will happen just because it always has—like the sun rising or a friend's reaction? This lesson says such judgments are not necessary, only habitual. What is one area of your life where you have mistaken a customary pattern for an absolute truth, and how might that assumption be affecting your reliance on God?
?
The passage distinguishes between a single observation (rational) and repeated observation (customary). Think of a time you judged someone's character based on a single interaction. Did you treat it as certain? How can you better align your certainty with what the lesson teaches about probability and repetition?
?
This lesson shows that even natural patterns like fire burning are not necessary—they are customary and can be broken by God's will. Where in your life do you feel most 'in control' of outcomes? Are you willing to admit that that control is an illusion, and that your trust should ultimately be in the One who sustains the custom?

Rational Judgments

The Rational Judgment and Its Three Parts
Sanusi defines rational judgment; al-Ajhuri explains its three constituent parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of rational judgment and be able to identify its three constituent parts within any proposition.

Core Idea
Rational judgment is the proposition that links something to something else by affirmation or negation.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī) as defined by Sanusi. It is the core building block of all theological and logical reasoning. Al-Ajhuri's commentary unpacks the definition to reveal the three necessary parts of every proposition, setting the stage for the study of kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of rational judgment: إثباتُ أَمْرِ لأمر أو نَفْيُهُ عنه. Note that it excludes judgments based on repetition or convention.
This definition separates rational judgment from customary or legal judgments.
2
Identify the three parts
From the commentary, list the three parts: the subject (المحكوم عليه), the predicate (المحكوم به), and the relation of existence or non-existence (الثبوت أو الانتفاء).
The third part is implicit in the act of affirmation or negation.
3
Apply the analysis
Take a simple statement like 'The world is created' and identify all three parts: subject (the world), predicate (created), and the relation (existence of creation).
This exercise solidifies the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: الحكم العقلي: وهو إثباتُ أَمْرِ لأمر أو نَفْيُهُ عنه من غير تَوَقُّف على تَكْرَارٍ ولا وَضْع واضع ، وينحصر في ثلاثة أقسام كما سيذكره المصنف.
(قوله: وَهُوَ إِثْبَاتُ... إلخ) المراد بالإثبات اعتقاد الثبوت، والمراد بالنفي اعتقاد الانتفاء، والأمر الأول المحكوم به والثاني المحكوم عليه، وهذان جزان من أجزاء القضية، والثالث الثبوت الذي في ضمن الإثبات والانتفاء الذي في ضمن النفي، فالمأخوذ من هذا التعريف أن أجزاء القضية ثلاثة.
The first: the rational judgment: it is the affirmation of a matter for a matter or the negation of it from it without dependence on repetition nor on the imposition of an imposer, and it is confined to three divisions as the author will mention. (His saying: 'And it is affirmation... etc.') What is meant by affirmation is the belief of existence, and what is meant by negation is the belief of non-existence. The first matter is the subject of judgment and the second is the object of judgment, and these two are two parts of the proposition, and the third is the existence which is within affirmation and the non-existence which is within negation. So taken from this definition is that the parts of the proposition are three.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī) and then explains the components of its definition. Sanusi first states that rational judgment is the affirmation or negation of one thing concerning another, without reliance on repeated experience or on a legislator's convention. This distinguishes it from customary and legal judgments. The commentary by al-Ajhuri expands on the terms: 'affirmation' means believing something to exist, 'negation' means believing something not to exist. The first matter is what is judged (the subject), the second is that about which judgment is made (the predicate). These two are two parts of the proposition. The third part is the existence or non-existence that is affirmed or negated. Thus, every proposition implicitly has three parts: subject, predicate, and the judgment linking them. This analysis is foundational for logic and kalam.

Key Terms
الحكم العقلي
إثبات
نفي
القضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that rational judgment requires sense experience or authority.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that rational judgment is not dependent on repetition (experience) or the imposition of an imposer (authority).

Checkpoint
According to al-Ajhuri's commentary, how many parts does a proposition (al-qadiyya) have?
A) Two: subject and predicate
B) Three: subject, predicate, and the judgment of existence or non-existence
C) Four: subject, predicate, affirmation, negation
D) Two: affirmation and negation

Key Takeaway
Rational judgment is the affirmation or negation of a predicate about a subject, and every proposition contains three parts: subject, predicate, and the relational judgment of existence or absence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you consciously separate the subject (Allah), predicate (existence), and your judgment (affirmation)? How does this logical structure help you avoid confusing Allah's existence with created things?
?
Think of a belief you hold about yourself (e.g., 'I am weak'). Can you identify the three parts? Now, if you changed the judgment from affirmation to negation, how would that affect your self-perception and your reliance on Allah?
?
The rational judgment is independent of repetition and legislation. Is your faith built on rational certainty or on repetition (habit) or following others? What would it take for your core beliefs to be truly rational judgments?

Customary Judgment: Definition and Four Types
Sanusi defines customary judgment and enumerates its four types with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what a customary judgment is, its four logical forms, and why repetition is essential to distinguish it from rational judgment.

Core Idea
Customary judgment is built on repeated observation, not on logical necessity.

In this passage, Sanusi defines the second kind of judgment—customary judgment—and breaks it down into four precise logical patterns. The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies each pattern with everyday examples and stresses the crucial role of repetition. This lesson will help you see how Islamic theology classifies empirical causality as a matter of custom (ʿāda) rather than rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read Sanusi's definition of customary judgment carefully. Notice that it involves affirmation or negation 'by means of repetition' (bi-wāsiṭat al-takrār).
The phrase 'by means of repetition' is the key condition.
2
Identify the four types
The passage lists four combinations of existence (wujūd) and non-existence (ʿadam). Write them down and match each with its example from the commentary.
The first and third are affirmations; the second and fourth are negations.
3
Understand the repetition condition
Read the gloss on 'by means of repetition' and note the example about coffee. Ask: why does a single occurrence not produce a customary judgment?
This distinguishes rational from customary judgment.
4
Test your understanding
Try to create your own example for each of the four types. Then check the checkpoint question to see if you grasp the repetition condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: الحكم العادي: وهو إثبات أمر لأمر أو نفيه عنه بواسطة التكرار، ويَنْحَصِرُ في أربعة أقسام: رَبْطُ وجودِ بوجودٍ كربط وجود الشبع بوجود الأكل، ورَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بعدم كربط عدم الشَّبَع بعدم الأكل، وربط وجود بعدم كربط وجود البرد بعدم السَّتْر، وربط عَدَم بوجود كربط عدم الإحراق بوجود الماء.
(قوله: رَبْطُ وُجُودِ بِوُجُودِ) أي: إثبات أمرٍ وجودي لأمر وجودي كإثبات الشبع للأكل في قولنا: الأكل مشبع. وقوله: رَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِعَدَمٍ) أي: نفي أمر وجودي عن أمر عدمي كقولنا: ليس عدم الأكل مشبعاً، فقد نفينا أمراً وجودياً هو الشبع عن أمر عدمي هو عدم الأكل. (وقوله: وَرَبْطُ وُجُودٍ بِعَدَمٍ أي: إثبات أمر وجودي لأمر عدمي، كما في قولنا: عدم الستر محصل للبرد. وقوله: وَرَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِوُجُودِ) أي: نفي أمر وجودي عن أمر وجودي كما في قولنا: النار مع وجود الماء على الحطب مثلاً ليست محرقة، فقد نفينا أمراً وجودياً هو الإحراق عن أمر وجودي هو النار عند وجود الماء، وبهذا ظهر أن الأول والثالث من باب الإثبات والثاني والرابع من باب النفي.
(قوله: بِوَاسِطَةِ التَّكْرَارِ) فإذا حَكَمَ الشخص بأن شُرْبَ القهوة أو أَكلَ الضَّأْن يُزَكِّي الفهم بواسطة استعماله لذلك أوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ؛ لم يكن حُكْماً عادياً، بل عقلياً، وإذا حَكَمَ بذلك بواسطة استعماله مرتين فأكثر؛ كان حكماً عادياً.
And the second: the customary judgment: it is the affirmation of a matter for a matter or the negation of it from it by means of repetition, and it is confined to four divisions: linking existence to existence like linking the existence of satiety to the existence of eating; and linking non-existence to non-existence like linking the non-existence of satiety to the non-existence of eating; and linking existence to non-existence like linking the existence of cold to the non-existence of covering; and linking non-existence to existence like linking the non-existence of burning to the existence of water. (His saying: 'linking existence to existence') means: affirming an existential matter to an existential matter like affirming satiety for eating in our saying: 'eating is satiating.' And his saying: 'linking non-existence to non-existence' means: negating an existential matter from a non-existential matter like our saying: 'not eating is not satiating,' so we have negated an existential matter (satiety) from a non-existential matter (not eating). (And his saying: 'and linking existence to non-existence' means: affirming an existential matter to a non-existential matter, as in our saying: 'absence of covering is a cause of cold.' And his saying: 'and linking non-existence to existence' means: negating an existential matter from an existential matter as in our saying: 'fire with the presence of water on wood, for example, is not burning,' so we have negated an existential matter (burning) from an existential matter (fire) in the presence of water. And by this it appears that the first and third are from the category of affirmation, and the second and fourth from the category of negation. (His saying: 'by means of repetition') So if a person judges that drinking coffee or eating sheep sharpens the intellect by using it the first time, it is not a customary judgment but a rational one; and if he judges that by using it twice or more, it is a customary judgment.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines customary judgment and then explains its divisions and condition. Sanusi defines customary judgment as affirmation or negation based on repeated observation, distinguishing it from rational judgment which does not require repetition. He then lists four types, each combining existence (wujūd) or non-existence (ʿadam) of two things. Al-Ajhuri's commentary clarifies each type with examples: (1) existence linked to existence (e.g., eating causes satiety), (2) non-existence linked to non-existence (e.g., not eating causes not satiety), (3) existence linked to non-existence (e.g., lack of covering causes cold), (4) non-existence linked to existence (e.g., presence of water prevents burning). The first and third are affirmations, the second and fourth are negations. Finally, the gloss on 'by means of repetition' gives an example: if someone drinks coffee once and judges it sharpens intellect, that is a rational judgment (based on innate knowledge?), but if repeated twice or more, it becomes customary—meaning it is based on empirical habit. This reinforces that custom (ʿāda) relies on recurrence.

Key Terms
الحكم العادي
التكرار
ربط وجود بوجود
ربط عدم بعدم
ربط وجود بعدم
ربط عدم بوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any habitual association is a rational necessity or that a single experience is enough to establish a customary judgment.
Correction: Sanusi clearly states that customary judgment requires repetition (takrār); a judgment from one experience remains in the realm of rational judgment, not custom.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key factor that distinguishes a customary judgment from a rational judgment?
A) The use of logical reasoning
B) The repetition of the observed connection
C) The strength of the causal link
D) The type of things being linked

Key Takeaway
Customary judgment is based on repeated observation and has four logical forms: existence to existence, non-existence to non-existence, existence to non-existence, and non-existence to existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if the regularities you observe—satiety from eating, cold from lack of covering—are absolute necessities, forgetting that they are merely customary by God's will? When was the last time this forgetfulness caused you to feel self-sufficient or neglectful of the Creator? What will you do today to remind yourself of Allah's ongoing power over cause and effect?
?
The example of fire not burning when water is present shows that even the most consistent natural laws are contingent. Is there a 'fire' in your life—a person, situation, or habit—that you assume will always behave in a certain way, making you complacent in your reliance on Allah? What would change if you truly believed that Allah can alter that outcome without any known cause?
?
Sanusi distinguishes rational judgment (based on one occurrence) from customary judgment (based on repetition). Think of a belief you hold strongly about the world that is based on repeated observation rather than logical necessity. Are you willing to admit that it could be otherwise if Allah wills? If not, what is holding you back from that surrender?

Examples of Obligation, Recommendation, Prohibition, and Dislike
Al-Bajuri gives examples for four taklif categories with their wad' elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the five wad' categories (cause, condition, impediment, soundness, corruption) apply to four of the five taklif categories: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and dislike.

Core Idea
Every taklif category has its own set of wad' elements that define its application.

Al-Bajuri provides concrete examples to illustrate how the framework of cause, condition, impediment, soundness, and corruption operates across different types of legal rulings. These examples show that the same wad' categories apply differently depending on whether the ruling is a command, a recommendation, a prohibition, or a disliked act. By studying these examples, you will see the consistency and nuance of the theoretical framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four examples
Read the passage and locate the examples for obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and dislike. Notice how each lists a cause, condition, impediment, and whether soundness/corruption are mentioned.
The first example starts with 'مثال جريانها في الإيجاب'.
2
Understand the pattern
For each taklif category, there is a cause that triggers it, a condition that must be met, an impediment that prevents it, and then soundness/corruption are stated where applicable.
3
Contrast prohibition and dislike with obligation and recommendation
Notice that for prohibition and dislike, soundness and corruption do not apply because these are not actions that are performed; rather, they are rulings that prevent or discourage. For obligation and recommendation, soundness and corruption apply because these acts can be valid or invalid.
4
Relate to the overall framework
This lesson reinforces that the wad' categories are universal but their application varies with the type of ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَتَوْضِيحُ ذَلِكَ يُطْلَبُ مِنَ الْمُطَوَّلاتِ) مثال جريانها في الإيجاب: جعل الزوال سبباً لوجوب الظهر، والبلوغ والعقل شرطاً له، والحيض مانعاً منه، وجعلها صحيحة إن اجتمعت الشروط والأركان، وفاسدة إن لم تجتمع. ومثال جريانها في الندب جعل دخول وقت العشاء سبباً لندب الوتر، وجعل البلوغ والعقل شرطاً له، وجعل الحيض مانعاً منه، وجعل صلاة الوتر صحيحة إن استوفت ما يعتبر فيها، وفاسدة إن لم تستوفها. ومثالها في التحريم: جعل خبث الميتة سبباً لتحريمها، وجعل شرطه عدم الاضطرار، وجعل مانعه الاضطرار والصحة والفساد لا يجريان في تحريمها كما لا يخفى. ومثالها في الكراهة: كراهة اصطياد صيد البر، فإن سببها اللهو، وشرطها عدم الاحتياج ومانعها الاحتياج، والصحة والفساد لا يجريان فيها كما لا يخفى. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي - وتحريمُ البيع بعد أذان الجمعة، وسَبَبُهُ: الاشتغال عن ذِكْرِ الله، وشرطه: التكليف ومانعه: اضطرار المشتري أو عُذْرُ البائع والمشتري بعُذْرٍ من أعذار الجمعة، وصحة البيع باستكماله الشروط، وفساده بانتفائه. وكراهة البيع لمن ينجز في أكفان الموتى، سببها: تمني كثرة الموت، وشرطها: التكليف، ومانعها: الاضطرار، والصحة باستكماله الشروط، والفساد بانتفائه. وإباحة البيع، سببها: الاحتياج العام، وشرطها: التكليف، ومانعها: كونه وقت أذان الجمعة مثلاً، والصحة والفساد كما تقدم.
(His saying: 'And clarification of that is sought from the detailed works.') Example of their application in obligation: making the zenith a cause for the obligation of Zuhr, and puberty and reason a condition for it, and menstruation an impediment from it, and making it sound if the conditions and pillars are met, and corrupt if not met. And an example of their application in recommendation: making the entry of Isha time a cause for the recommendation of Witr, and making puberty and reason a condition for it, and making menstruation an impediment from it, and making the Witr prayer sound if it fulfills what is considered in it, and corrupt if it does not fulfill it. And an example of them in prohibition: making the impurity of carrion a cause for its prohibition, and making its condition the absence of necessity, and making its impediment necessity, and soundness and corruption do not apply in its prohibition as is not hidden. And an example of them in dislike: the dislike of hunting land game, for its cause is frivolity, its condition is lack of need, its impediment is need, and soundness and corruption do not apply in it as is not hidden. The glosses of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi - And the prohibition of selling after the Friday adhan, its cause: being occupied from remembrance of Allah, its condition: obligation (taklif), its impediment: the buyer's necessity or the seller's excuse or the buyer with an excuse from the excuses of Friday, and the soundness of the sale by completing its conditions, its corruption by their absence. And the dislike of selling to one who hastens in shrouds of the dead, its cause: wishing for much death, its condition: obligation, its impediment: necessity, and soundness by completing its conditions, corruption by their absence. And the permission of selling, its cause: general need, its condition: obligation, its impediment: it being at the time of the Friday adhan for example, and soundness and corruption as previously.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples of how the five wad' categories (cause, condition, impediment, soundness, corruption) apply to each of the five taklif categories, thus illustrating the twenty-five combinations. For obligation (ijab), the example is the obligation of Zuhr prayer: its cause is the sun's zenith, its condition is puberty and sanity, its impediment is menstruation, and its soundness depends on fulfilling conditions and pillars, otherwise it is corrupt. For recommendation (nadb), the example is Witr prayer: its cause is the entry of Isha time, its condition is puberty and sanity, its impediment is menstruation, and its soundness depends on fulfilling its conditions. For prohibition (tahrim), the example is carrion: its cause is its impurity, its condition is the absence of necessity (i.e., not being forced to eat it), its impediment is necessity, and soundness/corruption do not apply because prohibition is not a category that can be valid or invalid. For dislike (karahah), the example is hunting land game for sport: its cause is frivolity, its condition is lack of need, its impediment is need, and again soundness/corruption do not apply. Then additional examples from al-Anbabi focus on sales: prohibited sale after Friday adhan with its cause, condition, impediment, soundness, and corruption; disliked sale of shrouds to one who hastens death; and permitted sale with its cause (general need) and condition (being legally responsible) and impediment (being at the time of the adhan). These examples demonstrate the comprehensive framework.

Key Terms

شرط
مانع
صحة
فساد
إيجاب
ندب
تحريم
كراهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Soundness and corruption apply to all acts, including pure prohibitions and dislikes.
Correction: Soundness and corruption only apply to acts that are performed (like obligations and recommendations) or to transactions. For pure prohibitions and dislikes that do not involve human performance, they do not apply.

Checkpoint
In the example for obligation, what is the cause for the obligation of Zuhr?
A) Puberty
B) Zenith
C) Menstruation
D) Fulfilling conditions

Key Takeaway
The five wad' categories apply to each taklif category with specific details, and soundness/corruption are only relevant for categories that involve human performance of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the prohibition of carrion is lifted by necessity (necessity). Think of a time when you faced a difficult choice between a forbidden act and a pressing need. Did you genuinely assess the necessity, or did you use it as an excuse? What would change if you examined your intentions more carefully?
?
When you perform Zuhr, do you ever pause to consider the conditions (puberty, reason) and impediments (menstruation) that Allah has placed? How does reflecting on these details deepen your awareness of His mercy and the precision of His law?
?
The examples show that soundness and corruption do not apply to prohibitions and dislikes. Yet, we often judge ourselves or others for such actions as if they are either 'done right' or 'done wrong.' How might this misunderstanding affect your relationship with Allah? Will you now accept that certain rulings simply require avoidance, not performance scrutiny?

Examples from Sales: Prohibition, Dislike, and Permission
Al-Anbabi illustrates prohibition, dislike, and permission with sales examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the wad' categories apply to transactional rulings such as selling after Friday adhan (prohibited), selling shrouds to those eager for death (disliked), and general permissible selling.

Core Idea
Transactional rulings also follow the same wad' framework, with cause, condition, impediment, soundness, and corruption.

Al-Anbabi extends the examples to the realm of sales, showing that the framework is not limited to worship. He gives three examples: a prohibited sale (after Friday adhan), a disliked sale (of shrouds to one who hastens death), and a permissible sale (general need). Each illustrates the application of cause, condition, impediment, soundness, and corruption where relevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the three examples
Identify the ruling for each: prohibition of selling after Friday adhan, dislike of selling shrouds to one who hastens death, and permission of general selling.
The examples are introduced with 'تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي'.
2
Note cause, condition, impediment for each
For each example, find the cause, condition, and impediment. Also notice that soundness and corruption are mentioned for all three.
3
Compare with previous lesson
In the previous examples for prohibition and dislike of pure acts (eating carrion, hunting), soundness/corruption did not apply. Here they do because the sale is a contractual act that can be valid or invalid regardless of the ruling.
4
Understand the nuances
For prohibited sale, the impediment includes necessity or excuse. For disliked sale, the impediment is necessity. For permissible sale, the impediment is being at a prohibited time.

The Five Components in Karaha and Ibaha Examples
Four examples showing cause, condition, preventer, validity, invalidity for karaha, tahrim, and ibaha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the five components (cause, condition, preventer, validity, invalidity) apply to the rulings of karaha (disliked) and ibaha (permissible), as illustrated through concrete examples.

Core Idea
The five components govern all Shariah rulings, not just obligation and prohibition.

Having learned the abstract definitions of cause, condition, preventer, validity, and invalidity, we now see them in action through specific examples from the categories of karaha (disliked) and ibaha (permissible). The author, along with a quote from al-Anbabi, provides four distinct cases showing how these components operate across different types of rulings, including a case of tahrim (prohibition) for contrast. This lesson will help you internalise the pattern by examining real-world applications.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, paying attention to how each example is structured: ruling, then its cause, condition, preventer, and finally validity/invalidity.
Note that the first example (hunting) explicitly states that validity/invalidity do not apply.
2
Identify the pattern in each example
For each of the four examples, list the cause, condition, preventer, and whether validity/invalidity is mentioned. Look for similarities and differences.
The cause relates to the purpose of the act, the condition to the state of the agent, and the preventer to an overriding factor.
3
Compare the rulings
Observe how the components vary depending on whether the ruling is karaha, tahrim, or ibaha. Ask yourself why validity/invalidity applies to some acts (like sales) but not to others (like hunting).
Validity and invalidity apply to acts that have specific conditions (like contracts), not to all acts.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
Understand that the five-component framework is universal across the five Shariah values. The examples show that even disliked and permissible acts have causes, conditions, and preventers.
This corrects the common misconception that only obligations and prohibitions have such components.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثالها في الكراهة: كراهة اصطياد صيد البر، فإن سببها اللهو، وشرطها عدم الاحتياج ومانعها الاحتياج، والصحة والفساد لا يجريان فيها كما لا يخفى. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي - وتحريمُ البيع بعد أذان الجمعة، وسَبَبُهُ: الاشتغال عن ذِكْرِ الله، وشرطة: التكليف ومانعه: اضطرار المشتري أو عُذْرُ البائع والمشتري بعُذْرٍ من أعذار الجمعة، وصحة البيع باستكماله الشروط، وفساده بانتفائه. وكراهة البيع لمن ينجز في أكفان الموتى، سببها: تمني كثرة الموت، وشرطها: التكليف، ومانعها الاضطرارُ ، والصحة باستكماله الشروط، والفساد بانتفائه. وإباحة البيع، سببها: الاحتياج العالم، وشرطها التكليف، ومائعُها: كَوْنُهُ وقتَ أذان الجمعة مثلاً، والصحة والفساد كما تقدم.
And its example in karaha (disliked): the dislikedness of hunting land game, for its cause is frivolity, its condition is lack of need, its preventer is need, and validity and invalidity do not apply to it as is not hidden. Taqrirat al-Allama Muhammad al-Anbabi - And the prohibition of selling after the Friday call to prayer, its cause: being occupied from the remembrance of Allah, its condition: legal capacity, its preventer: the necessity of the buyer or the excuse of the seller and the buyer with an excuse from the excuses of Friday, and the validity of the sale by its completion of conditions, and its invalidity by its absence. And the dislikedness of selling to one who hurries in [buying] shrouds for the dead, its cause: wishing for many deaths, its condition: legal capacity, its preventer: necessity, and validity by its completion of conditions, and invalidity by its absence. And the permissibility of selling, its cause: universal need, its condition: legal capacity, its preventer: its being at the time of the Friday call to prayer, for example, and validity and invalidity as previously.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage provides examples for the rulings of karaha (disliked) and ibaha (permissible) to illustrate how the five components (cause, condition, preventer, validity, invalidity) apply to them. The author first gives an example of karaha: hunting land game. Its cause is frivolity, condition is lack of need, preventer is need, and validity/invalidity do not apply. Then, from the Taqrirat of al-Anbabi, additional examples are given: the prohibition of selling after the Friday call to prayer (which is a case of tahrim, not karaha, but included for comparison) with its cause, condition, preventer, and validity/invalidity. Then karaha of selling shrouds to one who hurries, with similar components. Finally, ibaha of selling, with cause being universal need, condition legal capacity, preventer being the time of Friday prayer, and validity/invalidity as before. The pattern shows that cause, condition, and preventer are related to the ruling itself (the five values), while validity and invalidity are related to the act that is the subject of the ruling, applying to all rulings except where the act cannot be described as valid or invalid (e.g., hunting for leisure is not an act that has validity/invalidity in the same way as a contract). The commentary emphasizes that validity and invalidity do not apply in certain rulings like karaha and ibaha in these examples, but they do apply in others like contract-based rulings.

Misconception: Only obligatory and prohibited acts have causes, conditions, and preventers; disliked and permissible acts are simply neutral.
Correction: This passage demonstrates that even disliked and permissible acts have specific causes, conditions, and preventers that determine their ruling. For example, the dislikedness of hunting depends on frivolity as a cause and need as a condition/preventer.

The Third Type of Restriction: Non-Exiting
Al-Ajhuri defines the third hasr as عدم الخروج with examples and a gloss.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third type of hasr (restriction) as meaning 'non-exiting' and how it differs from the first two types in the kalam framework.

Core Idea
The third hasr simply confines something to a set without predication or analysis.

This passage from al-Ajhuri's hashia clarifies the third type of restriction used by Sanusi. Before we can properly categorize rational judgments, we need a precise understanding of how restrictions work. The third type is the simplest: it just means that something does not go beyond a certain limit.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice how al-Ajhuri first gives a simple definition then an elaborate gloss.
Pay attention to the examples of the ruler and the thinker.
2
Identify the meaning of the third hasr
Find where the text defines the third hasr as 'adam al-khuruj' (non-exiting). Reflect on the examples: the ruler's authority confined to the city, thoughts confined to sins.
3
Understand how it differs from the first two
In the gloss, al-Ajhuri explains that all three types share the meaning of non-exiting, but the first type involves predication of the whole upon parts, the second involves analysis of the whole into parts, and the third is neither.
This distinction is crucial for applying hasr in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالثُ حَصْرٌ بمعنى عدم الخروج، كما في قول الشخص : انْحَصَرَ حُكْمُ الأمير في البلد وانحصرت فكرتي في ذنوبي، بمعنى أن حُكْمَ الأمير لا يَخْرُجُ عن البلد، وأن فِكْرَته لا تخرجُ عن ذنوبه.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: بِمَعْنَى : عَدَمِ الْخُرُوج الثلاثة بهذا المعنى، لكن الأول على وجه صحة الإخبار بالمقسم عن كل قسم والثاني على وجه صحة تفكيك المقسم إلى الأقسام أي: تفريقها وفصل بعضها عن بعض، والثالث على غير هذين الوجهين
:
:(قوله لا يَخْرُجُ المراد بعدم خروجه عنها: أن متعلقه - وهو المحكوم به ؛ إما واحد منها، كقولنا: قدرة الله واجبة وشريكه مستحيل، وبعثة الرسل جائزة، والمحكوم به في الأول الوجوب، وفي الثاني الاستحالة، وفي الثالث الجواز، أو موصوف بواحد منها، كقولنا : الله قادر والله ليس بعاجز والله خالق والمحكوم به في الأول القدرة، وصفتها الوجوب، والمحكوم به في الثاني على وجه النفي العجز وصفته الاستحالة، وفي الثالث الخلق، وصفته الجواز.
And the third is hasr (restriction) meaning عدم الخروج (non-exiting), as in a person's saying: 'The ruler's authority is confined to the city' and 'my thought is confined to my sins,' meaning that the ruler's authority does not extend beyond the city, and that his thought does not extend beyond his sins. The gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: meaning عدم الخروج), the three [divisions] are with this meaning, but the first is in the way of the validity of predicating the divided term (al-muqsam) of each of its subdivisions, and the second is in the way of the validity of analyzing the divided term into its subdivisions, i.e., separating them and distinguishing some from others, and the third is in neither of these two ways. (His saying: لا يَخْرُجُ), the intended meaning of its not exiting from them is that its relation (al-muta'allaq) – which is the حكم به (predicated judgment) – is either one of [the three] themselves, as when we say: 'God's power is wajib (necessary), His partner is mustahil (impossible), and the sending of messengers is ja'iz (possible);' the predicated [judgment] in the first is wujub, in the second istihala, and in the third jawaz; or [the relation] is a thing described by one of them, as when we say: 'God is qadir (powerful), God is not 'ajiz (powerless), God is khaliq (creator);' the predicated [judgment] in the first is qudra (power) and its attribute is wujub, in the second [the predicated judgment] is the negation of 'ajz (inability) and its attribute is istihala, and in the third khala (creation) and its attribute is jawaz.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage from the hashia defines the third type of حصر (restriction) that Sanusi uses. The commentator clarifies that this third type means عدم الخروج, i.e., that something does not go outside a certain set. He gives two examples: the ruler's authority does not go outside the city, and a person's thought does not go outside his sins. Then the commentator al-Ajhuri explains that the three types of restriction all share this meaning of non-exit, but they differ in how the restriction relates to the divided term and its parts. In the first type, the divided term can be truly predicated of each part – for example, 'animal' can be predicated of 'human', 'horse', etc. In the second type, the whole (like 'house') can be analyzed into its parts (roof, walls, etc.). The third type is neither of these; it is simply the statement that something is confined to a set without implying that the whole is predicable of each part or analyzable into them. The commentator then elaborates on what is meant by 'non-exit' in Sanusi's context: the relation (متعلق) of the judgment is either one of the three categories (وجوب, استحالة, جواز) directly, as in examples like 'God's power is wajib' – here the judgment is that the attribute of power has the quality of necessity. Or the relation is a thing described by one of the three, as in 'God is qadir' – here the predicated thing is qudra, and its attribute is wujub. This illustrates how the three categories (wujub, istihala, jawaz) apply either to the judgments themselves or to entities described by them.

Key Terms
حصْر
عدم الخروج
قسم
مقسم
متعلق
محمول
وجوب
استحالة
جواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All three types of restriction are identical in meaning and function.
Correction: The text teaches that while all three involve 'non-exiting', the first type has the added feature of predication of the whole on each part, the second has analysis of the whole into parts, and the third has neither.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what does the third type of 'hasr' mean?
A) That the divided term can be predicated of each part.
B) That the whole can be analyzed into parts.
C) That something does not go outside a set.
D) That the parts are equal.

Key Takeaway
The third type of ḥaṣr simply means that something is confined to a set, without implying that the set is a predicate or analyzable into parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your own thoughts: Are they confined (hasr) to your sins, as the example says? When you reflect on your past mistakes, do you ever feel trapped in that confinement? How does knowing that this is a 'non-exiting' type of hasr—meaning your thoughts do not extend beyond your sins—make you feel? What can you do to break that confinement and let your thoughts extend to God's mercy?
?
The lesson shows that God's power is wajib (necessary), not just possible. When you consider God's attributes, do you ever treat them as if they were merely possible or contingent? How does understanding that God's power is 'confined' to wujub (necessity) change your reliance on Him in your daily life? Will you actively remind yourself of this when facing uncertainty?
?
The third hasr is neither predication nor analysis—it is simply non-exiting. In your relationship with God, are there areas where you try to 'predict' God's actions or 'analyze' His decrees as if they fit into human logic? How can you submit to God's will without trying to confine Him to your understanding?

Rational Judgments
