The Four Categories of Existents: Locus and Specifier
Al-Bajuri classifies existents into four categories based on need for locus and specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians classify existents into four types based on their need for a locus and a specifier, and why this matters for understanding Allah's self-subsistence.

Core Idea
Allah alone needs neither a locus nor a specifier; everything else depends on one or both.

Al-Bajuri unpacks a key classification from al-Sanusi: every existent can be placed into one of four categories depending on whether it requires a place (mahall) to inhere in and a cause (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to bring it into existence. This framework helps distinguish the Creator from all created things. First, however, he clarifies two different understandings of the term 'qā'im bi nafsih' (self-subsisting).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Notice the flow from the two interpretations to the fourfold classification.
The first part about 'qā'im bi nafsih' is a clarification before the main classification.
2
Understand the two interpretations
Identify the two theological definitions of 'self-subsisting': the famous one (not needing a locus or specifier) and another (not needing a locus only, because not needing a specifier follows from pre-eternity).
This shows how precise theological terminology can be.
3
List the four categories
Write down the four divisions: (1) needs neither locus nor specifier (Allah's essence), (2) needs both (accidents of originated things), (3) needs specifier only (substance of originated things), (4) needs locus only (attributes of Allah).
Each category has a clear example given.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Ask yourself: What does this classification tell us about Allah's uniqueness? How does it help avoid anthropomorphism or denial of attributes?
This connects to the broader theme of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية (وقوله: وَلَا مُخَصّص أي موجود وتفسير قيامه تعالى بنفسه بعدم الافتقار إلى كلّ من المحل والمخصص اصطلاح لبعض المتكلمين، وهو المشهور، وفي اصطلاح بعضهم أنه بمعنى عدم الافتقار إلى المحلّ فقط؛ لأن عدم الافتقار إلى المخصص معلومٌ من صفة القِدَم. واعلم أن الموجودات بالنسبة إلى المحل والمخصص أربعة أقسام كما ذكره المصنف في المقدّمات: قِسْمٌ لا يَفْتَقِرُ إليهما، وهو ذاتُ الله تعالى، وقسم يفتقر إليهما، وهو أعراض الحوادث، وقسم لا يفتقر إلى المَحَلّ ويفتقر إلى المخصص، وهو ذاتُ الحوادث، وقسم يقومُ بالمحلّ ولا يفتقر إلى المخصص وهو صفاتُ الله تعالى
The marginal commentary of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusiyya: And his saying: 'and no specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ)' i.e. existent. And interpreting His being qā'im bi nafsih as the lack of need for both the locus (maḥall) and the specifier is the terminology of some theologians, and it is the well-known one. In the terminology of some others, it means only the lack of need for the locus, because the lack of need for the specifier is known from the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam). And know that existents, in relation to the locus and the specifier, are four divisions, as the author mentioned in the introductions: a division that does not need either of them, and that is the essence of Allah (may He be exalted); a division that needs both of them, and that is the accidents of originated things (a'rāḍ al-ḥawādith); a division that does not need the locus but needs the specifier, and that is the substance of originated things (dhāt al-ḥawādith); and a division that subsists in the locus and does not need the specifier, and that is the attributes of Allah (may He be exalted).
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is the main commentary (hashiya) of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusiyya, addressing the attribute 'qā'im bi nafsih' (self-subsisting). It first clarifies the term 'mukhaṣṣiṣ' (specifier) as 'existent'. It then presents two theological interpretations: the famous one (al-mashhūr) that 'self-subsisting' means not needing either a locus (maḥall) or a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ); and another opinion that it means not needing a locus only, because not needing a specifier is already known from the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam). Following this, al-Bajuri explains the fourfold classification of existents relative to need for a locus and a specifier, as presented by al-Sanusi in his introductions: (1) That which needs neither – the essence of Allah. (2) That which needs both – the accidents of originated things (a'rāḍ), which inhere in substances and require a specifier to come into existence. (3) That which does not need a locus but needs a specifier – the substance of originated things (jawāhir), i.e., individual bodies. (4) That which subsists in a locus but does not need a specifier – the attributes of Allah (like knowledge, power), which inhere in His essence but are eternal and not caused by an external specifier.

Key Terms
مخصص (mukhaṣṣiṣ)
محل (maḥall)
قيام بنفسه (qā'im bi nafsih)
قدم (qidam)
أعراض (a'rāḍ)
حوادث (ḥawādith)
ذات (dhāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'self-subsisting' (qā'im bi nafsih) means independent in all respects, without distinguishing between needing a locus and needing a specifier.
Correction: The passage shows a technical distinction: the well-known view is that it means not needing either, but another view says it means only not needing a locus, because not needing a specifier is already known from pre-eternity (qidam). The fourfold classification further clarifies that different existents have different dependencies.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which category of existents needs a specifier but not a locus?
A) The essence of Allah
B) The accidents of originated things
C) The substance of originated things
D) The attributes of Allah

Key Takeaway
Allah alone is absolutely self-sufficient, needing neither a place nor a cause; everything else falls into one of three dependent categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a problem, do you instinctively feel self-sufficient, forgetting that you are a contingent being in constant need of Allah's specifier and locus? How can you train your heart to turn to Him first, every time?
?
Allah's attributes like knowledge and power are eternal and need no cause. Do you ever take pride in your own knowledge or power as if they are self-generated? Will you consciously attribute every good quality you have to Allah's gift and sustainance?
?
The category 'substance of originated things' describes you: you exist but need a cause. Does your daily schedule reflect that you live every moment by Allah's will, or do you act as if you are the master of your time and choices? Commit to one concrete change today that acknowledges your complete dependence on Him.

Oneness of Essence: Negating Composition and Multiplicity
Definition of oneness of essence and negation of two types of quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of oneness of essence in Islamic theology and how it involves the negation of two types of quantity: continuous and discrete.

Core Idea
God's oneness means no composition and no equal — negating both internal parts and external counterparts.

In this passage, Sanusi begins to unpack the meaning of wahdaniyya (oneness) by focusing on the essence of God. The author explains that oneness of essence means the absence of any composition or multiplicity within God's being. This requires negating two kinds of quantity: continuous quantity (which would imply physical extension) and discrete quantity (which would imply a second being like God). Al-Ajhuri's gloss provides deeper insight into the metaphorical use of 'quantity' here.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the glosses (hashiya) from al-Ajhuri embedded within.
2
Identify the two negated quantities
The passage defines oneness of essence as the negation of two types of quantity: continuous (kamm muttasil) and discrete (kamm munfasil). Find each definition and notice what each implies about God.
Continuous quantity relates to composition; discrete quantity relates to having a counterpart.
3
Understand the metaphorical language
The commentator explains that 'quantity' is used metaphorically: the true cause (composition or multiplicity) is what is negated, and the term 'quantity' is applied to the negation as a figure of speech. Reflect on why this metaphor is necessary.
This clarifies that God is not being described as having or lacking quantity directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: (وَالْوَحْدَانِيَّةُ) أي: في الذات والصفات والأفعال أخذاً من تفسير المصنف - أعني قولَه (أي: لا ثاني له إلخ) ، ويُعْلَمُ من ذلك أن أقسام الوحدانية ثلاثة: وحدانية في الذات، ومعناها: عدم التركب في الذات، وعدم التعدد فيها، فهي عبارة عن نَفْي الكمّ المتّصل في الذات وهو عَرَضُ يقوم بمتصل الأجزاء، وعن نفي الكم المنفصل في الذات وهو عَرَضُ يقوم بمنفصل الأجزاء. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَهُوَ عَرَضٌ أي امتداد، وهو يشمل الامتدادات الثلاثة التي هي الطول والعرض والعمق والمراد بالكم المنفي هنا التركب من أجزاء، وإطلاق الكم عليه مجاز من إطلاق اسم المسبب على السبب؛ لأنه لو تركب من أجزاء لقام به الكم الحقيقي الذي هو الامتدادات الثلاثة أو بعضها. (قوله: وَهُوَ عَرَضٌ أي عددٌ بمعنى التعدد والمراد بالكم المنفصل المنفي: النظير، وإطلاق الكم عليه مجاز من إطلاق اسم المسبب على السبب؛ لأنه لو كان هناك نظير الله في الألوهية؛ لقام بهما، أي بالله والنظير عدد أي تعدد، وذلك التعدد هو الكم المنفصل، وبانتفاء النظير انتفى التعدد الذي هو الكم المنفصل. (قوله: بِمُنْفَصِلِ الْأَجْزَاءِ فيه مسامحة؛ لأن الموصوف به أشياء متفرقة لا تركب بينها، فلا تسمى أجزاء.
(His saying: 'And the oneness [wahdaniyya]') i.e., in essence, attributes, and actions, taken from the author's interpretation — I mean his saying (i.e., no second to Him, etc.), and from that it is known that the divisions of oneness are three: oneness in essence, and its meaning is the lack of composition in the essence and the lack of multiplicity in it; so it is an expression of negating the continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil) in the essence, and it is an accident subsisting in connected parts, and of negating the discrete quantity (al-kamm al-munfasil) in the essence, and it is an accident subsisting in separate parts. Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'and it is an accident') i.e., extension, and it includes the three extensions which are length, width, and depth; and what is meant by quantity negated here is composition from parts, and applying 'quantity' to it is a metaphor by way of applying the name of the effect to the cause, because if it were composed of parts, the true quantity which is the three extensions or some of them would subsist in it. (His saying: 'and it is an accident') i.e., number meaning multiplicity; and what is meant by the negated discrete quantity is the counterpart (al-nazir), and applying 'quantity' to it is a metaphor by way of applying the name of the effect to the cause, because if there were a counterpart to God in divinity, then number—i.e., multiplicity—would subsist in both, i.e., in God and the counterpart; and that multiplicity is the discrete quantity, and with the negation of the counterpart, the multiplicity which is the discrete quantity is negated. (His saying: 'in separate parts' [bi-munfasil al-ajza'] is a leniency, because that which is described by it is separate things with no composition between them, so they are not called parts.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the first division of oneness (wahdaniyya), specifically oneness of essence. It begins by quoting Sanusi's text that oneness applies to essence, attributes, and actions, and then deduces three types. The focus here is on oneness of essence, meaning the absence of composition and multiplicity in God's essence. This is explained as the negation of two types of quantity: continuous quantity (kamm muttasil), which would imply composition from parts and extension, and discrete quantity (kamm munfasil), which would imply multiplicity or having a counterpart. Al-Ajhuri's gloss clarifies that 'continuous quantity' means extension (length, width, depth) and the true quantity arises from composition; applying the term 'quantity' to the negation is metaphorical because composition is the cause of quantity. Similarly, negating a counterpart negates multiplicity, which is the cause of discrete quantity. The commentator also notes that describing 'separate parts' (munfasil al-ajza') is a leniency because the items are not actually parts, as there is no composition.

Key Terms
وحدانية
كم متصل
كم منفصل
تركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because the text mentions 'continuous quantity', some might think God has physical extension or is composed of parts.
Correction: The text explicitly negates continuous quantity, denying any extension or composition. The mention is only to define what is negated.

Checkpoint
What does 'continuous quantity' (al-kamm al-muttasil) refer to in this context?
A) God's power
B) Extension and composition from parts
C) The number of attributes
D) A synonym for 'essence'

Key Takeaway
God's oneness of essence means He is not composed of parts (no continuous quantity) and has no equal (no discrete quantity).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes mentally split God into 'parts'—His mercy here, His justice there—as if they conflict? How does this lesson on the negation of composition change the way you think about His attributes being one with His essence?
?
Where in your daily life do you unconsciously treat something else as a 'counterpart' to God—seeking ultimate security, validation, or pleasure from a person or thing? What would it take to truly negate that counterpart from your heart?
?
If God has no composition and no equal, then your entire existence depends on a single, indivisible, unparalleled Reality. Are you living as if that is true, or do your actions suggest you believe there are 'parts' to life He doesn't control?

Six Types of Quantum Negated by Oneness
Bajuri explains the deficiency in al-Sanusi's phrase and enumerates six quanta.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that oneness (tawhid) negates six types of quantum: continuous and discrete in essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
Oneness negates every form of multiplicity, whether external or internal.

Bajuri observes that al-Sanusi's phrase 'no second to Him' primarily negates discrete quantum (a separate being), but can be extended to negate continuous quantum (composition within). This leads to the understanding that oneness actually negates six quanta—continuous and discrete in each of essence, attributes, and actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice that Bajuri starts by stating a deficiency in the author's wording.
The phrase 'no second to Him' is immediately understood as negating another separate entity.
2
Understand the deficiency
Bajuri says the immediate understanding (al-mutabadir) only covers discrete quantum. Reflect on why this is a deficiency: it leaves out continuous quantum (internal division).
Discrete quantum means separate individuals; continuous quantum means parts within a single thing.
3
See the possible extension
Bajuri explains that we can extend the meaning to include continuous quantum by saying 'no second to Him neither by connection nor by separation' in essence, attributes, and actions.
This gives a more comprehensive negation of multiplicity.
4
Count the six quanta
Bajuri concludes that the quanta are six: for each of essence, attributes, and actions, there are two types—continuous and discrete. All are negated by oneness.
This is the key takeaway: oneness negates all forms of multiplicity in God.
5
Reflect on the sixth quantum
Bajuri notes that the negation of the sixth (continuous quantum in actions) depends on how it is depicted—if as participation, it is negated; if as multiplicity of acts, it is affirmed (as learned earlier).
This nuance shows careful consideration of divine actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عَلِمْتَ ذلك؛ عَلِمْتَ أن في قول المصنف (أي: لا ثاني له... إلخ) قصوراً؛ لأن المتبادِرَ منه إنما هو نفي الكمّ المنفصل في الذات والصفات والأفعال، ويُمْكِنُ أن يستفاد منه أيضاً: نفي الكم المتصل في الذات والصفات والأفعال بناءً على تصويره بما ذكر بأن يقال: المراد: لا ثاني له لا اتصالاً ولا انفصالاً في ذاته ولا في صفاته ولا في أفعاله. 2 والحاصل أن الكُمُومَ سِتَّة، وكلُّها منفيَّة بالوحدانية، لكن محله في السادس: : إن صُوّرَ بالمشارَكَة كما علمت فتدبر.
If you know that, you know that in the author's statement (i.e., 'no second to Him... etc.') there is deficiency, because what is immediately understood from it is only the negation of discrete quantum in essence, attributes, and actions. But it is possible to also benefit from it the negation of continuous quantum in essence, attributes, and actions, based on its depiction as mentioned, by saying: the intended meaning is: no second to Him, neither by connection nor by separation, in His essence, nor in His attributes, nor in His actions. And the conclusion is that the quanta are six, and all of them are negated by oneness, but its place in the sixth is if it is depicted as participation, as you have learned, so reflect.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the author's (al-Sanusi's) phrase 'no second to Him' (la thani lahu) in defining oneness. Bajuri points out a deficiency because the immediate understanding (al-mutabadir) from that phrase is only the negation of discrete quantum (al-kamm al-munfasil) in essence, attributes, and actions. However, it is possible to extend the meaning to also include the negation of continuous quantum (al-kamm al-muttasil) based on how it is depicted. The intended meaning can be: no second to Him, neither by connection (ittisal) nor by separation (infisal), in His essence, attributes, and actions. Then he summarizes that the quanta (kumum) are six: for each of essence, attributes, and actions, there are two types—continuous and discrete. All six are negated by oneness (wahdaniyya). However, concerning the sixth type (which is continuous quantum in actions), its negation depends on how it is depicted: if it is depicted as participation (musharaka) in the same act, it is negated; but if depicted as multiplicity of acts, it is affirmed, as learned earlier.

Key Terms
الكُمُوم
الكم المتصل
الكم المنفصل
الذات
الصفات
الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'no second to Him' only negates another separate being (discrete quantum), but does not address internal composition.
Correction: The phrase can be extended to also negate continuous quantum (internal composition) in essence, attributes, and actions, resulting in six quanta being negated by oneness.

Checkpoint
How many types of quantum are negated by oneness according to the passage?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Six
D) Nine

Key Takeaway
Oneness negates all six forms of quantum—continuous and discrete in essence, attributes, and actions—ensuring that God is free from any multiplicity or composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If oneness negates every form of multiplicity in God, what does the multiplicity of your own distractions and divided intentions in worship say about the state of your tawheed? Today, will you consciously strive to unify your heart in a single act of devotion?
?
Bajuri teaches that oneness negates continuous quantum in actions only if it is participation. Are there areas in your life where you feel you are 'cooperating' with God rather than submitting to His sole agency? How will you realign your actions to reflect pure tawheed al-af'al?
?
The lesson shows that 'no second to Him' can be misunderstood to exclude only separate beings. Where in your life have you subtly admitted a 'second'—a source of good or power that you rely on alongside God? What concrete step can you take this week to uproot that hidden association?

The Objection to the Definition of Oneness and Its Answer
Bajuri answers the objection that 'no second' is a definition of al-wahid not al-wahdaniyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the concepts of al-wahid and al-wahdaniyya, and why the author defined al-wahdaniyya as 'no second to Him' rather than as 'negation of duality'.

Core Idea
The direct negation of 'second' is the heart of declaring God's oneness.

In this passage, Bajuri addresses an objection raised against Sanusi's phrasing. The objector claims that defining oneness (al-wahdaniyya) as 'no second to Him' actually defines the One (al-wahid), not oneness. Bajuri explains the author's deliberate choice and defends it, showing how the direct denial of any partner is more explicit and central to the meaning of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's claim: that 'no second to Him' defines al-wahid, not al-wahdaniyya. Understand the confusion between the attribute and the concept.
2
Understand the correct definition
The correct definition of al-wahdaniyya is the negation of duality (nafy al-ithnayniyya) in essence, attributes, and actions.
3
Analyze the author's nukta
The author deliberately used 'no second' to directly state the negation of the second, which is the intended goal, even though it is implied by the negation of duality.
This is a teaching point about explicit vs. implicit expression.
4
Study al-Ajhuri's gloss
Al-Ajhuri clarifies that the objection only applies if the author had said 'one who has no second', but he said 'no second to him', which directly negates the second and is the meaning of oneness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: أَيْ: لَا ثَانِيَ لَهُ... إلخ) اعْتُرِضَ: بأن هذا تفسير للواحد لا للوحدانية، مع أن ظاهِرَ كلام المصنف أنه تفسير للوحدانية، والصوابُ في تفسيرها أن يقول: أي: نفي الإثنينية في الذات والصفات والأفعال. وأجيب بأن نُكتَةَ ارتكاب المصنف لهذا الصنيع التصريح بنفي الثاني الذي هو المقصود؛ وإن كان يُؤخَذُ من نفي الإثنينية نَفْيُه بطريق اللزوم لا بطريق التصريح، التعدد أنه لا تتحقَّقُ الوَحْدَانِيَّةُ إلا بنفي وإنما اقتصر المصنف على نَفْي الثاني - مع حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري . (قوله: وَهُوَ ثُبُوتُ فِعْلٍ) إطلاق الكم على هذا من إطلاق اسم المسبب على السبب؛ لأن حقيقة الكم العدد القائم بفعل الله وفعل غيره. و (قوله: اعْتُرِضَ... إلخ) هذا الاعتراض لا يرد إلا لو قال المصنف: أي: من لا ثاني له، وهو لم يقل ذلك، وإنما قال: لا ثاني (له الذي معناه انتفاء الثاني، وهو معنى الوحدانية لا معنى الواحد.
His saying: 'i.e., no second to Him... etc.' It was objected that this is a definition of al-wahid (the One) not of al-wahdaniyya (oneness), while the apparent meaning of the author's statement is that it is a definition of al-wahdaniyya. The correct in its definition is to say: i.e., the negation of duality (ithnayniyya) in essence, attributes, and actions. And it was answered that the point (nukta) of the author's doing this is to explicitly state the negation of the second (al-thani) which is the intended goal; even though it is taken from the negation of duality by way of implication (al-luzum) not by direct statement. He only limited himself to negating the second. - With the gloss of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'and it is the establishment of an act...' The application of quantum to this is from the application of the name of the effect to the cause, because the reality of quantum is the number subsisting in the act of God and the act of another. And his saying: 'it was objected... etc.' This objection only applies if the author said: i.e., one who has no second, but he did not say that; rather he said: 'no second to him' which means the negation of the second, and that is the meaning of al-wahdaniyya, not the meaning of al-wahid.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the interpretation of the author's phrase 'no second to Him' as a definition of على الوحدانية (oneness). The objector says that this phrase actually defines الواحد (the One), not الوحدانية. The correct definition of الوحدانية should be 'negation of duality in essence, attributes, and actions'. The answer is that the author intentionally used this wording to directly state the negation of the second (الثاني), which is the real goal, even though the negation of the second is implied by the negation of duality. The author deliberately avoided the indirect expression to make the meaning clear. Then a commentary by al-Ajhuri follows, explaining that the application of quantum (الكم) to the establishment of an act is a metonymy (إطلاق اسم المسبب على السبب) because quantum in reality is the number subsisting in the acts of God and others. He also clarifies that the objection would only be valid if the author had said 'من لا ثاني له' (one who has no second), but he said 'لا ثاني له' (no second to him), which directly means the negation of the second, and that is the meaning of الوحدانية, not the meaning of الواحد.

Key Terms
لا ثاني له
الوحدانية
الواحد
نفي الإثنينية
لزوم
نكتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'لا ثاني له' defines الواحد (the One), not الوحدانية (oneness).
Correction: The phrase 'لا ثاني له' correctly defines الوحدانية because it directly negates the second, which is the core meaning of oneness, and the author's wording avoids confusion.

Checkpoint
What is the nukta (point) behind the author's definition of الوحدانية as 'لا ثاني له' instead of 'نفي الإثنينية'?
A) To be brief
B) To explicitly state the negation of the second, which is the intended goal
C) To follow the wording of the Quran
D) To avoid confusion with attributes

Key Takeaway
The definition of oneness as 'no second to Him' is deliberate to emphasize the explicit negation of any partner, which is the essence of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your heart truly affirm that there is no second to Allah in every sense—essence, attributes, and actions—or do you sometimes act as if something else shares His uniqueness? Identify one area of your life where you have not fully negated a 'second' and commit to explicitly rejecting it today.
?
The author deliberately chose direct negation over implication. In your own practice of tawhid, are you direct and explicit in negating anything that rivals Allah, or do you allow indirect compromises to creep in? What 'second' have you been hesitant to name and reject?
?
If someone asked you to define your faith in Allah, would you say 'no second to Him' with the same clarity and conviction as the author? What fears or attachments prevent you from being that explicit in your own declaration of tawhid?

Negation of a Second in Divine Attributes
Al-Bajuri explains the negation of a second partner in essence, attributes, and acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negating a second partner in the divine essence and attributes logically entails negating all higher numbers, and how the term 'second' is grammatically connected to the categories of essence and attributes.

Core Idea
Negating a second in God negates all partners, because the third depends on the second.

This lesson examines a key passage from Al-Bajuri's commentary on Sanusi's creed, where he clarifies the absolute negation of any partner in God's essence and attributes. The argument is logical: if there is no second, there can be no third or beyond. Al-Bajuri also provides grammatical analysis and notes the author's intention of generality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation together. Notice the logical chain from negating the second to negating all numbers.
2
Understand the logical argument
The passage states that negating the second entails negating all higher numbers. Why? Because the third cannot exist without the second.
This is a simple deductive argument: if no second, then no third, fourth, etc.
3
Examine the grammatical analysis
Al-Bajuri explains that 'in His essence' and 'in His attributes' are attached to 'second', and the preposition 'in' implies 'partner'. This shows the negation covers all aspects.
The grammatical structure reinforces the theological point.
4
Reflect on the marginal note
Al-Ajhuri clarifies that the author intended generality, meaning 'second' stands for any partner, not just the number two.
This resolves any potential misunderstanding about the scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلقاً سواء كان بالتثنية أو بالتثليث أو غير ذلك ؛ لأنه يَلْزَمُ من نفيه نَفْي غيره من العدد؛ إذ لا يتأتى الثالثُ فما فوقَهُ إلا بعد تحقق الثاني. ويُمْكِنُ أن المصنف قصد التعميم في نفي الأعداد مطلقاً. فتأمل. والنظير. (قوله: فِي ذَاتِهِ) متعلّق بقوله: (ثاني)، وعدَّاه بـ (في) لتضمنه معنى: الشريك (وقوله: وَلَا فِي صِفَاتِهِ أي ولا ثاني له في صفاته، فالجار والمجرور متعلّق بقوله: (ثاني) كالذي قبله وكالذي بعده. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: قَصَدَ التَّعْمِيمَ بأن يكون أراد بالثاني مطلق المشارك الشامل للثاني ومن فوقه.
Absolutely, whether it be by duality or trinity or otherwise; because from its negation follows the negation of other numbers; since the third and beyond cannot occur except after the realization of the second. And it is possible that the author intended generality in negating numbers absolutely. Consider. And the counterpart. (His saying: IN HIS ESSENCE) is attached to his saying: (SECOND), and he made it transitive with (in) because it includes the meaning of partner (And his saying: AND NOT IN HIS ATTRIBUTES) meaning there is no second to Him in His attributes, so the prepositional phrase is attached to his saying: (SECOND) similar to what preceded it and what follows it. Marginal note by the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: HE INTENDED GENERALITY) meaning he meant by 'second' any partner in general, encompassing the second and those above it.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on Sanusi's statement negating any partner in essence, attributes, and acts. Al-Bajuri begins by explaining that the negation of a 'second' (thani) is absolute, covering duality, trinity, or any other number. The logical reason is that negating the second entails negating all higher numbers, because the third and beyond depend on the existence of the second. He then suggests that the author may have intended a general negation of all numbers. The grammatical analysis follows: 'in His essence' (fi dhatihi) is attached to 'second' (thani), and the preposition 'in' (fi) is used to imply 'partner' (sharik). Similarly, 'in His attributes' means no second in attributes, with the same grammatical structure. The marginal note by al-Ajhuri confirms that the author intended a general meaning of 'second' as any partner, including the second and those above it. The entire lesson focuses on the exclusivity of God in essence and attributes.

Key Terms
ثاني
شريك
تعميم
نفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating a second only applies to the number two.
Correction: The argument shows that negating the second logically entails negating all higher numbers, so it applies to any number beyond one.

Checkpoint
Why does negating a second entail negating all higher numbers?
A) Because numbers are infinite
B) Because the third cannot exist without the second
C) Because the author intended generality
D) Because the second is the only partner

Key Takeaway
Negating a second partner in God's essence and attributes logically negates any possible partner, because any number beyond one depends on the existence of a second.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever, even subtly, imagine a second alongside God in your mind when thinking about His attributes? If so, what does this lesson reveal about that thought?
?
How does realizing that the very concept of a third depends on a second affect your certainty that there is absolutely no partner?
?
If God's attributes are unique and cannot be shared, how should that change the way you speak about God's mercy or power in comparison to creation?

The Meaning of 'ثم' in the Order of Attributes
Sanusi explains why 'ثم' indicates order of mention, not temporal order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'ثم' in the context of divine attributes indicates only order of mention, not any real temporal sequence, and how the ordering of negative and positive attributes follows the analogy of purification before adornment.

Core Idea
In divine attributes, 'ثم' signals order of mention, not time.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a potential misunderstanding about the word 'thumma' (then) when listing divine attributes. He explains that it does not imply any temporal delay in the necessity of God's attributes, but merely indicates the sequence in which they are mentioned. This leads to a deeper insight about the relationship between negative and positive attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice the word 'ثم' and the claim that there is no delay in the necessity of attributes.
The passage begins with 'قوله: ثُمَّ يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى'.
2
Understand the distinction between temporal and mention order
The author clarifies that 'ثم' is for order of mention (al-tartīb al-dhikrī), not temporal order. Grasp why temporal delay would imply originatedness (huduth), which is impossible for divine attributes.
3
Reflect on the analogy of purification and adornment
The negative attributes are like purification (takhliya) and the positive attributes like adornment (tahliya). Just as one cleans before putting on fine clothes, the negative attributes are mentioned first. This is a conventional order.
The analogy of the bather (داخل الحمام) illustrates this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ثُمَّ يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى لا يخفى أنه لا تأخر في وجوب صفاته تعالى، وإلا؛ لكان المتأخّرُ وجوبه حادثاً، وهو محالٌ. وبهذا يُعْلَمُ أن (ثُمَّ) لمجرد الترتيب الذكري - أي: الإخباري - بمعنى: أنه بَعْدَ أن أخبر بصفات السُّلُوب أخير بصفات المعاني، وإنما قَدَّمَ صفاتِ السلوب على صفات المعاني؛ لأن الأولى من قبيل التخلية - بالخاء المعجمة - والثانية من قبيل التحلية - بالحاء المهملة ، والأولى مُقَدَّمَةٌ عرفاً على الثانية؛ إذ الإنسانُ لا يتزيَّن بجميل الثياب ونحوها إلا بعد إزالة ما به من الأوساخ، كداخِلِ الحَمَّام، فإنه يُزِيلُ أَدْرَانَهُ - أي: أوساخه -، ثم يَلْبَسُ ثِيَابَهُ. وإنما أعاد لفظ (يجب) مع تقدمه سابقاً في قوله: (فمما يجب... إلخ) للفصل بقوله: (فهذه ست صفات... إلخ)، وللريّ صريحاً على من نفى وجوب صفات المعاني كالمعتزلة.
His saying: 'Then it is necessary for Him, exalted is He.' It is not hidden that there is no delay in the necessity of His attributes, exalted is He; otherwise, the one whose necessity is delayed would be originated (hadith), and that is impossible. By this it is known that 'thumma' is merely for order of mention (al-tartīb al-dhikrī), i.e., informative order, meaning that after he informed of the negative attributes, he informs of the attributes of meanings (sifāt al-ma'ānī). He only placed the negative attributes before the attributes of meanings because the former are of the type of purification (takhliya) and the latter are of the type of adornment (tahliya), and the former is conventionally placed before the latter, just as a person does not adorn himself with beautiful garments and the like except after removing the filth from him, like the bather who removes his dirt (adrānahu) then puts on his garments. He only repeated the word 'yajibu' even though it preceded earlier in his saying 'fammā yajibu...' etc., because of the separation by his saying 'fahādhihi sittu sifāt...' etc., and as a clear refutation of those who denied the necessity of the attributes of meanings, like the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage explains the use of 'thumma' (then) in the phrase 'thumma yajibu lahu ta'ālā'. The author first asserts that there is no temporal delay in the necessity of God's attributes, as that would imply originatedness (huduth). Then he clarifies that 'thumma' here indicates only order of mention (al-tartīb al-dhikrī), i.e., the author first mentioned the negative attributes (sifāt salbiyya) and then the attributes of meanings (sifāt al-ma'ānī). The reason for this order is that the negative attributes are like purification (takhliya) and the attributes of meanings are like adornment (tahliya). An analogy is given: a person in a bathhouse first removes dirt (adrān) and then wears fine clothes. The passage also explains why the word 'yajibu' is repeated: it was separated by an intervening statement, and it also serves as a clear refutation of the Mu'tazila who deny the necessity of the attributes of meanings. The passage ends with a mention of the objection (which is cut off in the text).

Key Terms
ثم
ترتيب ذكري
صفات المعاني
تخلية
تحلية
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'thumma' might be understood as indicating a temporal sequence in the necessity of God's attributes, implying that some attributes became necessary later than others.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that 'thumma' here is solely for order of mention (al-tartīb al-dhikrī), not for any real temporal delay. Any delay in necessity would imply originatedness, which is impossible for the Eternal.

Checkpoint
Why did the author place the negative attributes before the attributes of meanings?
A) Because they are more important
B) Because they are temporal
C) As a principle of purification before adornment
D) By random choice

Key Takeaway
'ثم' in divine attributes is for order of mention, and the sequence of negative then positive attributes follows the natural order of purification before adornment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The analogy of the bather removes dirt before putting on clothes. In your own life, do you prioritize removing your faults (takhliya) over acquiring virtues (tahliya), or do you skip the purification? What step are you avoiding right now?
?
If you learned that a certain truth about God's attributes is only mentioned in a particular order for pedagogical reasons, could that challenge your assumptions about what is most important in your faith? How would you adjust your priorities?

The Six Contradicting Possibilities
Sanusi enumerates the six contradicting possibilities with examples and a poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six pairs of contradicting possibilities (al-mumkināt al-mutaqābilāt) and why they demonstrate the scope of God's power over all specific things.

Core Idea
God's power covers all specific possibilities, each paired with its opposite.

Sanusi introduces a key classification in kalam: the six contradicting possibilities. These are specific instances that can be affirmed or negated for any possible thing. Understanding this classification helps us grasp that God's power encompasses every possible state, from existence to non-existence, specific attributes, times, places, directions, and quantities. Let's explore the list and the poem that aids memorization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage starts by linking back to the previous discussion on what is possible for Allah.
2
Identify the six pairs
The passage lists six specific things, each contrasted with its opposite. For each item, note the specific instance (e.g., existence) and its opposite (non-existence).
3
Reflect on the poem
The poem summarizes the six pairs in a memorable verse. Read it aloud or write it down. It is a tool for memorization used by traditional scholars.
The poem says 'our existence and non-existence' – meaning existence and non-existence are included.
4
Connect to kalam
Ask yourself why Sanusi lists these six. They show that God's power extends over every possible specific thing, whether it exists or not, having this attribute or that, in this time or another, etc.
This classification is foundational for arguments about God's omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد ببعض ما يجوز عليه الأشياءُ السِّتَّةُ التي تقابلها ستة أخرى، وتلك الأشياءُ هي الوجودُ بدلاً عن العَدَم والصفةُ المخصوصة بدلاً عن سائر الصفات، والزمانُ المخصوص بدلاً عن سائر الأزمنة، والمكان المخصوص بدلاً عن سائر الأمكنة، والجهة المخصوصة بدلاً عن سائر الجهات والمقدار المخصوص بدلاً عن سائر المقادير. وهذه الأشياء تسمَّى المُمْكِنَاتِ المتقابلات. وقد نَظَمَها بعضُهم بقوله: الْمُمْكِنَاتُ الْمُتَقَابِلَاتُ وُجُودُنَا وَالْعَدَمُ الصَّفَاتُ أَزْمِنَةٌ أَمْكِنَةٌ جِهَاتُ كَذَا الْمَقَادِيرُ رَوَى الثَّقَاتُ
And what is meant by 'some of what is possible for Him' are the six things that are opposed by six others. Those things are: existence instead of non-existence, the specific attribute instead of other attributes, the specific time instead of other times, the specific place instead of other places, the specific direction instead of other directions, and the specific quantity instead of other quantities. These things are called the contradicting possibles (al-mumkināt al-mutaqābilāt). And some of them have versified them saying: 'The contradicting possibles are: our existence and non-existence, the attributes, times, places, directions, likewise the quantities – the trustworthy have transmitted.'
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines a key term in kalam: 'some of what is possible for Him' refers to the six pairs of contradicting possibilities. Each of these six is a specific instance contrasted with its opposite. The six are: 1) existence vs. non-existence, 2) a specific attribute vs. other attributes, 3) a specific time vs. other times, 4) a specific place vs. other places, 5) a specific direction vs. other directions, 6) a specific quantity vs. other quantities. These are called 'al-mumkināt al-mutaqābilāt' (the contradicting possibles). A poem is then cited that lists them in verse form for memorization. The poem mentions 'our existence and non-existence' meaning that for each possible thing, it either exists or does not, and similarly for attributes, times, etc. This classification is foundational for understanding God's power over all possible states.

Key Terms
ممكنات متقابلات
الوجود
العدم
الصفة المخصوصة
الزمان المخصوص
المكان المخصوص
الجهة المخصوصة
المقدار المخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think these six are distinct types of possibilities, but they are actually six pairs of contradictories.
Correction: Each of the six items is a specific instance contrasted with its opposite, covering all specific possibilities.

Checkpoint
How many things are listed as 'contradicting possibles' (al-mumkināt al-mutaqābilāt)?
A) Three
B) Six
C) Twelve
D) Two

Key Takeaway
The six contradicting possibilities (existence, attribute, time, place, direction, quantity) are each paired with their opposites, showing that God's power encompasses every specific state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you wake each morning, do you truly believe that your existence was not a chance but a specific act of God's will? How would your day change if you lived with this certainty?
?
Are you anxious about the future because you doubt that God's power encompasses every specific time and place in your life? What does this lesson say about that?
?
Think of a specific attribute you dislike about yourself. Do you realize that your specific attribute exists only because God willed it, and He could have willed otherwise? How does that shape your acceptance of yourself?

Definition of Special Possibility (al-Imkan al-Khas)
Al-Bajuri defines and explains special possibility (al-imkan al-khas).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of special possibility as the negation of necessity from both sides and its essence as the permissibility of existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Special possibility means a thing's existence and non-existence are equally permissible — neither is necessary.

This lesson dives into one of the most fundamental terms in kalam: al-imkan al-khas (special possibility). Al-Bajuri explains what it means for something to be 'possible' in the technical sense used by theologians. You will see the core definition, the logical consequences, and a subtle refinement about when one formulation of the definition applies.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of special possibility
Al-Bajuri defines special possibility as 'the negation of necessity from both sides' — the side agreeing with a judgment and the side opposing it. Read the passage and identify this core idea.
Notice the example of saying 'Zayd exists with special possibility'.
2
Follow the logical inference
The passage then infers that negating necessity from both sides also negates impossibility from both sides, leaving only permissibility. Trace this reasoning in the text.
This shows that special possibility is essentially the permissibility of existence and non-existence.
3
Understand the nuance in the definition
Al-Bajuri notes that the 'agreeing side/opposing side' formulation only works when a judgment (e.g., 'Zayd exists') has been made. If no judgment has been made, it is better to say 'negation of necessity from existence and non-existence' or simply 'permissibility of existence and non-existence'.
This clarifies that the definition can be expressed in different ways depending on context.
4
Note the grammatical point
The phrase 'bi al-imkan al-khas' is a circumstantial qualifier (hal) describing Zayd, not a circumstantial object. Understand why this matters for the intended meaning.
Possibility is a description of the thing itself, not a mere condition of the verb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: بِجَمِيعِ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ أي الأمور التي يجوز وجودها وعَدَمُها بحيث يستوي إليها نسبة الوجود والعدم، فهي من قبيل الممكن بالإمكان الخاص، وهو سَلْبُ الضرورة بمعنى الوجوب عن الطرفين - أي: الطرف الموافق لما نَطَقْتَ والطرف المخالف له ، فإذا قلت: زيد موجود بالإمكان الخاص؛ كان المعنى حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري. (قوله: وَهُوَ سَلْبُ الضَّرُورَةِ ... إلخ) يلزم من سلب الضرورة عنهما: سلب الإحالة عنهما؛ لأن استحالة أحدهما تتوقف على وجود الآخر، والفرض أن الوجوب منتف عنهما، وإذا انتفى وجوبهما واستحالتهما ثبت جوازهما وهو معنى الإمكان الخاص وبهذا تعلم أن تعريف الإمكان الخاص بما ذكر من التعريف بالملزوم أو اللازم وحقيقته جواز الوجود والعدم، فالممكن بالإمكان الخاص ما جاز وجوده وعدمه. ثم إن تعبيرهم بالطرف الموافق لما نطقت به والمخالف له إنما يظهر في ممكن حكم عليه بالوجود أو العدم، كأن قيل زيد موجود أو ليس بموجود، وقيد ذلك الحكم بالإمكان الخاص ولا يظهر في الممكن الذي لم يحكم عليه بواحد منهما، فإن طرفيه وهما الوجود والعدم لا يوصفان حينئذ بموافقة ولا بمخالفة لما نطقت به؛ إذ الفرض أن لا نطق، فكان الظاهر في التعريف أن يقال: هو سلب الضرورة عن الوجود والعدم، بل كان الأولى من ذلك أن يقال: هو جواز الوجود والعدم. (وقوله: بِالْإِمْكَانِ الْخَاصِ متعلق بمحذوف حال من الضمير المستتر في (موجود) العائد على (زيد)؛ لأن المقصود جعل الإمكان صفة لزيد، وليس ظرفاً لغواً متعلقاً بموجود؛ لأنه خلاف المقصود.
His saying: 'with all possible things' means things whose existence and non-existence are permissible such that the relation of existence and non-existence is equal to them. They are of the type of possible by special possibility (al-imkan al-khas), which is the negation of necessity (darura) in the sense of obligation (wujub) from the two sides—i.e., the side agreeing with what you stated and the side opposing it. So if you say: 'Zayd exists with special possibility', the meaning is... marginal note of al-Allamah Ahmad al-Ajhuri. His saying: 'And it is the negation of necessity... etc.' It follows from the negation of necessity from them that impossibility is negated from them, because the impossibility of one of them depends on the existence of the other, and the assumption is that obligation is negated from both of them. When their obligation and impossibility are negated, their permissibility is established, and that is the meaning of special possibility. By this you know that the definition of special possibility is by what was mentioned as a definition by the concomitant or the necessary, and its essence is the permissibility of existence and non-existence. So the possible by special possibility is that whose existence and non-existence are permissible. Then, their expression 'the side agreeing with what you stated and the side opposing it' only appears in a possible that is judged by existence or non-existence, such as if it is said: 'Zayd exists' or 'is not existent', and that judgment is restricted by special possibility. It does not appear in the possible that has not been judged by either of them, because its two sides—existence and non-existence—are not then described by agreement or opposition to what you stated, since the assumption is that there is no statement. So it would be apparent in the definition to say: 'it is the negation of necessity from existence and non-existence', rather it would be better than that to say: 'it is the permissibility of existence and non-existence.' And his saying: 'with special possibility' is connected to an omitted circumstantial qualifier from the implicit pronoun in 'exists' referring to Zayd, because the intent is to make possibility a description of Zayd, not as a circumstantial object connected to 'exists', because that would be contrary to the intent.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the concept of special possibility (al-imkan al-khas). The author begins by quoting Sanusi's statement from the matn: 'with all possible things', and glosses it as things whose existence and non-existence are equally possible. This is special possibility, defined as the negation of necessity from both the side that agrees with the judgment and the side that opposes it. For example, saying 'Zayd exists with special possibility' means that neither his existence nor his non-existence is necessary. The commentator then infers that negating necessity from both sides implies negating impossibility as well, and when both are negated, the thing becomes permissible—this is the essence of special possibility. He also notes that the definition using 'the side agreeing with what you stated' is only appropriate when a judgment has been made (e.g., 'Zayd exists'). If no judgment has been pronounced, it is better to define it as 'negation of necessity from existence and non-existence' or even more simply as 'permissibility of existence and non-existence'. Finally, a grammatical point: the phrase 'bi al-imkan al-khas' is a circumstantial qualifier (hal) describing Zayd, not a circumstantial object (zarf).

Key Terms
الإمكان الخاص
سلب الضرورة
جواز الوجود والعدم
الطرف الموافق
الطرف المخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition 'negation of necessity from the side agreeing and the side opposing' is always the correct formulation.
Correction: This formulation only applies when a judgment (e.g., 'Zayd exists') has been made. If no judgment has been made, it is better to say 'negation of necessity from existence and non-existence' or simply 'permissibility of existence and non-existence'.

Checkpoint
What is the essence (ḥaqīqa) of special possibility according to the text?
A) Necessity of existence
B) Necessity of non-existence
C) Permissibility of existence and non-existence
D) Negation of permissibility

Key Takeaway
Special possibility means that a thing's existence and non-existence are equally permissible — neither is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your existence is necessary, forgetting that you are merely a special possible? Take a moment to realize that your continued existence is a gift from the Necessary Being. How does this awareness change your attitude toward the next hour of your life?
?
If your existence and non-existence are equally permissible from a rational standpoint, then every breath you take is a mercy. What specific action will you take today to acknowledge that you are not self-necessary but dependent on the One who gives you existence?

Definition of General Possibility (al-Imkan al-`Amm) and Its Application to God
Al-Bajuri defines general possibility and demonstrates its inapplicability to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how general possibility is defined in Islamic theology and why it cannot be applied to God, while also clarifying the scope of divine power and will.

Core Idea
General possibility negates impossibility, but applying it to God would make the necessary contingent.

Al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin defines a key term: general possibility (al-imkan al-`amm). This concept is foundational for distinguishing between what is necessary, possible, and impossible for God. In this passage, Al-Bajuri explains the logical structure of general possibility and then demonstrates why it is inappropriate to apply it to the Divine Essence, thereby preserving God's necessary existence and the proper function of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of general possibility
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where Al-Bajuri defines general possibility as the absence of impossibility. Note how it relates to the 'negation of necessity' from the opposing side.
The term 'opposing side' refers to the non-existence of the thing.
2
Apply the concept to God
Examine the application where Al-Bajuri considers saying 'God exists with general possibility'. Identify why this leads to a problem: it treats God's existence as merely possible rather than necessary.
The agreeing side (existence) becomes obligatory for God, so general possibility is not applicable.
3
Clarify the function of divine power and will
Read the final part about power and will not relating to impossibilities. Understand why not relating is not a sign of incapacity but a logical necessity.
This guards against the misconception that God's power is limited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَهُوَ سَلْبُ الضَّرُورَةِ... إلخ) يلزم من سلب الضرورة عن الطرف المخالف سلب الاستحالة عن الطرف الموافق، وهو معنى الإمكان العام، فالإمكان العام عدم استحالة الشيء الصادق بجوازه ووجوبه ولصدقه بالوجوب والجواز الذي هو الإمكان عاماً. الخاص سمي ثم إن التعبير بالطرف المخالف إنما يظهر في ممكن عام حكم عليه بالوجود، وقيد ذلك الحكم بالإمكان العام ولا يظهر في الممكن العام الذي لم يحكم عليه بالوجود، حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية أن الطرف الموافق لما نطقت به - وهو ثبوتُ الوجود له ـ ليس بواجب، وكذلك الطرفُ المخالِفُ لما نطقت به ـ وهو عدم ثبوته له - لا بالإمكان العام، وهو سَلْبُ الضرورة، بمعنى: الوجوب عن الطرف المخالف فقط، فإذا قلت: الله موجود بالإمكان العام كان المعنى أن الطرف المخالف ـ وهو عدم ثبوت الوجود له تعالى - ليس بواجب. وأما الطرف الموافق؛ فهو واجب له هنا، وإنما لم يصح إرادة الإمكان العام هنا؛ لدخول الواجبات في الممكنات حينئذ، مع أنَّ كُلّاً أنَّ كُلّاً من القدرة والإرادة لا يتعلق بها كما لا يتعلق بالمستحيلات، ولا يلزم من عدم تعلق القدرة بهما عَجْز؛ لأنهما ليسا من وظيفتها، ولأنها لو تعلَّقَتْ بهما؛ لَزِمَ الفسادُ؛ إذ يلزم عليه تعلقها بإعدام الذات العلية، وبسَلْب الألوهية عنها، ونحو ذلك.
His saying: 'And it is the negation of necessity... etc.' It follows from the negation of necessity from the opposing side that impossibility is negated from the agreeing side, and that is the meaning of general possibility (al-imkan al-`amm). General possibility is the absence of impossibility of a thing, true for its possibility and necessity, and its truth by obligation and possibility—that is general possibility. The special was named... Then, the expression 'the opposing side' only appears in a general possible that is judged by existence, and that judgment is restricted by general possibility; it does not appear in the general possible that has not been judged by existence. The gloss of al-Bajuri on al-Sanussiyya: That the agreeing side to what you stated—which is the affirmation of existence for it—is not obligatory, and likewise the opposing side to what you stated—which is the non-affirmation of existence for it—is not by general possibility, which is the negation of necessity in the sense of obligation from the opposing side only. So if you say: 'God exists with general possibility', the meaning is that the opposing side—which is the non-affirmation of existence for Him, exalted—is not obligatory. As for the agreeing side, it is obligatory for Him here. And it is not correct to intend general possibility here because it would include necessary beings among possibles then. Moreover, that each of power and will does not relate to them just as they do not relate to impossibilities, and it does not follow from the non-relation of power to them that there is incapability, because they are not part of its function. And because if power were to relate to them, corruption would necessarily follow, for it would necessitate its relation to the annihilation of the Supreme Essence and to the negation of divinity from it, and similar things.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines general possibility (al-imkan al-`amm). The commentator explains that negating necessity from the opposing side implies negating impossibility from the agreeing side, which is the meaning of general possibility. General possibility is defined as the absence of impossibility of a thing, and it can be true for both possible and necessary things. The expression 'the opposing side' is only used when a judgment of existence has been made. Al-Bajuri then applies this to God: if one says 'God exists with general possibility', the meaning is that the opposing side (non-existence) is not necessary, but the agreeing side (existence) is necessary for God. Therefore, general possibility cannot be correctly applied to God because it would treat necessary beings as possible. The passage also clarifies that divine power and will do not relate to impossibilities, and that not relating to them is not a sign of incapability; rather, relating to them would lead to corruption, such as the annihilation of God's essence or the negation of divinity.

الإمكان العام
عدم الاستحالة
الطرف المخالف
القدرة
الإرادة
المستحيلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That general possibility can be applied to God.
Correction: General possibility cannot be applied to God because it would treat necessary existence as merely possible, contradicting God's necessary nature.

Checkpoint
Why is general possibility not correctly applied to God according to the text?
A) Because God's existence is impossible
B) Because it would include necessary beings among possibles
C) Because God's power is limited
D) Because the opposite side is necessary

Key Takeaway
General possibility is the absence of impossibility, but it cannot be attributed to God because His existence is necessary, not merely possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray to God, do you ever subconsciously think of Him as merely 'probably' or 'possibly' there, rather than as the Necessarily Existent? What would change in your prayer if you fully internalized that His existence is necessary and not a possibility?
?
This lesson shows that power and will do not relate to impossibilities, and that does not imply incapability. Is there a situation in your life where you have doubted God's power because something seemed impossible? How does this teaching reshape your view of that situation?
?
The passage warns against including God among possible beings. In your daily life, do you sometimes treat God as if He is contingent—affected by your actions or circumstances? What would it mean to live with the constant awareness that God is absolutely necessary and transcendent?

Refining the Definition of General Possibility
Al-Ajhuri refines the definition of general possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of general possibility as clarified by al-Ajhuri.

Core Idea
General possibility is simply the absence of impossibility.

Al-Ajhuri offers a final refinement to the definition of general possibility (al-imkan al-`amm). He suggests two formulations, ultimately settling on 'absence of impossibility' as capturing its true reality. This clarification resolves a subtle nuance from the earlier discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two formulations al-Ajhuri proposes.
The passage is a marginal note, so it assumes prior context.
2
Compare the two definitions
Identify the first definition: 'negation of necessity from non-existence'. Then the second: 'absence of impossibility'. Ask yourself why al-Ajhuri prefers the second.
Think about which one captures the essence of possibility without extra conditions.
3
Understand the reasoning
Al-Ajhuri says the second definition is the 'reality' (haqiqa) of general possibility. Reflect on why 'absence of impossibility' is more fundamental.
This definition applies to all possible things, whether they exist or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري . فكان الظاهر أن يقال: هو سلب الضرورة عن العدم ليشمل الممكن المذكور، بل كان الظاهر أن يقال: هو عدم الاستحالة؛ لأن ذلك هو حقيقة الإمكان العام.
Marginal note of al-Allamah Ahmad al-Ajhuri: So it would be apparent to say: it is the negation of necessity from non-existence to include the mentioned possible; rather, it would be apparent to say: it is the absence of impossibility, because that is the reality of general possibility.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This is a brief clarifying note from the commentator al-Ajhuri. He states that the definition of general possibility (al-imkan al-`amm) could be expressed as 'the negation of necessity from non-existence' in order to include the possible that has not been judged. However, an even better formulation is 'the absence of impossibility' (`adam al-istihala), because that captures the true reality of general possibility. This note serves as a final refinement of the definition discussed earlier.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام
سلب الضرورة عن العدم
عدم الاستحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think general possibility is best defined as 'negation of necessity from non-existence'.
Correction: Al-Ajhuri clarifies that 'absence of impossibility' is the truer definition, as it captures the reality of general possibility without additional conditions.

Checkpoint

A) The negation of necessity from existence
B) The permissibility of existence
C) The absence of impossibility
D) The necessity of non-existence

Key Takeaway
General possibility is most accurately defined as the absence of impossibility.

Definition of General Possibility and Refutation of the Heretic
Al-Bajuri defines general possibility and uses it to refute a heretic's claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) and how it refutes the heretical claim that God must be able to do all things, including taking a son.

Core Idea
General possibility only negates necessity from the opposing side; it does not make necessary things possible.

This lesson begins by clarifying the precise meaning of general possibility, a key term in Islamic theology. Understanding this term is essential for correctly interpreting statements about God's power. The author then applies this concept to refute a common heretical claim about God's ability to take a son.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Pay attention to the definition of general possibility and how it is used.
Notice the distinction between the agreeing side and opposing side.
2
Identify the definition of general possibility
The passage defines general possibility as 'the negation of necessity from the opposing side only.' Find this line and understand why it is not simply 'negation of necessity from both sides.'
3
See how the definition refutes the heretic
The author argues that power and will do not apply to impossible things, so the heretic's claim that God must be able to take a son is false. This step is the core refutation.
Understand that impossibility does not indicate powerlessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الطرف الموافق لما نطقت به - وهو ثبوتُ الوجود له ـ ليس بواجب، وكذلك الطرفُ المخالِفُ لما نطقت به ـ وهو عدم ثبوته له - لا بالإمكان العام، وهو سَلْبُ الضرورة، بمعنى: الوجوب عن الطرف المخالف فقط، فإذا قلت: الله موجود بالإمكان العام كان المعنى أن الطرف المخالف ـ وهو عدم ثبوت الوجود له تعالى - ليس بواجب. وأما الطرف الموافق؛ فهو واجب له هنا، وإنما لم يصح إرادة الإمكان العام هنا؛ لدخول الواجبات في الممكنات حينئذ، مع أنَّ كُلّاً أنَّ كُلّاً من القدرة والإرادة لا يتعلق بها كما لا يتعلق بالمستحيلات، ولا يلزم من عدم تعلق القدرة بهما عَجْز؛ لأنهما ليسا من وظيفتها، ولأنها لو تعلَّقَتْ بهما؛ لَزِمَ الفسادُ؛ إذ يلزم عليه تعلقها بإعدام الذات العلية، وبسَلْب الألوهية عنها، ونحو ذلك.
وبهذا يُعْلَمُ سقوط قول بعض المبتدعة : إن الله قادِرٌ على أن يتَّخِذَ ولداً؛ إذ لو لم يَقْدِرُ عليه لكان عاجزاً، وكأنه أَخَذَه من قصة إدريس مع إبليس، وهي أن إدريس كان يَخِيطُ حُلَّةً وهو يقول في دخول الإبرة وخروجها: سبحان الله والحمد لله، فجاءه إبليس في صورة إنسان بقشرة بيضة - وقيل بقشرة فستقة - وقال: هل الله يَقْدِرُ أن يجعل الدنيا في هذه القشرة؟ فقال: الله يَقْدِرُ أن يجعل الدنيا في سَم هذه الإبرة - أي: خَرْقِها ، ونَخَسَ إحدى عينيه، فصار أعور - قال بعضهم وأرجو أن تكون اليمنى ، واختار نخس إحدى عينيه ليُطْفِئَ نور بصره كما أراد أن يطفئ نور الإيمان، فإن الجزاء من جنس العمل. ووجه الأخذ أنه تُوُهِمَ أن مرادَ إدريس أن الله يَقْدِرُ أن يجعل الدنيا بهيئتها التي هي عليها في القشرة المذكورة بهيئتها التي هي عليها، مع أن هذا مستحيل؛ لاستحالة اجتماع الأجسام الكثيفة في حَيّز واحد، وليس هذا مراداً. بل المراد: أن الله يصغر الدنيا جداً أو يُكَتِرُ القشرة كذلك، ويجعل هذه في هذه، وليس بمستحيل، وإنما لم يُصَرّح له إدريس بذلك؛ لأنه سائل مُتَعَنِّت قبحه الله. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري .
فكان الظاهر أن يقال: هو سلب الضرورة عن العدم ليشمل الممكن المذكور، بل كان الظاهر أن يقال: هو عدم الاستحالة؛ لأن ذلك هو حقيقة الإمكان العام.
That the unanimous side - which is the affirmation of existence for Him - is not necessary, and likewise the opposing side - which is the non-affirmation of existence for Him - is not by general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm), which is the negation of necessity (salb al-darurah), meaning the removal of obligation from the opposing side only. So when you say: God exists by general possibility, the meaning is that the opposing side - which is the non-affirmation of existence for Him, exalted - is not necessary. As for the agreeing side, it is necessary for Him here. And general possibility is not intended here because it would include necessary things in possible things at that time, even though each of power (qudrah) and will (iradah) does not apply to them just as they do not apply to impossible things. And it does not follow from the non-application of power to them that there is incapability ('ajz), because they are not among its functions, and because if power applied to them, corruption would necessarily follow, as it would necessitate its application to the annihilation of the High Essence and to the negation of divinity from it, and so on.
And by this is known the fall of the statement of some heretics: 'Allah is able to take a son,' because if He were not able to do it, He would be incapable. And it seems he took this from the story of Idris with Iblis, which is that Idris was sewing a garment and saying upon the entering and exiting of the needle: 'Subhan Allah wa al-hamdu lillah,' so Iblis came to him in the form of a man with an eggshell - and it is said with a pistachio shell - and said: 'Is Allah able to put the world in this shell?' He said: 'Allah is able to put the world in the eye of this needle' - meaning its hole - and he stabbed one of his eyes, so he became one-eyed - some said: I hope it is the right eye - and he chose to stab one of his eyes to extinguish the light of his sight just as he wanted to extinguish the light of faith, for the recompense is of the same kind as the deed. And the point of taking this story is that it was imagined that Idris meant that Allah is able to put the world in the mentioned shell in its current state, while this is impossible due to the impossibility of the union of dense bodies in one place, and this is not the intended meaning. Rather, the intended meaning is that Allah greatly shrinks the world or enlarges the shell likewise, and places this in this, and that is not impossible. And Idris did not clarify this to him because he was a stubborn questioner, may Allah curse him. Commentary of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri.
So it was apparent that one should say: It is the negation of necessity from non-existence to include the mentioned possible, rather it was apparent that one should say: It is the absence of impossibility, because that is the reality of general possibility.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a heretical claim about divine power. It begins by clarifying the concept of general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm), which is defined as the negation of necessity from the opposing side only. In the case of God's existence, the agreeing side (existence) is necessary, while the opposing side (non-existence) is not necessary. The author explains that general possibility does not apply here because it would wrongly include necessary things among possible things. He then argues that power (qudrah) and will (iradah) do not apply to impossible things, and if they did, it would lead to absurdities like the annihilation of God's essence. From this, he refutes the heretic's claim that God must be able to take a son, or else He would be powerless. Taking a son is an impossibility, so it does not indicate powerlessness. To illustrate the heretic's misunderstanding, the author narrates the story of Idris and Iblis, where Iblis asked a trick question about putting the world in an eggshell, and Idris's apparent response was actually a different interpretation. The author notes that the heretic misunderstood Idris's meaning, showing that the heretic is a stubborn questioner. Finally, the author clarifies that the correct definition of general possibility is either the negation of necessity from non-existence or, more properly, the absence of impossibility (adam al-istihalah). This guards against the misconception that divine power must extend to all things without exception.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام
الضرورة
الوجوب
القدرة
الإرادة
المستحيلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God cannot do something (like taking a son), He must be powerless.
Correction: This passage shows that certain things are impossible by nature and thus outside the scope of power; inability to do the impossible is not a defect.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm)?
A) Negation of necessity from both sides.
B) Negation of necessity from the agreeing side only.
C) Affirmation of possibility for necessary things.
D) Negation of necessity from the opposing side only.

Key Takeaway
General possibility does not apply to God's existence because existence is necessary for Him; it only negates necessity from non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God was powerless because He didn't answer a prayer in the way you expected? How does the distinction between impossibility and incapacity in this lesson challenge that feeling, and what must you change in your heart when facing disappointment?
?
When you hear someone say 'God can do anything' without qualification, do you immediately think of logical impossibilities as exceptions? This lesson shows that power does not apply to impossibilities. How will you now guard your speech about God's power to avoid the heretic's error?
?
If you truly believe that God's power and will only apply to what is possible in themselves, how should that affect your reliance on Him when you face what seems impossible? What specific action will you take this week to trust in God's wisdom rather than demand a particular outcome?
