The Story of Idris and the Correct Definition of General Possibility
Al-Bajuri tells the story of Idris and Iblis to correct a misunderstanding and then gives the final definition of general possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the story of Idris and Iblis and how it illustrates the correct understanding of general possibility as the absence of impossibility.

Core Idea
The story of Idris shows that impossible things are not within the scope of divine power, and the true definition of general possibility is the absence of impossibility.

This lesson narrates the well-known story of Idris and Iblis, which some heretics misuse to argue for unlimited divine power. The author explains the correct interpretation and uses it to refine the definition of general possibility, concluding that it is essentially the absence of impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of Idris and Iblis
Read the story carefully in Arabic and translation. Note the details: Idris was sewing, Iblis came with a shell and asked a tricky question.
The story is meant to illustrate a misunderstanding about divine power.
2
Understand the heretic's misinterpretation
The heretic claimed that Idris said God can put the world in a shell, implying God can do impossible things. The author explains that Idris meant something else: God can shrink the world or enlarge the shell.
The heretic is described as a stubborn questioner.
3
Learn the final definition of general possibility
The passage concludes with the correct definition: general possibility is the absence of impossibility (adam al-istihalah). This is the key takeaway.
This definition avoids the confusion about necessary things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الطرف الموافق لما نطقت به - وهو ثبوتُ الوجود له ـ ليس بواجب، وكذلك الطرفُ المخالِفُ لما نطقت به ـ وهو عدم ثبوته له - لا بالإمكان العام، وهو سَلْبُ الضرورة، بمعنى: الوجوب عن الطرف المخالف فقط، فإذا قلت: الله موجود بالإمكان العام كان المعنى أن الطرف المخالف ـ وهو عدم ثبوت الوجود له تعالى - ليس بواجب. وأما الطرف الموافق؛ فهو واجب له هنا، وإنما لم يصح إرادة الإمكان العام هنا؛ لدخول الواجبات في الممكنات حينئذ، مع أنَّ كُلّاً أنَّ كُلّاً من القدرة والإرادة لا يتعلق بها كما لا يتعلق بالمستحيلات، ولا يلزم من عدم تعلق القدرة بهما عَجْز؛ لأنهما ليسا من وظيفتها، ولأنها لو تعلَّقَتْ بهما؛ لَزِمَ الفسادُ؛ إذ يلزم عليه تعلقها بإعدام الذات العلية، وبسَلْب الألوهية عنها، ونحو ذلك.
وبهذا يُعْلَمُ سقوط قول بعض المبتدعة : إن الله قادِرٌ على أن يتَّخِذَ ولداً؛ إذ لو لم يَقْدِرُ عليه لكان عاجزاً، وكأنه أَخَذَه من قصة إدريس مع إبليس، وهي أن إدريس كان يَخِيطُ حُلَّةً وهو يقول في دخول الإبرة وخروجها: سبحان الله والحمد لله، فجاءه إبليس في صورة إنسان بقشرة بيضة - وقيل بقشرة فستقة - وقال: هل الله يَقْدِرُ أن يجعل الدنيا في هذه القشرة؟ فقال: الله يَقْدِرُ أن يجعل الدنيا في سَم هذه الإبرة - أي: خَرْقِها ، ونَخَسَ إحدى عينيه، فصار أعور - قال بعضهم وأرجو أن تكون اليمنى ، واختار نخس إحدى عينيه ليُطْفِئَ نور بصره كما أراد أن يطفئ نور الإيمان، فإن الجزاء من جنس العمل. ووجه الأخذ أنه تُوُهِمَ أن مرادَ إدريس أن الله يَقْدِرُ أن يجعل الدنيا بهيئتها التي هي عليها في القشرة المذكورة بهيئتها التي هي عليها، مع أن هذا مستحيل؛ لاستحالة اجتماع الأجسام الكثيفة في حَيّز واحد، وليس هذا مراداً. بل المراد: أن الله يصغر الدنيا جداً أو يُكَتِرُ القشرة كذلك، ويجعل هذه في هذه، وليس بمستحيل، وإنما لم يُصَرّح له إدريس بذلك؛ لأنه سائل مُتَعَنِّت قبحه الله. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري .
فكان الظاهر أن يقال: هو سلب الضرورة عن العدم ليشمل الممكن المذكور، بل كان الظاهر أن يقال: هو عدم الاستحالة؛ لأن ذلك هو حقيقة الإمكان العام.
That the unanimous side - which is the affirmation of existence for Him - is not necessary, and likewise the opposing side - which is the non-affirmation of existence for Him - is not by general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm), which is the negation of necessity (salb al-darurah), meaning the removal of obligation from the opposing side only. So when you say: God exists by general possibility, the meaning is that the opposing side - which is the non-affirmation of existence for Him, exalted - is not necessary. As for the agreeing side, it is necessary for Him here. And general possibility is not intended here because it would include necessary things in possible things at that time, even though each of power (qudrah) and will (iradah) does not apply to them just as they do not apply to impossible things. And it does not follow from the non-application of power to them that there is incapability ('ajz), because they are not among its functions, and because if power applied to them, corruption would necessarily follow, as it would necessitate its application to the annihilation of the High Essence and to the negation of divinity from it, and so on.
And by this is known the fall of the statement of some heretics: 'Allah is able to take a son,' because if He were not able to do it, He would be incapable. And it seems he took this from the story of Idris with Iblis, which is that Idris was sewing a garment and saying upon the entering and exiting of the needle: 'Subhan Allah wa al-hamdu lillah,' so Iblis came to him in the form of a man with an eggshell - and it is said with a pistachio shell - and said: 'Is Allah able to put the world in this shell?' He said: 'Allah is able to put the world in the eye of this needle' - meaning its hole - and he stabbed one of his eyes, so he became one-eyed - some said: I hope it is the right eye - and he chose to stab one of his eyes to extinguish the light of his sight just as he wanted to extinguish the light of faith, for the recompense is of the same kind as the deed. And the point of taking this story is that it was imagined that Idris meant that Allah is able to put the world in the mentioned shell in its current state, while this is impossible due to the impossibility of the union of dense bodies in one place, and this is not the intended meaning. Rather, the intended meaning is that Allah greatly shrinks the world or enlarges the shell likewise, and places this in this, and that is not impossible. And Idris did not clarify this to him because he was a stubborn questioner, may Allah curse him. Commentary of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri.
So it was apparent that one should say: It is the negation of necessity from non-existence to include the mentioned possible, rather it was apparent that one should say: It is the absence of impossibility, because that is the reality of general possibility.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a heretical claim about divine power. It begins by clarifying the concept of general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm), which is defined as the negation of necessity from the opposing side only. This guards against the misconception that divine power must extend to all things without exception.

The Logical Opposition Between Qidam and Huduth and the Two Meanings of Huduth
Al-Bajuri explains the logical opposition between qidam and huduth and distinguishes two meanings of huduth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the logical relation between qidam (eternity) and huduth (temporal origination) depends on whether huduth is interpreted in its metaphorical sense (renewal after non-existence) or its genuine sense (existence after non-existence), and how the same analysis applies to baqa' (permanence) and fana' (annihilation).

Core Idea
The logical relation between qidam and huduth shifts between equivalence and specificity depending on the meaning of huduth.

This passage from Al-Bajuri's commentary on Umm al-Barahin dissects the logical relationship between two foundational kalam terms: qidam (eternity) and huduth (temporal origination). Al-Bajuri shows that the nature of their opposition is not fixed but depends on which meaning of huduth is intended. By carefully distinguishing these meanings, we avoid confusion in theological arguments. The passage also applies the same logical framework to the pair baqa' (permanence) and fana' (annihilation).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the full English translation. Notice the technical vocabulary and the structure of the argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'مساوي' (equivalent) and 'أخص' (more specific).
2
Identify the two meanings of huduth
Al-Bajuri distinguishes between the metaphorical sense of huduth (renewal after non-existence) and the genuine sense (existence after non-existence). Find where each is introduced and how they affect the logical relation with qidam.
3
Understand the two types of opposition
The passage describes two types of logical opposition: 'between a thing and the equivalent of its negation' and 'between a thing and the more specific of its negation'. Work through how each applies depending on the sense of huduth.
The negation of qidam is 'lā qidam' (not-eternity). The question is whether huduth is coextensive with 'lā qidam' or a subset of it.
4
Apply the same analysis to fana' and baqa'
Notice that the same reasoning is used for the pair fana' (annihilation) and baqa' (permanence). Verify that the opposition is of the 'equivalent' type, and understand why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْحُدُوثُ) معطوف على (العدم)، والتقابل بينه وبين القدم من التقابل بين الشيء والمساوي لنقيضه؛ لأن نقيض القدم لا قدم، وهو عين الحدوث؛ لأنه لا واسِطَةً بينهما. هذا إن فُسّر الحدوث بمعناه المجازي: وهو التجدُّدُ بعد عَدَمٍ، وأما إن فُسِّرَ بمعناه الحقيقي وهو الوجود بعد عَدَمٍ؛ فالتقابل بينهما من التقابل بين الشيء والأخص من نقيضه؛ لأن نقيض القِدَم: لا قِدَمَ كما علمتَ، وهو يشمل الحدوث بالمعنى المذكور والتجدد بعد عدم، فعلى هذا؛ الحدوث أخصُّ من لا قِدَم الذي هو نقيضُ القدم. (قوله: وَطُرُقُ الْعَدَمِ) أي حصوله بعد أن لم يكن، وهو الفناء، والتقابل بينه وبين البقاء من التقابل بين الشيء والمساوي لنقيضه؛ لأن نقيض البقاء: لا بَقَاءَ، وهو عين طرق العدم الذي هو الفناء. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَهُوَ عَيْنُ الْحُدُوثِ المراد: أنه مساءٍ له وليس المراد: أنهما متحدان حقيقة، بل حقيقة انتفاء القدم غير حقيقة الحدوث، سواء كان الحدوث بمعنى التجدد بعد العدم، أو بمعنى الوجود بعد العدم، والمراد بتساويهما أنه كلما تحقق أحدهما تحقق الآخر وإن تباينا حقيقة وهكذا يقال في كل متساويين. (قوله: لَا وَاسِطَةَ بَيْنَهُمَا :أي بين القدم والحدوث، والحدوث بمعنى التجدد بعد العدم، وعلى تقدير الواسطة تكون محققة في القدم ) ، فيكون أعم من الحدوث، وهو التجدد بعد العدم، أي: الحصول بعد العدم، وليس المراد بالتجدد هنا: التكرر وإن كان يطلق عليه أيضاً. (قوله: وَهُوَ التَّجَدُدُ بَعْدَ عَدَمٍ) أي: الحصول بعد العدم، وليس المراد بالتجدد هنا: التكرر وإن كان يطلق على ذلك أيضاً كما مر. (قوله: وَالتَّجَدُّدُ بَعْدَ عَدَمٍ) أي: المتحقق في الأحوال الحادثة والاعتبارات الحادثة. (قوله: وَهُوَ عَيْنُ طُرُقِ الْعَدَمِ) أي: مساءٍ له لا متحد معه بحيث تكون حقيقتهما واحدة كما تقدم التنبيه عليه.
His saying: 'and temporal origination (al-ḥudūth)' is coordinated with 'non-existence (al-ʿadam)'. The opposition between it and eternity (al-qidam) is of the type of opposition between a thing and the equivalent of its negation, because the negation of eternity is 'not eternity', and it is identical to temporal origination, since there is no intermediary between them. This is if temporal origination is interpreted in its metaphorical sense: renewal after non-existence. However, if it is interpreted in its genuine sense, which is existence after non-existence, then the opposition between them is of the type of opposition between a thing and the more specific of its negation, because the negation of eternity is 'not eternity', as you know, and it includes temporal origination in the mentioned sense and renewal after non-existence. So on this, temporal origination is more specific than 'not eternity', which is the negation of eternity. His saying: 'and the ways of non-existence' means its occurrence after it was not, which is annihilation (al-fanāʾ). The opposition between it and permanence (al-baqāʾ) is of the type of opposition between a thing and the equivalent of its negation, because the negation of permanence is 'not permanence', and it is identical to the ways of non-existence, which is annihilation. Commentary of al-ʿAllāma Aḥmad al-Ujhūrī: His saying: 'and it is identical to temporal origination'—the intention is that it is equivalent to it, not that they are identical in reality. Rather, the reality of the negation of eternity is not the reality of temporal origination, whether temporal origination means renewal after non-existence or existence after non-existence. The intention by their equivalence is that whenever one of them is realized, the other is realized, even if they are distinct in reality. And so it is said regarding every pair of equivalents. His saying: 'there is no intermediary between them' means between eternity and temporal origination—and temporal origination is in the sense of renewal after non-existence. And on the assumption of an intermediary, it would be included in eternity, so it would be more general than temporal origination, which is renewal after non-existence, i.e., occurrence after non-existence. And renewal here does not mean repetition, although it is also applied to that. His saying: 'and it is renewal after non-existence' means occurrence after non-existence, and renewal here does not mean repetition, although it is also applied to that, as previously mentioned. His saying: 'and renewal after non-existence' means that which is realized in temporal states and temporal considerations. His saying: 'and it is identical to the ways of non-existence' means it is equivalent to it, not identical with it such that their realities are one, as previously indicated.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the logical opposition between qidam (eternity) and huduth (temporal origination) and their negations. It begins by stating that the opposition between huduth and qidam, when huduth is taken metaphorically as renewal after non-existence, is of the type 'between a thing and the equivalent of its negation' because the negation of qidam (lā qidam) is exactly huduth in that sense, with no intermediary. But if huduth is taken genuinely as existence after non-existence, then the opposition is 'between a thing and the more specific of its negation' because lā qidam includes both this genuine huduth and the metaphorical renewal; thus huduth is more specific. The same logic is applied to fana' (annihilation) and baqa' (permanence). Then al-Ajhuri's commentary clarifies that the claim 'it is identical' means equivalence in extension, not identity in reality. He explains that the reality of the negation of qidam is not the reality of huduth, whether huduth is genuine or metaphorical. He also notes that 'no intermediary' means between qidam and huduth in the metaphorical sense, and that if there were an intermediary, it would be included in qidam, making qidam more general. He further clarifies that 'renewal' here does not mean repetition. The passage enforces precise use of terms in kalam reasoning.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
حدوث (huduth)
عدم (ʿadam)
تجدد (tajaddud)
فناء (fanāʾ)
بقاء (baqāʾ)
مساوي (musāwī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that huduth and qidam are simple opposites in all contexts.
Correction: Al-Bajuri shows that the logical relation depends on which meaning of huduth is used: it can be either equivalent to the negation of qidam or a subset of it.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, when huduth is taken in its genuine sense (الوجود بعد العدم), what is the logical relation between qidam and huduth?
A) Equivalence (التقابل بين الشيء والمساوي لنقيضه)
B) Opposition of thing and its more specific negation (التقابل بين الشيء والأخص من نقيضه)
C) Contradiction
D) No relation

Key Takeaway
The logical relation between qidam and huduth is not fixed; it varies based on whether huduth means renewal after non-existence or existence after non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read theological texts, do you sometimes assume that a single Arabic term has only one meaning? This lesson shows that huduth has two distinct meanings that change logical relations. What other Islamic terms might you be misunderstanding because you haven't asked about the precise intended meaning? What specific step will you take this week to verify meanings in a source you are studying?
?
In this lesson, al-Ajhuri warns against mistaking equivalence in extension (musāwāt) for identity in reality (ittiḥād). Have you ever conflated two concepts that always occur together with being the same thing—perhaps in your understanding of God's attributes or actions? How can this precision protect you from theological oversimplification? Commit to investigating one such pair of attributes this month.

The Ten Types of Similarity to Temporal Things
Al-Bajuri enumerates the ten types of similarity to temporal things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ten specific categories of similarity to temporal things that must be negated from Allah in order to affirm divine transcendence (tanzih).

Core Idea
There are ten ways something could resemble creation; all must be denied of Allah.

Al-Bajuri, commenting on al-Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin, presents a complete enumeration of the ways in which something might resemble created, temporal things. This classification is not merely descriptive but serves as a tool for negation: each category represents an attribute that must be removed from our conception of Allah. Understanding this list is essential for properly affirming Allah's absolute uniqueness and transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the rhythmic listing of ten items introduced by 'الأول', 'الثاني', etc.
The passage ends with 'فتدبر' (so reflect), inviting deeper contemplation.
2
Identify the ten categories
Go through each of the ten items. For each, understand what aspect of temporality it refers to—whether it's being a body, an accident, being in a direction, etc.
These categories are not arbitrary; they cover all major ways created beings are characterized.
3
Understand the theological purpose
Recognize that each category is a potential point of similarity between Allah and creation. The purpose of listing them is to negate each one from Allah, establishing that He is unlike anything in creation.
This negation is central to the Islamic concept of tanzih (transcendence).
4
Reflect on the list's completeness
Consider why al-Sanusi chose exactly these ten. Reflect on how they cover spatial, temporal, and qualitative dimensions. The final item about 'purposes' is particularly profound.
'فتدبر' encourages the student to ponder the thoroughness of this categorization.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the basic fact of the enumeration. Then try to explain to yourself why each category must be negated.
Mastering this list is foundational for later discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنَّ أنواع المماثلة عَشَرَةُ: الأول: أن يكون جزماً، الثاني: أن يكون عَرَضاً يقوم بالجزم، والثالث: أن يكون في جهةٍ، الرابع: أن يكون له هو جهةً، الخامس أن يكون في مكان السادس أن يكون في زمان، السابع: أن يكون مَحَلّاً للحوادث، الثامن: أن يكون متّصفاً بالصَّغَر التاسع أن يكون متصفاً بالكبر، العاشر : أن يكون متصفاً بالأغراض في الأفعال أو الأحكام وقد ذكرها المصنف على هذا الترتيب. فتدبر.
Know that the types of similarity are ten: First: that it be a jirm (body occupying space). Second: that it be an accident subsisting in a jirm. Third: that it be in a direction. Fourth: that it have direction [as a property]. Fifth: that it be in a place. Sixth: that it be in a time. Seventh: that it be a locus for temporal things. Eighth: that it be characterized by smallness. Ninth: that it be characterized by greatness. Tenth: that it be characterized by purposes in actions or judgments. The author (al-Sanusi) has mentioned them in this order. So reflect.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a classification — an enumeration of the ten types of similarity (mumathala) to temporal things (hawadith). These are the ways in which something could resemble creation, all of which must be negated from Allah. The author (al-Sanusi, via al-Bajuri's commentary) lists them systematically: (1) being a jirm (a body that fills space), (2) being an accident (a quality that inheres in a body), (3) being in a direction (jiha), (4) having direction as an attribute of itself, (5) being in a place, (6) being in a time, (7) being a substrate for temporal occurrences, (8) being characterized by smallness, (9) by greatness, (10) by having purposes in actions or judgments. The list follows the order in the original text, and the reader is told to 'reflect' (tadabbur) because each item implies a negation of a creaturely attribute from Allah. The commentary frames this as a complete framework for tanzih (divine transcendence).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the list is merely descriptive of creatures, but it is actually a tool for negating each category from Allah.
Correction: The passage is not describing what created things are like; it is providing a systematic list of attributes that must be denied of Allah to affirm His transcendence.

B) 10

?
The author commands 'so reflect'. When was the last time you truly reflected on the absolute incomparability of Allah? How does that reflection change how you conceive of Him in your daily prayers?
?
If you truly internalize that Allah is not limited by time, space, or purpose, how would that reshape your trust in His decree and your patience in trials?

Hadith Proof and Defining Body and Accident
Al-Bajuri cites a hadith and defines 'jirm' and 'arad' to negate them from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith 'Reflect on everything except Allah's essence' serves as evidence for negating body and accident from Allah, and how al-Bajuri defines 'imaginary void', 'body', and 'accident' in this context.

Core Idea
The hadith forbids thinking about Allah's essence, and al-Bajuri uses definitions to clarify what is negated.

Al-Bajuri begins this section with a hadith that commands reflection on everything except Allah's essence, establishing that Allah is beyond human conceptualization. He then explains two key terms—'imaginary void' and 'accident'—to clarify what is being negated when we say Allah is not a body or accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its implication
Read the Arabic hadith: 'Reflect on everything, and do not reflect on the essence of Allah.' Understand that this forbids conceptualization of Allah's essence, indicating His transcendence.
The hadith is narrated by Ibn Hajar.
2
Understand the explanation of 'void'
Al-Bajuri explains that 'void' (farāgh) is only imaginary; the space between heaven and earth is actually filled with air. This clarifies that when we negate body from Allah, we are not dealing with physical spaces.
The term 'imaginary void' (farāgh mawhūm) is used.
3
Learn the definition of 'accident'
Al-Bajuri defines 'accident' (ʿaraḍ) as an originated attribute that subsists in something else. It is more specific than 'attribute' because eternal attributes are not accidents.
This helps negate accidents from Allah.
4
Connect the definitions to the hadith
The hadith forbids thinking of Allah in terms of bodies or accidents. The definitions show exactly what bodies and accidents are, making it clear that Allah is free from them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل للأول ما رواه ابن حجر «تفكروا في كل شيء، ولا تتفكروا في ذات الله تعالى». (قوله: قدراً من الفراغ) أي: مقداراً من الفراغ، وهو ما بين السماء والأرض، وتسميته فراغاً إنما هو بحسب الوهم، ولذلك يسمى فراغاً موهوماً، وإلا؛ فهو مملوء بالهواء غاية الأمر أن الهواء جسم لطيف يتداخل بعضه في بعض إذا حل جسم آخر في مكانه. (قوله: أو يكون عرضاً) معطوف على قوله: (يكون جرماً) والعرض ما قام بغيره من الصفات الحادثة، فهو أخص من مطلق الصفة لانفرادها في الصفة القديمة.
And it indicates for the first what Ibn Hajar narrated: 'Reflect on everything, and do not reflect on the essence of Allah the Exalted.' (His saying: 'a quantity of void') i.e., a measure of void, and it is what is between the heaven and the earth, and it is called 'void' only according to imagination (wahm), therefore it is called 'imaginary void' (farāgh mawhūm). Otherwise, it is filled with air; the matter is that air is a subtle body which interpenetrates itself when another body occupies its place. (His saying: 'or be an accident') is conjoined to his saying: 'be a body'. And the accident (ʿaraḍ) is that which subsists in other than itself of originated attributes (ṣifāt ḥāditha); thus it is more specific than absolute attribute (muṭlaq al-ṣifa) because of its exclusivity to originated attributes as opposed to the eternal attribute (al-ṣifa al-qadīma).
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from a hadith and defines two key terms. Al-Bajuri begins by citing the hadith narrated by Ibn Hajar (probably Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani) which commands reflection on everything except the essence of Allah, indicating that the essence is beyond human comprehension and cannot be likened to created things. Then he explains the phrase 'a quantity of void' (qadran min al-farāgh) which appears in the original text (likely from the matn). He clarifies that this 'void' is not absolute emptiness; it is an imaginary concept referring to the space between heaven and earth, which is actually filled with air. The air is a subtle body that can be displaced. Then he discusses the disjunction 'or be an accident' (aw yakūnu ʿaraḍan), explaining that 'accident' in kalam terminology is an attribute that subsists in a substrate (such as color or motion), and it is more specific than the general term 'attribute' (ṣifa) because 'attribute' can also apply to eternal attributes of Allah, while 'accident' applies only to created attributes. This serves to negate both body (jirm) and accident from Allah, as the hadith warns against thinking of His essence in such terms.

Key Terms
الفراغ الموهوم
الجرم
العرض
الصفة الحادثة
الصفة القديمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'void' means absolute emptiness or nothingness.
Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that the 'void' is imaginary; the space is actually filled with air, a subtle body.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Bajuri say the 'void' (al-farāgh) is called 'imaginary'?
A) Because it does not exist at all.
B) Because the space between heaven and earth is actually filled with air.
C) Because the term is used in the Quran.
D) Because it refers to the essence of Allah.

Key Takeaway
The hadith and definitions together establish that Allah is not a body or accident, reinforcing His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you ever catch yourself imagining a 'how' or 'where'? How does this hadith command you to discipline that tendency starting today?
?
The void between heaven and earth is actually filled with air. What areas of your faith do you assume are 'empty' or irrelevant, when in fact they are filled with meaning and divine presence?
?
If all created things are either bodies or accidents, and Allah is neither, what does that mean for how you relate to Him in worship? Will you let this truth deepen your awe and silence your presumption?

Is Direction Specific to Humans?
Al-Bajuri presents two opinions on the scope of direction (jiha) and gives the verification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scholars disagreed over whether 'direction' (jiha) can be attributed to non-human objects, and how the debate affects the interpretation of common phrases like 'to the right of the minbar'.

Core Idea
Direction is not specific to humans, but literal use is debated among scholars.

When we say a minbar is to our right, do we mean direction literally or figuratively? This lesson explores a classical kalam debate about the scope of direction (jiha). Al-Bajuri presents two positions: that jiha is exclusive to humans and requires a hidden genitive, or that it applies to all objects literally. We will see how this links to divine transcendence and how the 'verified opinion' settles the matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on jiha
Read the first part of the source where al-Bajuri presents the disagreement and the verification. Try to identify the two opinions.
The passage uses grammatical terms like 'حذف مضاف' and 'تقدير'.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find the statement of the first opinion (قيل: الجهة مختصة...) and the verification (والتحقيق: أنها ليست مختصة...). Understand how each opinion interprets phrases like 'to the right of the minbar'.
3
Understand the grammatical ellipsis argument
The first opinion claims an omitted genitive construct (مضاف) is implied. The verification argues for a literal reading without ellipsis.
This is a linguistic argument, not a theological one, but it has implications for divine transcendence.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Reflect on why this debate matters: if direction were specific to humans, then saying Allah is 'above' might be metaphorical. The verification keeps the literal sense, but Allah is above all in a way that does not imply spatial location.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف فقيل: الجهة مختصة بالنوع الإنساني دون غيره ولو حيواناً، فلا تضاف الجهة إليه لا بواسطة الإنسان، وعلى هذا يكون قولهم: عن يمين المنبر مثلاً على حذف مضاف، والتقدير: عن يمين ملاصق المنبر أو نحو ذلك، والتحقيق: أنها ليست مختصة به، بل تضاف له ولغيره، وعلى هذا فيكون قولهم: عن يمين المنبر مثلاً على ظاهره. (قوله: أو يتقيد بمكان) المراد من تقيده بمكان: حلوله فيه، لا اختصاصه به دون غيره؛ وإن كان هو المتبادر من لفظ التقيد. والمكان عند أهل السنة: هو الفراغ الموهوم، وحينئذ يكون قوله: (أو يتقيد... إلخ) مستغنى عنه بقوله: بأن يكون جرماً أي تأخذ ذاته العلية قدراً من الفراغ. وعند جمهور الفلاسفة هو السطح الباطن من الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوي كباطن الكوز المماس لظاهر الماء، وعلى هذا لا يكون قوله: (أو يتقيد... إلخ) مستغنى عنه بما ذكر.
And there is disagreement: some said that direction (jiha) is specific to the human species (al-nawʿ al-insānī) to the exclusion of others, even animals. So direction is not attributed to it except by means of a human. On this basis, their saying 'to the right of the minbar' for example is on the deletion of a genitive construct (mudāf), and the estimation is 'to the right of what is adjacent to the minbar' or the like. And the verified (al-taḥqīq) is that it is not specific to him, rather it is attributed to him and to others. On this basis, their saying 'to the right of the minbar' for example is taken literally (ʿalā ẓāhirihi). (His saying: 'or be confined to a place (makān)') – the intended meaning of His being confined to a place is His indwelling (ḥulūl) in it, not His being specific (ikhtiṣāṣ) to it to the exclusion of others, even if that is the immediate understanding from the word 'confined' (taqayyud). And place (makān) according to Ahl al-Sunna is imaginary void (al-farāgh al-mawhūm). In that case, his saying: 'or be confined... etc.' is rendered unnecessary by his saying: 'be a body', i.e., that His lofty essence takes a quantity of void. And according to the majority of philosophers (jumhūr al-falāsifa), it is the inner surface of the container (al-ḥāwī) that is contiguous with the outer surface of the contained (al-maḥwī), like the inner surface of a cup that is contiguous with the outer surface of the water. On this basis, his saying: 'or be confined... etc.' is not rendered unnecessary by what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage discusses two advanced topics: the applicability of direction (jiha) to non-human entities, and the definition of 'place' (makān). First, al-Bajuri reports a disagreement: some scholars hold that 'direction' (like right, left) is only literally applicable to humans, and when we say 'to the right of the minbar' it involves an ellipsis meaning 'to the right of something adjacent to the minbar'. The 'verified' opinion (al-taḥqīq) is that direction is not unique to humans and can be used literally for any object. Then he turns to the expression 'or be confined to a place' (aw yataqayyada bi-makān). He explains that 'confinement' here means indwelling (ḥulūl) in a place, not mere specificity. He notes the immediate understanding of 'taqayyud' is specificity, but the intended meaning is actual occupancy. He then gives two definitions of 'place': (1) According to Ahl al-Sunna (Ashʿaris), place is 'imaginary void' (al-farāgh al-mawhūm) – the space that a body occupies. If that is the definition, then the statement 'or be confined to a place' is redundant after the negation of being a body, because a body by definition takes up a volume of void. (2) According to most philosophers, place is the inner surface of the container that touches the outer surface of the contained (like the cup touching the water). On that definition, the statement 'or be confined to a place' is not redundant, because a body might not be in contact with a container. This shows that the matn (Sanusi) includes the negation of being confined to a place to cover both definitions, ensuring that Allah is not in a place in either sense.

Key Terms
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Direction (jiha) can never be used literally for non-human objects.
Correction: The verified opinion in this passage confirms that jiha can be used literally for non-human objects as well.

Checkpoint
According to the verified opinion (al-taḥqīq), what is the correct understanding of 'to the right of the minbar'?
A) It requires an implied genitive construct.
B) It is taken literally without ellipsis.
C) It only applies to humans.
D) It means 'to the right of the imam'.

Key Takeaway
The verified opinion is that direction (jiha) is not specific to humans and can be used literally for any object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the phrase 'to the right of the minbar' in the source, you saw two interpretations. Which one do you personally find more convincing, and why? This decision reflects how you approach language in general – do you prefer literal readings or are you comfortable with implied meanings? How does your choice affect your trust in the clarity of religious texts?
?
This lesson presents a linguistic disagreement that might seem trivial. But if you are someone who prays facing the qibla, or refers to the 'right side' of the mosque, do you ever wonder whether your words are exact or approximate? After seeing Al-Bajuri's verification, will you change how you speak about direction? What would it mean for you to take all such phrases literally?
?
The idea that direction might be specific to humans reveals an assumption that human language is anthropocentric. Does it trouble you that your everyday speech may be inherently centered on humans? How does this lesson challenge you to think about the relationship between language and reality? Are you prepared to refine your vocabulary to be more precise, even in casual talk?

Defining Place: Ahl al-Sunna vs Philosophers
Al-Bajuri defines 'place' according to Ahl al-Sunna and philosophers, and explains the redundancy or necessity of the matn's negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of 'place' (makān) — as imaginary void (Ahl al-Sunna) and as inner surface of container (philosophers) — and why the matn negates confinement to a place to cover both.

Core Idea
Place is defined differently by theologians and philosophers, but both are negated of Allah.

In this lesson, al-Bajuri explains what it means for something to be 'confined to a place' (yataqayyad bi-makān). He clarifies that the intention here is indwelling (hulul), not mere specificity. Then he presents two competing definitions of place: the Ash'ari definition as imaginary void and the philosophical definition as the inner surface of a container. Understanding both is crucial for grasping why Sanusi includes the negation of being confined to a place in the list of attributes impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on place
Read the second part of the source from 'أو يتقيد بمكان' to the end. Note the two definitions given for 'place'.
The passage uses technical terms like 'الفراغ الموهوم' and 'السطح الباطن من الحاوي'.
2
Understand the intended meaning of 'confinement'
Al-Bajuri clarifies that 'confined to a place' means 'indwelling in it' (hulul), not 'being specific to it'. This is important to avoid misunderstanding.
The immediate understanding of 'taqayyud' is specificity, but the intended meaning is occupancy.
3
Learn the Ash'ari definition of place
According to Ahl al-Sunna, place is imaginary void (al-farāgh al-mawhūm). If place is void, then the negation of being a body already negates occupancy of void, making the extra negation redundant.
This shows the matn's economy: one negation covers both.
4
Learn the philosophical definition of place
Philosophers define place as the inner surface of a container touching the contained. Under this definition, a body might not be in a container (e.g., in void), so the extra negation is needed.
This contrast shows why the matn includes both negations.
5
Reflect on the divine transcendence
Allah is not confined by either definition. This lesson reinforces that Allah is not spatial and does not inhere in anything.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage discusses two advanced topics: the applicability of direction (jiha) to non-human entities, and the definition of 'place' (makān). How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever unconsciously imagine Allah as 'present' in a specific direction (like the sky or the qibla), as if He were confined to a place? What does this lesson demand you to correct in your heart's orientation?
?
The philosophers thought place is like a container. Have you ever tried to 'contain' Allah in your understanding, assuming you fully grasp His nature? How does the precision of these theological negations humble your intellect?
?
The scholars debated whether a phrase is redundant. How many of your beliefs about Allah are redundant or unnecessary because you haven't thought them through? Will you take time to examine your creed and remove what falls short of His transcendence?

How 'Bi an Yakuna' Depicts Negation and the Two Meanings of Self-Subsistence
Al-Bajuri explains the function of 'bi an yakuna' and the two interpretations of self-subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phrase 'bi an yakuna' depicts the denial rather than the denied, and why the interpretation of 'qa'im bi nafsihi' determines how its negation is depicted.

Core Idea
The way you define a term shapes how you describe its opposite.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary clarifies a subtle but crucial point in kalam definitions: the phrase 'bi an yakuna' (that he be) is used to picture what is being denied, not the denial itself. The author then shows that the definition of Allah's self-subsistence, which can include both independence from a substrate and independence from a specifier, forces a corresponding twofold depiction of its negation. Understanding this methodological consistency is essential for reading advanced kalam texts.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the function of 'bi an yakuna'
The passage opens by stating that 'bi an yakuna' is a depiction (taswir) of the negation (al-nafy), not of the negated (al-manfi). This means the phrase illustrates what is being denied, not the impossible state itself.
This is a methodological principle common in theological definitions.
2
Recall the two aspects of self-subsistence
Earlier in the text, the author had defined Allah's self-subsistence (qiyamuhu ta'ala bi nafsihi) as both lack of need for a substrate (al-mahall) and lack of need for a specifier (al-mukhasis). This is the famous view.
Some theologians define it only as lack of need for a substrate.
3
See how the negation mirrors the definition
Because the definition includes two negations, the depiction of the opposite (not being self-subsistent) also includes two positive statements: being an attribute subsisting in a substrate and being in need of a specifier.
This shows the internal consistency of the argument.
4
Examine the nuance about 'attribute subsisting in a substrate'
The commentator explains that the phrase 'subsisting in a substrate' is not for exclusion (ihtiraz) but for stating the actual reality (bayan al-waqi'). It may also involve an ellipsis of the explicative 'an'.
This highlights the precision of technical language.
5
Note the gloss about eternity
Al-Ajhuri's gloss clarifies that the attribute mentioned is eternal (qadima), because the impossibility of a temporal attribute (haditha) was already covered earlier when discussing 'or be an accident subsisting in a substrate'.
This prevents confusion with accidental attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: بِأَنْ يَكُونَ... إلخ) تصوير للنفي لا للمنفي. ولما جرى المصنف فيما تقدم على تفسير قيامه تعالى بنفسه بـ عدم افتقاره تعالى إلى المحلّ، وبـ عدم افتقاره تعالى إلى المخصّص كما هو اصطلاح لبعض المتكلمين، وهو المشهور؛ جَرَى هنا على تصوير عدم قيامه تعالى بنفسه بـ كونه صِفَةً يقوم بمحلّ، وب: كونه يحتاج إلى مخصص. ولو جَرَى فيما تقدم على تفسير قيامه تعالى بنفسه بـ: عدم افتقاره تعالى إلى المحلّ فقط كما هو اصطلاح لبعضهم؛ لجرى هنا على تصوير عدم قيامه تعالى بنفسه بـ: كونه يحتاج إلى المحلّ فقط كما هو ظاهر. (قوله: صِفَةً يَقُومُ بِمَحَلّ) تقييد الصفة بقوله: (يقوم بمحلّ) ليس للاحتراز، بل لبيان الواقع، ويحتمل أنه على حذف (أني) التفسيرية، ويكون تفسيراً باللازم لقوله: أن يكون صفة) على نَسَق ما تقدم حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري. (قوله: بِأَنْ يَكُونَ صِفَةً أي قديمة، وأما استحالة كونه صفة حادثة؛ فقد تقدم في قوله: (أو يكون عَرَضاً يقوم بالجزم).
His saying: 'by that he be... etc.' is a depiction of the negation, not of the negated. And since the author, in what preceded, had interpreted His self-subsistence (qiyāmuhu taʿālā bi nafsihi) as His lack of need for a substrate (al-maḥall) and as His lack of need for a specifier (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ) according to the terminology of some theologians, and this is the famous view; he proceeded here to depict His not being self-subsistent as his being an attribute subsisting in a substrate, and as his being in need of a specifier. And if he had, in what preceded, interpreted His self-subsistence as only His lack of need for a substrate, according to the terminology of some, then he would have proceeded here to depict His not being self-subsistent as his being in need of a substrate only, as is apparent. (His saying: 'an attribute subsisting in a substrate') the restriction of 'attribute' by his saying: 'subsisting in a substrate' is not for exclusion, but for statement of actuality. And it is possible that it is with the elision of the explicative 'an' and it is an interpretation by the necessary implication of his saying: 'that he be an attribute' in the manner of what preceded. The gloss of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ajhūrī: (His saying: 'by that he be an attribute') i.e., eternal. As for the impossibility of his being a temporal attribute, it has preceded in his saying: 'or he be an accident subsisting in a substrate.'
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the phrase 'bi an yakuna' (that he be) used to depict the negation of self-subsistence. The commentator clarifies that this phrase depicts the negated state (what is impossible), not the negation itself. He then links this to the author's earlier definition of 'qa'im bi nafsihi' (self-subsistent). There are two interpretations: (1) not needing a substrate (al-mahall) and (2) not needing a specifier (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ). The author, following the famous usage, included both. Consequently, the depiction of the opposite (not being self-subsistent) also includes both: being an attribute subsisting in a substrate (i.e., like an accident) and needing a specifier. If the author had used only the first interpretation, he would have depicted the opposite only as needing a substrate. The additional note about 'attribute subsisting in a substrate' explains that the qualification 'subsisting in a substrate' is not to exclude something else but to describe what is actual in the case of attributes. It may also involve an ellipsis of the explicative 'an'. The gloss of al-Ajhūrī adds that the attribute meant here is eternal, because temporal attributes (accidents) were already excluded earlier when discussing 'or be an accident subsisting in a substrate'. This passage demonstrates the technical precision in defining theological terms and the importance of consistent interpretation throughout a work.

Key Terms
القيام بالنفس
المحل
المخصص
الصفة
النفي
المنفي
التصوير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'bi an yakuna' describes the impossible state itself (the negated), rather than the negation.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that 'bi an yakuna' is a depiction of the negation (al-nafy), not the negated (al-manfi). The phrase is used to picture what is being denied, not the impossible quality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author depict the negation of self-subsistence as both being an attribute in a substrate and needing a specifier?
A) Because he thinks both are necessary for the definition.
B) Because he had earlier defined self-subsistence as both not needing a substrate and not needing a specifier.
C) Because he wants to cover all possibilities.
D) Because the Quran requires it.

Key Takeaway
The depiction of a theological negation directly reflects the definition of the positive attribute, and each term must be used consistently throughout the text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah's attributes, do you take care to define terms consistently, or do you sometimes mix definitions from different theological systems without realizing the logical implications? How might inconsistency in your definitions affect your understanding of tawhid? This week, choose one attribute and trace its definition and negation carefully in your own study.
?
This lesson shows that how we define a quality shapes how we deny its opposite. Think of a negative belief you hold about yourself (e.g., 'I am not patient enough'). What definition of 'patience' are you using to make that negation? Is your definition consistent with Islamic understanding? Pledge to align your self-evaluations with sound definitions from the Qur'an and Sunnah.
?
The commentator distinguishes between describing the negation and describing the negated. When you reject a sin or innovation, are you clear about what you are rejecting (the act itself) versus the negation of it? Could a lack of clarity cause you to be excessive or negligent in your rejection? Resolve to be precise in your speech about what you oppose.

Two Kinds of Ignorance Impossible for Allah
Bajuri defines two types of ignorance and their opposition to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of compound and simple ignorance, why each is impossible for Allah, and the type of opposition each has with knowledge.

Core Idea
Both kinds of ignorance contradict the perfection of divine knowledge.

In this passage, al-Bajuri unpacks the impossibility of ignorance for Allah by distinguishing two types: compound ignorance (false belief) and simple ignorance (absence of knowledge). He also shows how each relates to knowledge through different kinds of opposition. Understanding these distinctions clarifies why ignorance is incompatible with the divine nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice that the passage has three parts: the claim, the definitions, and the analysis of opposition.
2
Identify the two kinds of ignorance
Find the definitions of compound and simple ignorance. Note that compound ignorance involves a false belief, while simple ignorance is just the absence of knowledge.
3
Understand why compound ignorance is called 'compound'
The passage explains that compound ignorance entails two ignorances: ignorance of the reality and ignorance of one's own ignorance. This makes it a compound state.
4
Grasp the two types of opposition with knowledge
Compound ignorance opposes knowledge as a contrary (like light and dark), while simple ignorance opposes knowledge as privation vs. possession (like blindness vs. sight).
5
Reflect on why both are impossible for Allah
Ask yourself: Could Allah hold a false belief? Could He lack any knowledge? The answers are clearly no, based on His absolute perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى الْجَهْلُ) أي: سواء كان مركباً: وهو اعتقاد الشيء على خلاف ما هو عليه، أو بسيطاً وهو عَدَمُ العلم بالشيء. والتقابُلُ بينه وبين العلم من تقابل الضّدَّين بالنسبة للأول، ومن تقابل العَدَم والملكة بالنسبة للثاني. وإنما سُمّي الأولُ مركَّباً لاستلزامه لجهلين، فكأنه مركب منهما: الأولُ: جَهْلُهُ بحقيقة الشيء، والثاني: جَهْلُه بحال نفسه؛ لأنه يجهل أنه جاهل.
His statement: 'And likewise ignorance is impossible for Him, exalted' means: whether it is compound: which is belief of a thing contrary to what it is, or simple: which is absence of knowledge of a thing. And the opposition between it and knowledge is opposition of contraries with respect to the first, and opposition of privation and possession with respect to the second. And the first is called compound because it entails two ignorances, as if it is composed of them: the first: his ignorance of the true nature of the thing, and the second: his ignorance of his own state, because he is ignorant that he is ignorant.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage explains that ignorance (jahl) is impossible for Allah, and defines two types: compound and simple. Compound ignorance is when one believes something contrary to reality, which entails two ignorances: ignorance of the thing itself, and ignorance of one's own ignorance. Simple ignorance is merely the absence of knowledge. The author notes the type of opposition each has with knowledge: compound ignorance is contrary to knowledge (like opposites cannot coexist), while simple ignorance is the privation of knowledge (like blindness vs. sight). This makes it clear why both are impossible for Allah: compound ignorance would imply false belief, and simple ignorance would imply lack of knowledge, both of which contradict divine perfection.

Key Terms
الجهل (jahl)
الجهل المركب (jahl murakkab)
الجهل البسيط (jahl basit)
العلم (ilm)
تقابل التضاد (taqabul al-tadadd)
تقابل العدم والملكة (taqabul al-adam wa al-malaka)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think simple ignorance is a lesser deficiency and perhaps not impossible for Allah because it is just an absence.
Correction: Simple ignorance, though a privation, is still an absence of knowledge, and Allah must possess all knowledge. Therefore, even simple ignorance is impossible for Him.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of compound ignorance according to this passage?
A) Absence of all knowledge.
B) Belief in something contrary to reality, which entails two ignorances.
C) Knowledge that is uncertain.
D) Ignorance of one's own existence.

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is false belief entailing self-ignorance, simple ignorance is absence of knowledge; both are opposed to knowledge and impossible for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a firm belief about Allah that you later discovered was false? That is compound ignorance. Are you willing to examine your current beliefs to root out any that contradict revelation?
?
The passage says compound ignorance includes 'ignorance of his own state'—being unaware that you are ignorant. In what area of your life might you be confidently wrong without realizing it? How will you invite correction today?
?
Both compound and simple ignorance are impossible for Allah, but you are human. Which type of ignorance do you struggle with more: the pride of false belief or the laziness of not seeking knowledge? What practical step will you take this week to replace that ignorance with knowledge?

Opinion, Doubt, and Illusion: The Three Types of Ignorance-Related Concepts
Bajuri defines the three terms that are in the meaning of ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of zann, shakk, and wahm, and how they relate to ignorance.

Core Idea
Zann, shakk, and wahm are degrees of belief between certainty and ignorance.

Before discussing knowledge itself, Bajuri clarifies concepts that are 'in the meaning' of ignorance. These are not ignorance proper but are weaker forms of cognition: opinion (zann), doubt (shakk), and illusion (wahm). Each is defined by the strength of one's apprehension of a proposition. Let's examine his precise definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This passage comes as a clarification of terms related to ignorance. Recognize that zann, shakk, and wahm are not knowledge but are often confused with it.
They are epistemic grades.
2
Memorize the definitions
Learn the specific definitions: zann is apprehension of the more probable, shakk is equal apprehension, wahm is apprehension of the less probable.
These are technical terms in kalam.
3
Contrast with ignorance
See how each differs from pure ignorance: they involve some apprehension, unlike complete ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُ أي: كالظن وهو إدراكُ الطَّرَفِ الراجح، والشكّ: وهو إدراك كل من الطرفين على حَدٍ سواء، والوهم: وهو إدراك الطرف المرجوح، ومما في معناه أيضاً: كَوْنُ العلم ضرورياً أو نظرياً أو بديهياً أو كشبياً. فالأولُ يُطْلَقُ على ما لم يَحْضُل عن نَظَرِ واستدلال، كالعلم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، وعلى ما قارَنَ الضرورة، كالعلم الحاصل بالتهديد والضرب مثلاً، وهو بالمعنى الثاني مُحَالٌ عليه تعالى لاستدعائه الضرورة وسَبْقَ الجهل. وأما بالمعنى الأول؛ فهو وإن كان يَصِحُ إرادته في حقه تعالى؛ لأن عِلْمَهُ لم يحصل عن نظر واستدلال؛ لكن يمتنعُ إطلاق ذلك في حقه تعالى؛ لئلا يتوهم المعنى الثاني، لا لكونه يستدعي سَبْقَ الجهل. والثاني: ما حَصَلَ عن نَظَرِ واستدلال كالعلم بوجود القدرة له تعالى، وهو مُحالٌ عليه لاستدعائه سَبْقَ الجهل. والثالثُ يطلق على ما لا يتوقَّفُ على نظرِ واستدلال وإن توقف على حَدْسِ أو تَجْربَةٍ. وعلى هذا يكون مرادِفاً للضروري لكن بمعناه الأول، ويطلق أيضاً على: ما لا يتوقف على شيءٍ أصلاً، وعلى هذا يكون أخص من الضروري بمعناه المذكور، وظاهر أنه على كلّ من الإطلاقين ليس بمستحيل في حقه تعالى، لكن لما كان يقال: بَدَة النفس الأمرُ إِذا أَتَاهَا بغتةً من غير سَبْقِ شعورٍ؛ امتنع إطلاقه في حقه تعالى لاقتضائه سبق الجهل.
His statement: 'and what is in its meaning' i.e., like opinion (zann), which is apprehension of the more probable side; doubt (shakk), which is apprehension of each of the two sides equally; and illusion (wahm), which is apprehension of the less probable side. And also among what is in its meaning: that knowledge is necessary (daruri) or theoretical (nazari) or intuitive (badihi) or similar. The first (daruri) is applied to what does not result from reflection and inference, like knowledge that one is half of two, and to what accompanies coercion, like knowledge obtained through threats and beating for example. And it (the second meaning) is impossible for Him, exalted, because it requires coercion and preceding ignorance. As for the first meaning, it is valid to intend it with respect to Him, exalted, because His knowledge did not result from reflection and inference; however, it is forbidden to apply that expression to Him, exalted, lest the second meaning be imagined, not because it requires preceding ignorance. The second (nazari) is what results from reflection and inference, like knowledge of the existence of power for Him, exalted, and it is impossible for Him because it requires preceding ignorance. The third (badihi) is applied to what does not depend on reflection and inference, even if it depends on intuition or experience. Thus it is synonymous with daruri according to its first meaning, and it is also applied to what does not depend on anything at all; thus it is more specific than daruri according to the mentioned meaning. And it is apparent that according to each of the two applications, it is not impossible with respect to Him, exalted; but because it is said: 'the matter came upon the soul suddenly' when it comes to it unexpectedly without prior awareness, it is forbidden to apply it to Him, exalted, because it implies preceding ignorance.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage explains terms that are 'in the meaning' of ignorance, i.e., concepts that share some aspect with ignorance but are distinct. These are: zann (opinion), shakk (doubt), and wahm (illusion), each defined by the apprehension of probability. Then the author discusses classifications of knowledge: daruri (necessary), nazari (theoretical), and badihi (intuitive). Daruri has two meanings: knowledge not from reflection, and knowledge accompanied by coercion. The latter is impossible for Allah. The first meaning is valid to intend but not to apply literally to avoid misunderstanding. Nazari knowledge from reflection is impossible for Allah as it implies prior ignorance. Badihi knowledge is not impossible in itself for Allah, but the term is avoided because its linguistic root suggests sudden occurrence, which implies prior ignorance. This shows the careful theological reasoning in attributing knowledge to Allah.

Key Terms
الظن
الشك
الوهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Zann, shakk, and wahm are all types of ignorance.
Correction: They are not ignorance but weaker forms of belief; they involve apprehension, whereas ignorance is absence of apprehension.

Checkpoint
Which term refers to apprehension of the more probable side?
A) Shakk
B) Zann
C) Wahm
D) Ignorance

Key Takeaway
Zann, shakk, and wahm are defined by the strength of one's apprehension of a proposition, ranging from probable to improbable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you rely on mere opinion (zann) about your own spiritual condition instead of seeking certainty? What is one area where you can commit to moving from assumption to verified knowledge this week?
?
Is there any matter of faith in which you are truly in doubt (shakk)—seeing both sides as equally plausible? How does that doubt affect your obedience to Allah? What is the first step you will take to resolve it?
?
Do you ever follow your wahm (the less probable side) in matters of religion, giving weight to a weak assumption over clear evidence? In what part of your life have you allowed a weak conjecture to guide your actions, and what will you do to correct that today?

Necessary, Theoretical, and Intuitive Knowledge: Their Meanings and Applicability to Allah
Bajuri classifies knowledge into necessary, theoretical, and intuitive, and discusses their applicability to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classifications of knowledge (daruri, nazari, badihi) and why some can and cannot be attributed to Allah.

Core Idea
Knowledge is classified as necessary (daruri), theoretical (nazari), or intuitive (badihi), each with subtle restrictions when applied to Allah.

After defining terms related to ignorance, Bajuri turns to the classification of knowledge itself. He presents three types: necessary (daruri), theoretical (nazari), and intuitive (badihi). Each has specific meanings, and careful theological reasoning determines which can be applied to Allah. This lesson unpacks those distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the passage and note the three types: daruri, nazari, and badihi. Also note that a fourth, kasbi, is mentioned but not elaborated.
2
Understand daruri's two meanings
Daruri has two meanings: knowledge not from reflection, and knowledge accompanied by coercion. Only the first is valid for Allah, but the term is avoided.
The second meaning implies coercion and prior ignorance.
3
Understand nazari's impossibility
Nazari knowledge is from reflection and implies prior ignorance, so it is impossible for Allah.
4
Understand badihi's meanings and restriction
Badihi is analogous to daruri but its linguistic root (suddenness) implies prior ignorance, so it is also avoided.
This shows careful language theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُ أي: كالظن وهو إدراكُ الطَّرَفِ الراجح، والشكّ: وهو إدراك كل من الطرفين على حَدٍ سواء، والوهم: وهو إدراك الطرف المرجوح، ومما في معناه أيضاً: كَوْنُ العلم ضرورياً أو نظرياً أو بديهياً أو كشبياً. فالأولُ يُطْلَقُ على ما لم يَحْضُل عن نَظَرِ واستدلال، كالعلم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، وعلى ما قارَنَ الضرورة، كالعلم الحاصل بالتهديد والضرب مثلاً، وهو بالمعنى الثاني مُحَالٌ عليه تعالى لاستدعائه الضرورة وسَبْقَ الجهل. وأما بالمعنى الأول؛ فهو وإن كان يَصِحُ إرادته في حقه تعالى؛ لأن عِلْمَهُ لم يحصل عن نظر واستدلال؛ لكن يمتنعُ إطلاق ذلك في حقه تعالى؛ لئلا يتوهم المعنى الثاني، لا لكونه يستدعي سَبْقَ الجهل. والثاني: ما حَصَلَ عن نَظَرِ واستدلال كالعلم بوجود القدرة له تعالى، وهو مُحالٌ عليه لاستدعائه سَبْقَ الجهل. والثالثُ يطلق على ما لا يتوقَّفُ على نظرِ واستدلال وإن توقف على حَدْسِ أو تَجْربَةٍ. وعلى هذا يكون مرادِفاً للضروري لكن بمعناه الأول، ويطلق أيضاً على: ما لا يتوقف على شيءٍ أصلاً، وعلى هذا يكون أخص من الضروري بمعناه المذكور، وظاهر أنه على كلّ من الإطلاقين ليس بمستحيل في حقه تعالى، لكن لما كان يقال: بَدَة النفس الأمرُ إِذا أَتَاهَا بغتةً من غير سَبْقِ شعورٍ؛ امتنع إطلاقه في حقه تعالى لاقتضائه سبق الجهل.
His statement: 'and what is in its meaning' i.e., like opinion (zann), which is apprehension of the more probable side; doubt (shakk), which is apprehension of each of the two sides equally; and illusion (wahm), which is apprehension of the less probable side. And also among what is in its meaning: that knowledge is necessary (daruri) or theoretical (nazari) or intuitive (badihi) or similar. The first (daruri) is applied to what does not result from reflection and inference, like knowledge that one is half of two, and to what accompanies coercion, like knowledge obtained through threats and beating for example. And it (the second meaning) is impossible for Him, exalted, because it requires coercion and preceding ignorance. As for the first meaning, it is valid to intend it with respect to Him, exalted, because His knowledge did not result from reflection and inference; however, it is forbidden to apply that expression to Him, exalted, lest the second meaning be imagined, not because it requires preceding ignorance. The second (nazari) is what results from reflection and inference, like knowledge of the existence of power for Him, exalted, and it is impossible for Him because it requires preceding ignorance. The third (badihi) is applied to what does not depend on reflection and inference, even if it depends on intuition or experience. Thus it is synonymous with daruri according to its first meaning, and it is also applied to what does not depend on anything at all; thus it is more specific than daruri according to the mentioned meaning. And it is apparent that according to each of the two applications, it is not impossible with respect to Him, exalted; but because it is said: 'the matter came upon the soul suddenly' when it comes to it unexpectedly without prior awareness, it is forbidden to apply it to Him, exalted, because it implies preceding ignorance.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage explains terms that are 'in the meaning' of ignorance, i.e., concepts that share some aspect with ignorance but are distinct. These are: zann (opinion), shakk (doubt), and wahm (illusion), each defined by the apprehension of probability. Then the author discusses classifications of knowledge: daruri (necessary), nazari (theoretical), and badihi (intuitive). Daruri has two meanings: knowledge not from reflection, and knowledge accompanied by coercion. The latter is impossible for Allah. The first meaning is valid to intend but not to apply literally to avoid misunderstanding. Nazari knowledge from reflection is impossible for Allah as it implies prior ignorance. Badihi knowledge is not impossible in itself for Allah, but the term is avoided because its linguistic root suggests sudden occurrence, which implies prior ignorance. This shows the careful theological reasoning in attributing knowledge to Allah.

Key Terms
الضروري
النظري
البديهي
الكسبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All types of knowledge are equally applicable to Allah.
Correction: Some types imply prior ignorance or coercion, so they are not used for Allah. Even valid types are avoided due to linguistic implications.

Checkpoint
Why is 'nazari' knowledge impossible for Allah?
A) Because it requires coercion
B) Because it implies prior ignorance
C) Because it is a synonym for badihi
D) Because it depends on intuition

Key Takeaway
Knowledge types like nazari are impossible for Allah because they imply prior ignorance, while daruri and badihi are avoided due to possible misunderstandings despite being conceptually valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge about Allah, do you ever rely on your own reasoning as if it is sufficient, forgetting that even the most rigorous theoretical knowledge (nazari) is a human process that cannot fully capture His eternal knowledge? How does this lesson challenge your approach?
?
Have you ever used words like 'obvious' or 'intuitive' (badihi) about matters of faith, assuming your immediate certainty is enough? This lesson warns that even such terms carry implications of suddenness that do not befit Allah's timeless knowledge. In what area of your faith do you need to be more humble about your 'gut feelings'?
?
The author forbids using the term daruri for Allah even though it is conceptually true in one sense, because of the risk of misunderstanding. Are there words you use to describe Allah that you have not examined for hidden implications? What concrete step can you take this week to refine your language about Him?

Compound Ignorance and Related Cognitive States
Al-Bajuri defines compound ignorance and lists related cognitive states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of compound ignorance, its two components, and how it differs from simple ignorance, and you will be able to distinguish between conjecture, doubt, and illusion as cognitive states opposed to knowledge.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is being ignorant of both the truth and your own ignorance.

Al-Bajuri begins by clarifying the relationship between ignorance and knowledge, explaining that the contrast is either of opposites or of privation and possession. He then defines 'compound ignorance' as consisting of two layers of ignorance. This lesson introduces that definition and then lists the other cognitive states that are in the same category as ignorance—namely conjecture, doubt, and illusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on compound ignorance
Read the Arabic passage once through. Notice how Al-Bajuri first contrasts ignorance and knowledge, then defines compound ignorance, and finally lists other states.
The passage includes a parenthetical annotation from the commentator.
2
Identify the two components of compound ignorance
Compound ignorance is said to entail two ignorances. Find both components: ignorance of the reality of the thing, and ignorance of one's own ignorance.
The second ignorance is what makes it 'compound'.
3
Understand the three related states
Conjecture (zann), doubt (shakk), and illusion (wahm) are mentioned as states in the meaning of ignorance. Note how each is defined as a type of perception with different strengths.
These are not knowledge but still cognitive states.

(قوله: وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُ أي: كالظن وهو إدراكُ الطَّرَفِ الراجح، والشكّ: وهو إدراك كل من الطرفين على حَدٍ سواء، والوهم: وهو إدراك الطرف المرجوح، ومما في معناه أيضاً: كَوْنُ العلم ضرورياً أو نظرياً أو بديهياً أو كشبياً. فالأولُ يُطْلَقُ على ما لم يَحْضُل عن نَظَرِ واستدلال، كالعلم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، وعلى ما قارَنَ الضرورة، كالعلم الحاصل بالتهديد والضرب مثلاً، وهو بالمعنى الثاني مُحَالٌ عليه تعالى لاستدعائه الضرورة وسَبْقَ الجهل. وأما بالمعنى الأول؛ فهو وإن كان يَصِحُ إرادته في حقه تعالى؛ لأن عِلْمَهُ لم يحصل عن نظر واستدلال؛ لكن يمتنعُ إطلاق ذلك في حقه تعالى؛ لئلا يتوهم المعنى الثاني، لا لكونه يستدعي سَبْقَ الجهل. والثاني: ما حَصَلَ عن نَظَرِ واستدلال كالعلم بوجود القدرة له تعالى، وهو مُحالٌ عليه لاستدعائه سَبْقَ الجهل. والثالثُ يطلق على ما لا يتوقَّفُ على نظرِ واستدلال وإن توقف على حَدْسِ أو تَجْربَةٍ. وعلى هذا يكون مرادِفاً للضروري لكن بمعناه الأول، ويطلق أيضاً على: ما لا يتوقف على شيءٍ أصلاً، وعلى هذا يكون أخص من الضروري بمعناه المذكور، وظاهر أنه على كلّ من الإطلاقين ليس بمستحيل في حقه تعالى، لكن لما كان يقال: بَدَة النفس الأمرُ إِذا أَتَاهَا بغتةً من غير سَبْقِ شعورٍ؛ امتنع إطلاقه في حقه تعالى لاقتضائه سبق الجهل. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري. (قوله: لِجَهَلَيْنِ) أي: بسيطين. (وقوله: بِحَقِيقَةِ الشَّيْء أي بحالة الشيء التي هو عليها في الواقع. And the contrast between it and knowledge is a contrast of opposites with respect to the first [type of ignorance], and a contrast of privation and possession with respect to the second. It is only called 'compound' because it entails two ignorances, as if it is composed of them: the first: his ignorance of the reality of the thing, and the second: his ignorance of his own state; because he is ignorant that he is ignorant. (His saying: 'and what is in its meaning' means: like conjecture, which is perception of the preponderant side; doubt, which is perception of each of the two sides equally; and illusion, which is perception of the weaker side; and also included in its meaning is: knowledge being necessary (daruri) or theoretical (nazari) or intuitive (badihi) or acquisitive (kasbi). The first [type, daruri] is applied to what is not obtained by reflection and inference, like knowledge that one is half of two, and to what accompanies necessity, like knowledge obtained by threat and beating for example, and it is in the second meaning impossible for Him, exalted, because it necessitates necessity and prior ignorance. As for the first meaning, it is valid to intend it for Him, exalted, because His knowledge was not obtained by reflection and inference; but it is forbidden to apply [the term] to Him, exalted, to avoid understanding the second meaning, not because it requires prior ignorance. The second [type, nazari] is what is obtained by reflection and inference, like knowledge of the existence of power for Him, exalted, and it is impossible for Him [to have this type] because it requires prior ignorance. The third [type, badihi] is applied to what does not depend on reflection and inference even if it depends on intuition or experience. In this sense it is synonymous with daruri but in its first meaning, and it is also applied to: what does not depend on anything at all, and in this sense it is more specific than daruri in its mentioned meaning, and it is apparent that in both usages it is not impossible for Him, exalted; but since it is said: 'the affair came suddenly upon the soul when it comes to it unexpectedly without prior perception', it is forbidden to apply [this term] to Him, exalted, because it implies prior ignorance. Annotation of the scholar Ahmad al-Ujhuri: (His saying: 'for two ignorances') i.e., simple ones. (And his saying: 'of the reality of the thing') i.e., the state of the thing as it is in reality. (His saying: 'but since it is said... etc.') From this it is known that the judgment of the impossibility of the intuitive [knowledge] is with respect to what is imagined from it of its occurrence to God suddenly, not with respect to each of its two previous meanings.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the types of ignorance and knowledge, focusing on their applicability to God. It begins by distinguishing compound ignorance (which entails two simple ignorances: ignorance of the thing and ignorance of one's own ignorance) from simple ignorance. It then defines terms related to knowledge: conjecture (zann), doubt (shakk), illusion (wahm), and then classifies knowledge into four types: necessary (daruri), theoretical (nazari), intuitive (badihi), and acquisitive (kasbi). The commentary then examines each type in relation to God. Daruri knowledge has two meanings: one without inquiry (like knowing one is half of two) and one that accompanies necessity (like knowledge from threat). The second meaning is impossible for God because it implies previous ignorance and necessity. The first meaning is valid for God but the term is avoided to prevent misunderstanding. Nazari knowledge is obtained by reflection and is impossible for God because it requires prior ignorance. Badihi knowledge is not dependent on reflection but may depend on intuition or experience; in one sense it is synonymous with daruri, in another it is more specific. It is not inherently impossible for God, but it is forbidden because of its connotation of suddenness (badaha) which implies prior ignorance. The marginal note by al-Ujhuri clarifies that the impossibility of badihi is due to the connotation of suddenness, not the other meanings.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think compound ignorance is simply not knowing something, without realizing the additional layer of not knowing that one does not know.
Correction: Compound ignorance specifically involves being ignorant of one's own ignorance, which makes it worse than simple ignorance.

Checkpoint

A) Ignorance of the thing and ignorance of the method.
B) Ignorance of the thing and ignorance of one's own ignorance.
C) Ignorance of the cause and ignorance of the effect.
D) Ignorance of the self and ignorance of others.

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is a double ignorance: not knowing the truth and not knowing that you don't know.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Who in your life would be honest enough to tell you the truth, and are you ready to listen to them without defensiveness? Classification of Knowledge: Daruri, Nazari, and Badihi
Al-Bajuri classifies knowledge and discusses each type's permissibility for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main types of knowledge (necessary, theoretical, and intuitive) and their applicability to God, including why some definitions are impossible for God due to implications of prior ignorance or necessity.

Core Idea
Knowledge that arises from necessity or reflection cannot be attributed to God; only knowledge without such implications is possible.

After defining ignorance, Al-Bajuri classifies knowledge into four types: necessary (daruri), theoretical (nazari), intuitive (badihi), and acquisitive (kasbi), though he focuses on the first three. He then examines each type, showing which meanings can and cannot be applied to Allah. This lesson unpacks each category and the reasons for their permissibility or impossibility regarding God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of knowledge
Read the passage and list the four types mentioned: daruri, nazari, badihi, and kasbi.
Kasbi is mentioned but not elaborated.
2
Understand the two meanings of daruri
Daruri has two meanings: one without reflection, and one accompanied by necessity. The second is impossible for God because it implies prior ignorance.
The first meaning is possible but the term is avoided to prevent misunderstanding.
3
Understand nazari knowledge
Nazari knowledge is obtained by reflection and inference. It is impossible for God because it requires prior ignorance.
God's knowledge is eternal and not acquired.
4
Understand badihi knowledge
Badihi (intuitive) knowledge has two meanings: one dependent on intuition/experience, and one independent of anything. Both are not inherently impossible, but the term is forbidden because it connotes suddenness (badaha) which implies prior ignorance.
The marginal note clarifies the reason.
